Bigger than the missed deadline. She gives a presentation with incorrect data. Gets called out in front of the whole team. Feels her face burn as her manager politely but firmly corrects her in real time.

The old scoreboard screams: -100 points. Worthless. Failure. You'll never recover from this.

But something's different now.

She feels the shame. Acknowledges it. Yes, this is embarrassing. That's true. The feeling is real.

But beneath it, there's something steady. Something that doesn't move when the scoreboard crashes.

I made an error. I'll correct it. I'll double-check my data next time. But I'm still here. Still valuable. Still worthy of taking up space in this room.

After the meeting, she approaches her manager.

"I'm sorry about the data error. I've already corrected it and sent the updated slides to everyone. I'm also implementing a new review process to prevent this."

Her manager nods. "Thanks for owning it. We all make mistakes."

And that's it. The mistake was made. The correction was offered. Life continues.

Priya goes to lunch with her sister. Enjoys it. Not because she "earned" it by fixing the mistake. But because she's allowed to enjoy her life whether the scoreboard reads high or low.

Her soul--the one that can't be cut, burned, wetted, or withered--doesn't consult the scoreboard. It never has.

The scoreboard still exists. It probably always will. But it's not her anymore.

She is what she's always been: the indestructible self.

And that can never be scored.

Reflection

- What's on your scoreboard? What makes you feel more or less worthy?
- Can you identify one recent situation where you confused your action with your self?
- What would change if you regulated your daily habits as a foundation for self-worth, rather than relying on achievement?

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 9: Pride

The View From the Top

James remembers exactly when he started believing he was better.

It was subtle at first. A promotion. Then another. Awards. Recognition. People seeking his opinion, his approval, his presence. Board meetings where his word carried weight. Conferences where audiences leaned forward to catch what he said.

Somewhere in that ascent, something shifted.

He stopped seeing himself as fortunate. Started seeing himself as superior. Not consciously. Not in words he'd ever say aloud. But in the way he walked into rooms. The way he listened--or didn't listen--when others spoke. The way he measured people instantly: above him (rare), below him (common), worth his time (few), beneath his notice (many).

Pride isn't loud. It doesn't announce itself. It's the quiet certainty that you're special. Different. Above. That the rules applying to others don't quite apply to you.

James is fifty-one. CEO of a mid-sized tech company. Married--though his wife feels more like staff lately. Two adult children who text less and less. A corner office with a view of the bay where he sits right now, reading an email that makes his jaw tighten.

The board is questioning his decision on the acquisition.

Questioning him.

Don't they know who he is? What he's built? How many times he's been right when everyone else was wrong?

He closes the laptop harder than necessary.

Pride doesn't like being questioned. Because pride's power comes from being above question.

And right now, James is discovering what happens when that elevation starts to crack.

His assistant knocks. "The board wants a meeting tomorrow at nine."

James nods. Dismisses her without looking up. He's already rehearsing his defense--not because the facts require defending, but because his identity does. If the board overrules him, it won't just be a business decision reversed. It will be proof that he's not who he thinks he is.

And that possibility is unbearable.

The Hidden Prison

We're taught to be proud. "Have some pride in your work." "You should be proud of yourself."

And there's truth in that. Healthy self-respect exists. Satisfaction in work well done is real.

But there's another kind of pride. The kind that doesn't just appreciate your achievements--it uses them to create separation. To elevate yourself above rather than simply acknowledging what you've done. To build an identity around being better, smarter, more worthy than others.

This pride is a prison. It looks like freedom--you're on top, after all--but it isolates you, exhausts you, and makes you fragile. Your entire sense of self rests on comparison. Anyone who challenges you becomes a threat to your identity itself.

And worst of all, pride blinds you. It can't see its own limitations. Can't learn from those it considers inferior. Can't recognize when it's wrong.

The Gita calls this ahankara--false ego. And it names pride as one of the doorways to self-destruction.

The Gita Speaks: The Anatomy of Pride

Krishna doesn't merely warn against pride. He dissects it. He shows Arjuna exactly what the prideful mind looks like from the inside--and the portrait is devastating in its accuracy:

    "Arrogance, pride, anger, conceit, harshness and ignorance--these qualities belong to those of demonic nature, O son of Prtha."

    -- Bhagavad-gita 16.4

Notice the company pride keeps. Not strength, not confidence, not competence--but arrogance, anger, conceit, harshness, and ignorance. These are not separate vices. They are a single syndrome. Pride generates anger when challenged. Conceit follows naturally from the belief in one's superiority. Harshness toward others becomes inevitable when you see them as beneath you. And ignorance--the inability to see yourself clearly--is both pride's cause and its consequence.

Then Krishna gives voice to the prideful mind itself:

    "The demoniac person thinks: 'So much wealth do I have today, and I will gain more according to my schemes. So much is mine now, and it will increase in the future, more and more.'"

    -- Bhagavad-gita 16.13

    "He is my enemy, and I have killed him; and my other enemy will also be killed. I am the lord of everything, I am the enjoyer, I am perfect, powerful and happy."

    -- Bhagavad-gita 16.14

    "I am the richest man, surrounded by aristocratic relatives. There is none so powerful and happy as I am. I shall perform sacrifices, I shall give some charity, and thus I shall rejoice.' In this way, such persons are deluded by ignorance."

    -- Bhagavad-gita 16.15

Read those verses again. They are a transcript of the inner monologue that pride produces. I have. I will gain. Mine. More. I am the lord. I am the enjoyer. I am perfect. None so powerful as I am.

Every sentence begins with "I." Every thought orbits the self. Every assessment inflates the ego. And the final line delivers the verdict: deluded by ignorance. Not powerful. Not special. Deluded.

James, sitting in his corner office rehearsing his defense against the board, is living inside these verses. He doesn't see it. That's the nature of the delusion. Pride is the one vice that cannot recognize itself.

But Krishna also shows the alternative--what a person freed from pride actually looks like:

    "The worker who is free from all material attachments and false ego, who is enthusiastic and resolute and who is indifferent to success or failure, is a worker in the mode of goodness."

    -- Bhagavad-gita 18.26

Free from false ego. Not free from effort--the worker is enthusiastic and resolute. Not passive. Not lazy. But indifferent to success or failure. Working because the work matters, not because the work proves something about who you are.

This is the difference between confidence and pride. Confidence does excellent work and lets the results speak. Pride does excellent work and demands that the results glorify the doer.

And finally, the warning and the promise together:

    "If you become conscious of Me, you will pass over all the obstacles of conditional life by My grace. If, however, you do not work in such consciousness but act through false ego, not hearing Me, you will be lost."

    -- Bhagavad-gita 18.58

False ego--pride--doesn't just distort your self-image. It blocks grace. It cuts you off from the very help that could free you. Because pride insists: I don't need help. I am enough. I am the lord of everything.

And that insistence is the lock on the prison door.

Living the Teaching: From Pride to Clarity

James sits in his therapist's office.

He didn't want to come. Therapy is for people with problems. He has challenges, sure--doesn't everyone at his level?--but not problems.

But his wife gave him an ultimatum. Therapy or divorce.

So here he is.

"Tell me about your week," the therapist says.

James recounts the board meeting. The questioning. How frustrating it is to have to explain himself to people who don't understand the complexity of his decisions.

"How did that feel?"

"Annoying. Like being second-guessed by people who aren't qualified to--"

He stops.

The therapist waits.

James hears what he was about to say: people who aren't qualified to question me.

"I sound arrogant," he says quietly.

"Do you feel arrogant?"

James pauses. "I feel like I've earned the right to be respected. To have my judgment trusted."

"And when it's not?"

"I feel threatened."

There it is. The crack in the armor. Pride isn't strength. It's fragility disguised as superiority.

Over the following months, James starts a practice. Simple. Humbling. Every day, he acknowledges one thing he didn't create. His intelligence. His first break. The market conditions that favored his company's growth. The team members who made him look good.

He starts listening differently in meetings. Actually listening. And discovering that people he'd dismissed have insights he'd missed.

He starts saying, "I don't know" when he doesn't know.

And something unexpected happens: he becomes more effective. Not less. Because humility doesn't mean weakness. It means accuracy. Seeing clearly what you can and can't do.

Practice Box

1. Inventory what you didn't create. Each morning, name one thing you benefit from that you didn't produce: your health, your opportunities, your abilities. Let gratitude replace the inner monologue of "I, I, I."

2. Listen to those you've dismissed. Find someone you've considered "beneath" you. Listen with full attention. Assume they have something to teach you.

3. Work without claiming the results. Do excellent work this week. Then let the results belong to the work, not to your ego. Notice the difference between enthusiasm and self-glorification.

4. Watch for the syndrome. When pride flares, check: Is anger present? Harshness? The need to dominate? These are not separate problems. They are pride expressing itself.

The Way Forward

Two years later, the board removes James as CEO.

It's respectful. They offer a generous severance. A board seat. But the message is clear: it's time for new leadership.

The old James would have been destroyed. His identity was CEO. Losing the title would have meant losing himself.

But this James--the one who's been practicing humility for two years--feels something different.

Disappointment, yes. Sadness. A sense of loss.

But not destruction.

Because he's no longer the lord of everything. He's no longer perfect, powerful, and beyond question. He's a person. Gifted, yes. Hardworking, yes. But also supported, dependent, limited, and equal.

At his farewell party, he gives a speech. He thanks people. Names specific contributions. Admits mistakes. And at the end, he says something that surprises everyone:

"I thought being CEO made me special. But what I've learned is that I'm exactly as valuable as every person in this room. The difference is just what role we're playing. And roles change."

Later, driving home, he feels light.

Not because he lost his job. But because he's lost his prison.

The false ego that kept him elevated, isolated, and exhausted--it's loosening. And what remains is simpler. Truer.

Just a human. Enthusiastic and resolute. Indifferent to success or failure. Free from the need to be the lord of everything.

And infinitely more at peace.

Reflection

- Where does pride show up in your life? Can you hear the inner monologue of "I, I, I"?
- What are three things you benefit from that you didn't create?
- What would change if you worked with enthusiasm but without needing the results to prove your superiority?

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 10: Feeling Shameful

What They Don't Know

There's a version of Carmen that everyone sees.

Confident. Competent. Put-together. The one who smiles at the morning team meeting, presents her work clearly, asks smart questions, stays late when needed. The one people describe as "having it all figured out."

And then there's the version she sees in the mirror.

The one who knows what she did three years ago. The one who remembers the lie she told to get the promotion. The affair with her colleague that nearly destroyed two marriages. The friend she betrayed when it served her career. The small cruelties and large cowardices that no one else witnessed but that she carries like stones in her chest.

That version--the real one, she thinks--is contaminated. Unforgivable. Fundamentally wrong.

Shame isn't guilt.

Guilt says, I did something bad. Shame says, I am bad.

Guilt has a specific target--an action, a mistake, a choice. Shame is diffuse, total, existential. It's not about what you did. It's about what you are. And what you are, shame insists, is broken beyond repair.

Carmen sits in the bathroom at work, door locked, scrolling through congratulatory messages about her latest project success. Each "congrats!" feels like mockery. If they knew, she thinks. If they really knew who I am...

Shame tells you you're an impostor. Not in your skills--you might actually be competent. But in your humanity. You don't deserve to be treated like everyone else. You don't deserve kindness, belonging, love.

And so you hide. Perform. Manage the image. Make sure no one sees the truth.

Because if they did, shame whispers, they'd leave. And they'd be right to.

She goes home that night and stands in the kitchen, unable to eat. The congratulatory messages keep arriving on her phone. Each one lands like an accusation. She turns the phone face down and sits in silence, wondering how long a person can carry this weight before it breaks something that can't be repaired.

The Weight You're Carrying

Most of us carry shame about something.

Maybe it's something you did. An affair. A betrayal. A lie. Something you're not proud of, something that violated your own values.

Maybe it's something that was done to you. Abuse. Violation. Trauma. And somehow, illogically, you feel ashamed of it--as if being harmed made you dirty, less than, unworthy.

Whatever the source, shame operates the same way. It hides. It isolates. It distorts. And it lies.

Shame tells you that you're uniquely broken. That everyone else is fine and you're the exception. That if people knew the truth, they'd reject you--because the truth is unacceptable.

But here's what the Bhagavad-gita reveals: your actions don't define your essence. Your history doesn't determine your worth. Your mistakes--even serious ones--don't make you fundamentally different from every other human being.

Because beneath all the actions, all the history, all the mistakes--there's something uncontaminated. Your eternal self.

The Gita Speaks: The Fire That Burns All Sin

Krishna addresses shame not with gentle reassurance but with fierce clarity. He doesn't minimize what Arjuna has done or what he fears doing. Instead, he points to a truth so radical it overturns shame's entire premise:

    "Even if you are considered to be the most sinful of all sinners, when you are situated in the boat of transcendental knowledge, you will be able to cross over the ocean of miseries."

    -- Bhagavad-gita 4.36

The most sinful of all sinners. Krishna doesn't say "if your mistakes were minor" or "if you didn't really mean it." He names the worst case. The absolute bottom. The person shame says is beyond redemption. And he says: even that person can cross over. Knowledge of your true nature is a boat that carries anyone who boards it. Shame says some people are too broken. Krishna says no one is.

And then he intensifies the image:

    "As the blazing fire turns firewood to ashes, O Arjuna, so does the fire of knowledge burn to ashes all reactions to material activities."

    -- Bhagavad-gita 4.37

Not some reactions. All reactions. The fire of knowledge doesn't sort through your past and decide which mistakes are forgivable. It burns everything to ashes. The lie Carmen told. The affair. The betrayal. Not erased from history--they happened, and their consequences are real. But the karmic residue, the weight that shame makes you carry as identity--that is fuel for the fire. And knowledge consumes it completely.

But this isn't a license to act carelessly. Krishna makes the standard of action clear:

    "One who performs his duty without attachment, surrendering the results unto the Supreme God, is not affected by sinful action, as the lotus leaf is untouched by water."

    -- Bhagavad-gita 5.10

The lotus leaf sits on water but is never wetted by it. This is the image of a person who acts in the world--makes mistakes, faces consequences, lives a fully human life--but whose essential self remains untouched. Not because they're perfect. Because their identity is not built on their actions. They do their duty, surrender the results, and remain clean at the level that matters most.

For those drowning in shame, Krishna offers the most direct lifeline in all of scripture:

    "Even if one commits the most abominable actions, if he is engaged in devotional service, he is to be considered saintly because he is properly situated."

    -- Bhagavad-gita 9.30

Shame says: your worst action defines you. Krishna says: your orientation defines you. Not where you've been, but where you're facing. Not what you did, but what you're devoted to now. A person who has committed the most abominable actions but who turns toward the divine is properly situated--is, in fact, to be considered saintly. Not because the actions were acceptable, but because the self that committed them is not the whole story.

And finally, the ultimate promise:

    "Abandon all varieties of religion and just surrender unto Me. I shall deliver you from all sinful reaction. Do not fear."

    -- Bhagavad-gita 18.66

Do not fear. Three words that strike at the heart of shame. Because shame is, at its core, fear. Fear of being known. Fear of being rejected. Fear that the truth about you is unacceptable.

Krishna says: surrender. Bring it all. The lies, the betrayals, the failures, the darkness. Hold nothing back. And I will deliver you. Not because you deserve it. Not because you earned it. But because that is the nature of grace. It meets you where you are--even at the very bottom--and carries you across.

Living the Teaching: Releasing the Weight

Carmen finally tells someone.

Not everyone. Not publicly. But her therapist. And then, months later, her sister.

She tells them what she did. The lie. The affair. The betrayal. All of it. The things that shame has been using to convince her she's irredeemable.

And something happens that shame didn't predict: they don't leave.

Her therapist listens without judgment. Helps her see the pain she was in, the patterns she was repeating, the human frailty that connects her to everyone else who's ever acted from fear or confusion.

Her sister cries with her. Says, "You're still my sister."

And Carmen realizes: shame lied. The truth didn't destroy her. It freed her.

She begins a practice. Every morning, before the shame can start its litany, she sits quietly and remembers: her essential self is like the lotus leaf. It sits on the water of her past but is not wetted by it. The fire of knowledge is burning through the residue, slowly, daily. And she is not the worst thing she's ever done. She is the one who turned around.

Practice Box

1. Name what you're ashamed of. Write it down. Be specific. Shame loses power when it's spoken or written, not hidden.

2. Separate action from essence. "I did X" (fact about action) is not the same as "I am X" (shame's false conclusion). The action is real. The identity shame constructs from it is not.

3. Remember the fire. Knowledge burns all reactions to ashes. You don't need to sort through your past deciding what's forgivable. The fire doesn't discriminate. Let it work.

4. Share with someone safe. Not to perform confession, but to discover that being known doesn't mean being rejected. Shame thrives in hiding. Truth defeats it.

The Way Forward

A year later, Carmen is at a team lunch when a colleague makes a joke about "people who lie to get ahead."

The old Carmen would have felt the comment like a knife. Would have gone silent. Would have spent the rest of the day spiraling.

This Carmen feels something different. A twinge. Recognition that yes, she did that. It wasn't okay. She's made amends where she could and changed her behavior since.

But she doesn't collapse into identity with it.

She laughs with the group. Not because she's denying what she did. But because she's not hiding anymore. The truth is no longer a weapon that shame holds over her.

Later, she mentors a young colleague who's struggling with a mistake. The colleague is drowning in self-recrimination, convinced she's ruined her career.

Carmen tells her: "You made a mistake. That's real. Fix it if you can, learn from it, do better. But don't confuse the action with who you are. You're not the mistake. You're the person learning from it."

The colleague looks surprised. "How do you know?"

Carmen smiles. "Because I've been where you are. And I'm still here."

That night, looking in the mirror--the same mirror where shame used to whisper contamination--Carmen sees something different.

Not perfection. Not someone who's never made mistakes.

But someone who surrendered everything--the worst of it, all of it--and was not rejected. Someone the fire has been working on. Someone the boat carried across.

The lotus leaf, sitting on water, untouched.

Do not fear.

Reflection

- What are you ashamed of? What are you hiding because you fear it makes you fundamentally unacceptable?
- Can you separate what you did from who you are?
- What would change if you believed that even the most sinful person, properly oriented, could be considered saintly?

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 11: Sorrow and Regret

The Unchangeable Past

Daniel's father died on a Tuesday.

Heart attack. Sudden. No warning. No chance to say goodbye.

But that's not what haunts Daniel three years later.

What haunts him is the argument they had the week before. Over something stupid--politics, maybe, or Daniel's career choices, one of those recurring battles they'd been having for twenty years. Daniel had hung up mid-sentence. Didn't call back.

Was planning to. Eventually. When he cooled down. When his father cooled down.

But eventually never came.

Now Daniel sits in the same kitchen where they used to argue, going through his father's things, and the regret is physical. Heavy. It presses on his chest, makes it hard to breathe, turns ordinary objects into accusations.

If only I'd called back.

If only I'd been less defensive.

If only I'd said I loved him before hanging up.

If only I'd known.

But he didn't know. And he didn't call. And now his father is gone and the past is unchangeable and Daniel is left with this: a sorrow that never softens and a regret that offers no resolution.

"You can't change what happened," his therapist tells him.

Daniel knows that. Intellectually. But knowing doesn't help. Because the past isn't just behind him. It's in him. Alive. Present. Replaying on an endless loop. And every replay ends the same way: with the thing he can never fix.

He finds his father's reading glasses on the kitchen counter. Still there. Three years later, still there, because his mother can't bring herself to move them. Daniel picks them up. The lenses are smudged. His father never cleaned his glasses properly. Daniel used to tease him about it.

He puts them down. Sits at the table. And the loop begins again.

Making Peace With What Cannot Be Changed

Regret is a strange torment.

Unlike fear, which points to the future, or anger, which demands action, regret focuses entirely on the past. On what you did or didn't do. On choices you can't unmake.

And the cruel paradox: the more you regret something, the more impossible it becomes to change it. You replay the moment constantly--not to understand it, but to fix it. To go back and choose differently.

But you can't. The past is fixed. Immutable. Beyond your reach.

And so regret becomes its own kind of prison. You're free to act in the present, but you're emotionally chained to a moment that no longer exists except in memory.

The Bhagavad-gita doesn't tell you to simply "let it go" or "forgive yourself." Instead, it points to something deeper: your relationship with impermanence itself. What if sorrow, though real, is not the final word? What if the wise relate to loss differently--not because they feel less, but because they understand more?

The Gita Speaks: The Wisdom Beyond Sorrow

Krishna opens his entire teaching with a statement about sorrow and regret. It is the very first thing he says to Arjuna after listening to his grief:

    "The Supreme Personality of God said: While you speak with cultured words, you lament over what is not worthy of lament. Those who are wise do not lament for the living or the dead."

    -- Bhagavad-gita 2.11

This verse is often misread as cold--as if Krishna is telling Arjuna to stop feeling. But look more carefully. He doesn't say "don't feel." He says the wise do not lament--meaning their understanding transforms their relationship to loss. They still love. They still lose. But they don't remain imprisoned by the loss, because they understand something about the nature of reality that sorrow obscures.

What do they understand? Krishna continues:

    "For one who has taken his birth, death is certain; and for one who is dead, birth is certain. Therefore, in the unavoidable discharge of your duty, you should not lament."

    -- Bhagavad-gita 2.27

Birth and death are certain. Not possible. Not likely. Certain. This is the nature of embodied existence: everything that begins will end. Every relationship, every body, every moment is temporary. Sorrow arises when we collide with this truth--when we expected permanence and received impermanence instead.

Daniel expected more time with his father. He expected another chance to call back. He expected the relationship to continue. And when it didn't--when impermanence arrived without warning--sorrow flooded in.

But Krishna says: lament is not worthy of the wise. Not because the loss isn't real, but because the wise understand that impermanence is not a tragedy. It is the nature of material existence. Fighting it is like fighting gravity. You don't have to like it. But resisting what is certain only adds suffering to loss.

Then Krishna describes the person who has integrated this understanding:

    "A person who neither rejoices upon achieving something pleasant nor laments upon receiving something unpleasant, who is wise in relation to being, not confused, and who understands the science of God, is already situated in transcendence."

    -- Bhagavad-gita 5.20

Neither rejoicing nor lamenting. This is not emotional numbness. It is equanimity--the capacity to receive both pleasant and unpleasant experiences without being destroyed by either. The wise person feels the loss. But they are not confused by it. They understand being itself. They know that the self which experiences sorrow is eternal, and that the circumstances which cause sorrow are temporary.

And finally, the destination of this understanding:

    "One who is thus transcendentally situated at once realizes the Supreme Brahman. He never laments nor desires to have anything; he is equally disposed to every living entity. In that state he attains pure devotional service unto Me."

    -- Bhagavad-gita 18.54

He never laments. Not because he never loved. Not because he avoided attachment. But because he has realized something so profound about the nature of reality that sorrow, while it may arise, cannot hold him. He is equally disposed to every living entity--those present and those gone, those who treated him well and those who didn't. This is not indifference. It is the deepest possible love: a love that isn't conditional on things staying the way you want them to.

Living the Teaching: Honoring What Was

Daniel starts a practice his grief counselor suggests.

Every morning, he sits quietly and acknowledges three things:

The argument with my father happened. That's real.

I wish I'd called him back. That's real.

This sorrow is not the whole story. Also real.

At first, the third statement feels like a lie. Like he's trying to minimize the loss.

But slowly, he begins to understand the distinction. Honoring the regret doesn't mean living in it. He can acknowledge the wish--I wish I'd called back--without demanding the impossible: that the past be different.

He can hold the sorrow--I miss him--without turning it into self-punishment.

One day, going through old photos, he finds a picture of his father laughing. Taken maybe five years before he died. Daniel remembers that day--just the two of them working on the old car in the garage.

And he realizes: the regret has been so loud that it drowned out everything else. That argument--painful as it was--was one moment in a relationship spanning forty-three years. There were thousands of other moments. Laughter. Connection. Ordinary days where nothing went wrong.

The regret wanted him to reduce the entire relationship to that final week. But it doesn't have to.

He can hold both: the regret and the love. The sorrow and the gratitude.

The past remains unchangeable. But his relationship to it can change.

Practice Box

1. Acknowledge what's real without drowning in it. Name the regret clearly: "I wish I had done X." Don't suppress it. But don't let it become the only truth about the situation.

2. Recognize impermanence as nature, not tragedy. Birth and death are certain. Relationships end. Moments pass. This is not a failure of reality. It is reality. Let this understanding soften the collision with loss.

3. Expand the frame. Regret narrows your vision to one moment. Deliberately recall other moments--good ones, ordinary ones, ones where love was present. The regretted moment is real, but it is not the whole story.

4. Act in the present. You cannot fix yesterday. But you can live today with more awareness, more compassion, more presence. Let regret become a teacher, not a prison.

The Way Forward

Five years after his father's death, Daniel is at a family gathering when his nephew--seventeen, angry, in the middle of a fight with his own father--storms out.

Daniel finds him in the driveway, sitting in his car.

"Want to talk?"

The kid shrugs. "He doesn't get it. He never gets it."

Daniel leans against the car. "Can I tell you something?"

"Sure."

"I had a fight with my dad, week before he died. Stupid fight. I hung up on him. Never called back. Then he was gone."

The kid looks at him. "Damn."

"Yeah. And I regretted it for years. Still do, sometimes. But you know what I learned?"

"What?"

"That I can't change that moment. But I can change this one. And so can you."

The kid is quiet.

"You don't have to make up with your dad right now. You don't even have to want to. But you get to choose how this goes. Whether this fight is the last word, or just one fight in a longer story."

Ten minutes later, the kid goes back inside. Daniel doesn't know if he'll talk to his father tonight. Maybe he will. Maybe he won't.

But Daniel knows this: he just did something his own regret couldn't do. He couldn't go back and call his father. But he could stand in this driveway and offer his nephew a choice. Could turn the sorrow into wisdom. The regret into service.

The past is still unchangeable.

But the present--right now, this moment--is wide open.

And in that openness, Daniel finds something he thought regret had stolen forever: the ability to act with love. Not because the sorrow has disappeared. But because the wise do not remain imprisoned by what they cannot change.

They learn from it. They honor it. And they live.

Reflection

- What past moment do you wish you could change? What would you do differently?
- Can you hold both the regret and the gratitude--the painful moment and the thousands of others?
- How might your sorrow, if you stopped fighting it and started learning from it, change how you live today?

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 12: Uncontrolled Mind

The Runaway

Lisa's trying to meditate.

Eyes closed. Legs crossed. Back straight. Breathing--in, out, in, out. She read the book. Watched the video. Set the timer for ten minutes. This is supposed to help. Calm the mind. Find peace.

In. Out.

Did I respond to that email?

In. Out.

No, back to the breath. Focus.

The breath. Right. In.

Out.

God, my knee hurts. Is it supposed to hurt? Maybe I'm sitting wrong.

Doesn't matter. Back to breath.

In.

What was that noise? Is someone at the door?

No. Nobody's there. You're alone. Breathe.

In.

Why is this so hard? Everyone says meditation is relaxing. This isn't relaxing. This is exhausting. I should be working. I have seventeen things on my to-do list and I'm sitting here doing nothing while my brain does everything and--

Breath. Back to the breath.

In.

Out.

I wonder if Tom's mad at me. He sounded weird on the phone. Was I rude? I don't think I was rude. But maybe--

The timer goes off.

Ten minutes.

It felt like an hour. And also like she didn't meditate at all. Just sat there watching her mind run wild, yanking her attention from breath to email to knee to door to work to Tom to breath again, over and over, an endless loop of thought she couldn't stop, couldn't slow, couldn't control.

Lisa opens her eyes.

"This isn't working," she says to the empty room.

But that's not quite right. It's not that meditation isn't working. It's that her mind isn't working the way she wants it to. Or rather, it's working too much. All the time. Without permission. Without pause.

And she doesn't know how to stop it.

The problem isn't limited to meditation. It follows her everywhere. At dinner with friends, she's physically present but mentally replaying a conversation from three days ago. In meetings, she catches herself drafting grocery lists while her colleague presents quarterly numbers. Lying in bed at night, she watches her mind cycle through tomorrow's anxieties with the relentless energy of a machine that has no off switch.

She's thirty-seven years old and she can't remember the last time she was fully present for a single uninterrupted minute.

The Mind That Won't Be Still

The mind is restless. Not sometimes. Not when you're stressed. Always. Constantly moving, constantly churning, constantly pulling your attention from one thing to the next before you've finished with the first.

You sit down to read--the mind wanders. You try to listen to someone--it interrupts with commentary. You want to sleep--it reviews every conversation from the past week. You want to focus on one task--it opens seventeen tabs simultaneously.

It's not that you're broken. The mind, left to itself, operates like this: chaotic, distractible, compulsive. The Buddhist tradition calls it "monkey mind." Modern psychology calls it the default mode network. The Bhagavad-gita calls it chanchala--unsteady, turbulent, difficult to control.

And it is difficult. Because you're not trying to control something external. You're trying to control the thing you think you are. Your mind feels like "you." The thoughts feel like "yours." So trying to control them creates a paradox: if you're trying to control you, who's in charge?

The Gita's answer is radical: you are not your mind. You are the witness of the mind. And from that position, you can begin to work with the turbulence rather than being drowned by it.

The Gita Speaks: Friend or Enemy

Arjuna knows his mind is out of control. He stands on the battlefield, paralyzed by conflicting thoughts--fight or flee, duty or compassion, honor or love. His mind spins, offering a thousand arguments, none conclusive, all demanding.

Krishna begins not with technique but with truth:

    "A man must elevate himself by his own mind, not degrade himself. The mind is the friend of the conditioned soul, and his enemy as well."

    -- Bhagavad-gita 6.5

The mind can be your friend or your enemy. Not two different minds--the same one. The same instrument that torments Lisa with seventeen simultaneous thoughts could, if directed, solve problems, create beauty, connect with others, contemplate the deepest questions of existence. The mind is not the problem. Its direction is the problem.

Krishna continues:

    "For him who has conquered the mind, the mind is the best of friends; but for one who has failed to do so, his very mind will be the greatest enemy."

    -- Bhagavad-gita 6.6

The best of friends or the greatest enemy. There is no middle ground. An undirected mind doesn't simply idle in neutral. It actively degrades your experience--pulling you into anxiety, regret, fantasy, distraction, compulsive thinking. Left to its own momentum, it will ruin your sleep, fracture your relationships, and steal every present moment from under you.

But conquered--brought under the direction of the conscious self--it becomes the most powerful instrument you possess.

Then Arjuna asks the obvious question. He tells Krishna that controlling the mind is harder than controlling the wind. And Krishna, remarkably, agrees:

    "The Blessed Lord said: O mighty-armed son of Kunti, it is undoubtedly very difficult to curb the restless mind, but it is possible by constant practice and by detachment."

    -- Bhagavad-gita 6.35

It is undoubtedly very difficult. Krishna doesn't minimize the challenge. He validates it. Yes, the mind is restless. Yes, it feels impossible. But--and this is the turning point--it is possible. Not through force. Not through willpower alone. Through two things: constant practice and detachment.

Practice means repeated, patient effort. Not expecting instant control. Not quitting when the mind wanders for the thousandth time. Just gently, persistently bringing it back. Again. And again.

Detachment means not identifying with the thoughts. Seeing them as objects in awareness, not as your self. When a thought arises, you don't have to claim it. You can watch it the way you'd watch a cloud: with interest, perhaps, but without ownership.

And Krishna gives the practical instruction for when the mind wanders:

    "From whatever and wherever the mind wanders due to its flickering and unsteady nature, one must certainly withdraw it and bring it back under the control of the Self."

    -- Bhagavad-gita 6.26

From whatever and wherever. No exceptions. No matter how interesting the thought, how urgent the worry, how compelling the distraction. The practice is always the same: notice the wandering, and bring the mind back. Not with violence. Not with self-recrimination. Just with the steady authority of the Self--the witness, the awareness that is always present beneath the noise.

This is the practice. Not silence. Not emptiness. Not the absence of thought. But the return. Every time the mind wanders and you bring it back, you are exercising sovereignty over the instrument. Every return is a repetition. Every repetition is practice. And practice, Krishna promises, makes it possible.

Living the Teaching: The Practice of Returning

Lisa tries again.

Not because the first attempt worked. But because her teacher said something that helped: "The mind will wander. That's not the problem. The problem is when you don't notice it wandering."

So she sits. Sets the timer. Closes her eyes.

In. Out.

And then: Did I respond to--

She catches it. Not before the thought starts. But she notices: Oh, there's a thought. I'm not breathing anymore. I'm thinking about email.

And then--this is the practice--she doesn't judge it. Just: Ah. Thinking. Back to breath.

In. Out.

Knee hurts.

Ah. Sensation. Back to breath.

In. Out.

What if Tom's--

Ah. Worry. Back to breath.

Over and over. Fifty times in ten minutes. A hundred times. The mind wanders. She notices. She returns.

And something begins to shift. Not that the mind stops wandering--it doesn't. But Lisa starts to recognize something: the thoughts aren't her. They're happening, yes. But to her. To the awareness that watches them.

This distinction changes everything. Because if she's not the thought, then she doesn't have to obey it. Doesn't have to follow it down the rabbit hole. Can just watch it arise, acknowledge it, and return.

The mind is still restless. But she's no longer at its mercy.

She's the witness. And the witness can choose.

Practice Box

1. Choose an anchor. Breath, a mantra, a sensation. This is where you're training the mind to rest.

2. Place attention on the anchor. Gently. Not forcing. Just noticing.

3. Notice when the mind wanders. It will. Within seconds. That's not failure--that's the mind being the mind. Just notice: "Ah, there's a thought."

4. Return without judgment. No scolding. Just: "Back to breath." Each return is the practice. A thousand returns in one session is a thousand repetitions of sovereignty.

The Way Forward

Six months later, Lisa's in a stressful meeting.

Her boss is criticizing her project. In front of the team. The old Lisa would spiral--He hates it. I'm going to get fired. Everyone thinks I'm incompetent.

The spiral would take over. She'd lose track of what's actually being said, consumed by thoughts about what's being said.

But this Lisa has practiced. Six months of returning. Thousands of repetitions.

She feels the spiral start. Feels the first thought hook her: He hates it.

And then: Ah. There's a thought.

She doesn't fight it. Just notices. That's the mind, doing what minds do. Spinning. Catastrophizing.

And she chooses to return. Not to breath--she's in a meeting. But to the actual moment. What's actually happening right now?

Her boss is asking a question. A real question. About implementation details.

He's not saying she's incompetent. He's asking how Phase 2 works.

She answers. Clearly. Because she's here, not lost in the spiral.

After the meeting, she notices: the thoughts still came. But they didn't take her. She saw them, acknowledged them, and chose to return to the present.

The mind is still restless. Still turbulent. Still offering a thousand thoughts she didn't ask for.

But she's no longer drowning in them.

The mind that was her enemy--the runaway that yanked her from moment to moment without permission--is becoming, slowly, through constant practice and detachment, the best of friends.

It still wanders. But now she decides when to follow and when to return.

And that makes all the difference.

Reflection

- When does your mind feel most out of control? What triggers the chaos?
- Can you identify the witness--the part of you that notices the mind wandering?
- What would change if you practiced returning to the present a thousand times, rather than demanding your mind be still on the first try?

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 13: Temptation

The Moment Before

Alex stands in front of the open laptop.

2:37 AM. His wife asleep upstairs. The house silent except for the hum of the refrigerator and the quiet voice in his head saying don't.

The browser is open. One click away. He knows the site. Knows the ritual. Knows exactly what will happen if he clicks: twenty minutes of escape, then the familiar wash of shame, then the promise to himself -- never again -- that will last until the next time temptation shows up at 2 AM when his defenses are down.

He's been here before. Hundreds of times. Maybe thousands.

His hand hovers over the trackpad.

Don't.

But the pull is strong. It's not just desire -- though that's part of it. It's the promise of relief. From the stress of work. From the argument with his wife earlier. From the constant low-grade anxiety that he carries like background noise. From himself.

The temptation isn't offering happiness. It's offering absence. A brief escape from the person he is, the problems he has, the life he's living.

Just for twenty minutes. Then he'll come back. Deal with reality then.

His finger moves toward the trackpad.

And in that space -- the microsecond between intention and action -- something else is present. Not the voice saying don't. Something quieter. More fundamental.

Who is choosing right now?


Reflective Bridge

Temptation is universal.

Not everyone's tempted by the same things. For Alex, it's pornography. For someone else, it might be alcohol, gambling, food, shopping, an emotional affair, cutting corners at work, gossiping, scrolling social media for hours -- anything that offers immediate gratification at the cost of long-term well-being.

But the mechanism is the same. There's a desire -- strong, immediate, insistent. There's a choice -- clear, often binary. And there's a conflict between what you want right now and what you value over time.

Your mind doesn't distinguish between survival-level needs and passing cravings. It treats the urge for pornography or whiskey or revenge with the same urgency it treats the need for oxygen. So when the desire arises, it feels mandatory. Like you have to satisfy it or you'll die. Even though you know -- intellectually, consciously -- that you won't.

Most of the time, the choice feels predetermined. Like the desire is too strong, the pull too powerful, the habit too ingrained. You tell yourself you had no choice.

But the Bhagavad-gita says something different: you always have a choice. But to see it, you have to recognize who's choosing.


The Gita Speaks: The Force That Carries You Away

Arjuna knows what it's like to want to do the right thing and feel unable to. On the battlefield, overwhelmed, he asks Krishna the question Alex is asking at 2:37 AM: Why can't I control myself?

Krishna answers with devastating honesty:

  "The senses are so strong and impetuous, Oh Arjuna, that they forcibly carry away the mind even of a man of discrimination who is endeavoring to control them."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 2.60

Even a man of discrimination. Even someone who knows better. The senses don't care about your resolutions, your promises, your values. They are impetuous -- they move faster than thought, faster than intention.

Alex knows better. He knows where the click leads. He's a man of discrimination. And yet the senses carry him away.

But Krishna doesn't stop there. He doesn't simply describe the problem and leave Arjuna helpless. He offers the way through:

  "One who restrains his senses and fixes his consciousness upon Me is known as a man of steady intelligence."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 2.61

Restraint. Not through gritted teeth and willpower alone -- that battle against the senses, as verse 2.60 just admitted, is one even the wise lose. The restraint Krishna describes has a positive anchor: fixes his consciousness upon Me. The senses are redirected, not merely suppressed. You don't defeat desire by fighting it head-on. You defeat it by placing your attention somewhere higher.

And then Krishna offers the image that changes everything:

  "A person who is not disturbed by the incessant flow of desires -- that enter like rivers into the ocean which is ever being filled but is always still -- can alone achieve peace, and not the man who strives to satisfy such desires."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 2.70

The ocean doesn't fight the rivers. It doesn't build dams. It doesn't panic when water rushes in. It simply is -- vast, deep, undisturbed. The rivers enter, and the ocean remains still.

That's the model. Desires will flow. They always will. You don't achieve peace by eliminating every desire -- that's impossible. You achieve peace by becoming so deep, so anchored in your true self, that the desires can enter without disturbing you.

Alex doesn't need to never feel temptation again. He needs to become the ocean.

But how? Krishna acknowledges the difficulty:

  "This divine energy of Mine, consisting of the three modes of material nature, is difficult to overcome. But those who have surrendered unto Me can easily cross beyond it."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 7.14

Difficult to overcome. Krishna doesn't minimize the struggle. The pull of material nature -- the modes of goodness, passion, and ignorance that drive every craving -- is genuinely formidable. But the solution isn't superhuman willpower. It's surrender. Those who turn toward something higher than their own cravings find that the cravings lose their absolute power.

Not because the cravings disappear. But because the person is no longer standing alone against them.


Living the Teaching

Alex's finger is still hovering over the trackpad.

But something has shifted. That question -- Who is choosing right now? -- has created a gap.

He's aware of the desire. Can feel it. Strong. Insistent. But he's also aware of being aware of it. Like he's watching himself from a slight distance.

The desire is happening to him. But it's not him.

He tries something new. Instead of fighting the desire or giving in to it, he just... watches it. There it is. The pull. The promise of relief. The familiar script: just this once, then never again.

He doesn't judge it. Doesn't scold himself for having it. Just notices it the way he'd notice a cloud passing or a sound outside.

And in the noticing, something happens: the urgency softens. Not gone. But less absolute. Less like a command he has to obey.

The rivers are flowing. But the ocean is still.

He closes the laptop. Not in a dramatic gesture of victory. Just: closes it. Stands up. Goes to the kitchen. Drinks water. Stands there in the dark, aware that the desire is still present but no longer in control.

Five minutes pass. Then ten. The desire is fading. Not because he conquered it through willpower. But because he didn't feed it with action or with resistance. He just let it be there. Watched it. And discovered it's temporary.

All desires are temporary. They arise. They peak. They pass.

But you -- the witness -- are constant. And from that constancy, you can choose.


Practice Box

1. Name the temptation. When the desire arises, name it clearly: "There's the urge to drink." "There's the craving for sugar." "There's the pull toward the forbidden." Naming creates distance -- it turns a command into an observation.

2. Become the ocean, not the dam. Don't fight the desire head-on. That battle, as Krishna warns, overwhelms even the wise. Instead, let the desire flow in without being disturbed by it. Watch it. It's a river. You're the ocean.

3. Fix your attention higher. The senses carry you away when your consciousness has nothing else to hold onto. Find your anchor -- prayer, a purpose, a person you don't want to betray, the self that exists beyond the craving. Redirect, don't merely restrain.

4. Wait it out. Desires peak and pass. The intensity you feel at 2:37 AM will not be the same at 2:47 AM. Give yourself ten minutes. The river will slow.


The Way Forward

Three months later, Alex faces temptation again.

Same time. Same setup. 2 AM, can't sleep, laptop open, desire present.

But he's practiced. Not perfectly. He's slipped a few times. But he's practiced the pause. The witnessing. The ocean.

Tonight, he feels the pull. Acknowledges it. There it is.

And then, instead of fighting or fleeing or giving in, he asks himself: What do I actually need right now?

Not what the desire is promising. But what he actually needs.

The answer surprises him: connection.

He's lonely. Has been for months. The marriage is strained. He and his wife haven't really talked -- really connected -- in weeks. They coexist. Manage logistics. But don't touch the real stuff.

The pornography was offering a substitute. A simulation. A way to feel something without the vulnerability of real connection.

But he knows now: it doesn't work. Never has. The relief is temporary. The loneliness returns. Often worse.

So he makes a different choice.

He closes the laptop. Goes upstairs. Sits on the edge of the bed where his wife is sleeping.

Gently touches her shoulder.

She stirs. "Alex? You okay?"

"Can we talk?"

"Now? It's two in the morning."

"I know. I'm sorry. But... yeah. Can we?"

She sits up. Looks at him. Something in his face must tell her this matters.

"Okay," she says.

And they talk. For an hour. About the distance. The resentment. The loneliness. The ways they've both been hiding.

It's hard. Uncomfortable. No instant resolution.

But it's real. Not a simulation. Not an escape. Real connection. Real vulnerability.

The desire for escape is still there, somewhere in the background. Probably always will be. The rivers never stop flowing. But Alex has discovered something the Gita promised: those who surrender to something higher than their cravings cross beyond material nature's grip.

Not because the grip releases. But because they've found something stronger to hold onto.

He is not his cravings. Not his impulses. Not his compulsions. He is the ocean -- ever being filled, always still.

And from that stillness, he's free. Not free from temptation. But free within it. Free to choose.


Reflection

- What's your temptation? What pulls you off course from who you want to be?
- Are you fighting desire head-on (the dam), or can you learn to let it flow through without being disturbed (the ocean)?
- What would change if you surrendered your struggle to something higher than willpower alone?


From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 14: Greed

The Hunger That Never Fills

Rachel remembers when $100,000 felt like wealth.

She was twenty-eight, just promoted to senior analyst, and that first six-figure salary felt transformative. This is it, she thought. This is enough.

It wasn't.

Within six months, the lifestyle adjusted. Better apartment. Nicer car. Restaurants she would have considered absurd extravagance a year before became normal Tuesday nights. The $100,000 became baseline. Necessary. Barely comfortable.

When she made partner at thirty-four, the number jumped to $250,000. Again, that feeling: Now. Now this is enough.

It lasted maybe three months.

Now, at forty-one, Rachel makes $680,000 a year. Her portfolio is worth $2.3 million. She has the apartment, the car, the wardrobe, the vacations. Everything she once thought would mean she'd "made it."

And she's looking at a job posting for a position that would pay $850,000.

She doesn't need it. Doesn't even want the job particularly -- more stress, longer hours, less time for the life she already barely lives. But the number pulls at her.

Because what if $850,000 is the threshold? What if that's the amount where the hunger finally stops?

Deep down, she knows the truth. There is no threshold. The hunger doesn't stop at any number. It just adjusts. Adapts. Always hungry. Never filled.


Reflective Bridge

Greed isn't about wanting things. It's about believing that having more things will satisfy the want. That acquisition leads to contentment. That there's a finish line somewhere ahead where you'll finally have enough and the hunger will stop.

But here's what actually happens: you get the thing -- the raise, the house, the car, the achievement. And for a brief moment you feel satisfied. Then the baseline shifts. What was once extraordinary becomes ordinary. What was once "more than enough" becomes barely sufficient. And the hunger returns, now aiming at something even further away.

The Bhagavad-gita calls it lobha -- greed. The insatiable desire for acquisition that can never be satisfied because it's not actually about the objects you're pursuing. It's about trying to fill an internal emptiness with external things.

And that never works. Because the emptiness isn't a lack of money, possessions, or status. It's a disconnection from your essential self -- the eternal consciousness that is already complete, already whole, already enough.

But greed doesn't know that. Greed thinks the next acquisition will do it. The next milestone. The next number.

Just a little more. Then I'll be happy. Then I'll be safe. Then I'll be enough.


The Gita Speaks: The Three Gates and the Three Modes

Krishna names greed as one of three gates to destruction:

  "There are three gates leading to this hell -- lust, anger, and greed. Every sane man should give these up, for they lead to the degradation of the soul."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 16.21

Lust. Anger. Greed. Notice they're linked. Lust is desire for pleasure. Anger is desire thwarted. Greed is desire insatiable -- the craving that no amount of satisfaction can quench. And Krishna says these lead to "degradation of the soul." Not in some metaphorical afterlife, but here, now, in this life. When you're ruled by greed, you reduce your infinite nature to a hungry ghost -- always consuming, never full.

Rachel makes $680,000 and feels poor. Not financially poor. Existentially poor. That's degradation -- not of her bank account, but of her capacity to experience sufficiency.

But why does greed take hold so completely? Krishna explains the mechanism:

  "From the mode of goodness, real knowledge develops; from the mode of passion, grief develops; and from the mode of ignorance, foolishness, madness and illusion develop."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 14.17

Three modes govern human consciousness. When goodness prevails, you see clearly -- you recognize what's enough, what truly satisfies. When passion dominates, you chase and acquire, and the result is grief -- the hollow feeling Rachel knows well after every raise that fails to satisfy. When ignorance rules, you can't even see what you're doing. You accumulate blindly, confusing quantity with quality, possessions with peace.

Rachel's greed isn't evil. It's passion -- the mode that drives restless activity, acquisition, and the endless pursuit of more. And as Krishna says plainly, the fruit of passion is grief. Not satisfaction. Grief.

So what's the way out? Not more acquisition. Not even the renunciation of all possessions. Something deeper:

  "One should perform sacrifice, penance and charity with the word tat. The purpose of such transcendental activities is to get free from the material entanglement."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 17.25

Tat -- "that," meaning the Absolute, the transcendent purpose beyond personal gain. The antidote to greed isn't deprivation. It's redirection. Instead of acquiring for yourself, you act for something beyond yourself. Sacrifice. Penance. Charity. Not as guilt offerings, but as a shift in orientation -- from "What can I get?" to "What can I give?"

When Rachel earns for herself, no amount is enough. But when earning becomes an instrument of contribution -- charity, service, support -- the relationship to money transforms. The number stops being a measure of her worth and becomes a tool for her purpose.


Living the Teaching

Rachel doesn't apply for the $850,000 job.

Not because she's conquered greed. But because she's started asking a different question.

Instead of How much do I need to feel enough? she asks: What if I'm already enough?

It sounds simple. Almost trite. But when she sits with it -- really sits with it -- it's revolutionary.

She makes a list one evening. Not of what she wants, but of what she has. Enough money to live comfortably. Enough food to eat well. Enough shelter to be safe. Enough health to function.

Actually -- more than enough. She has surplus. Significant surplus. And yet the hunger persists. Not for things she needs, but for more -- as if more itself were the point.

She tries an experiment. For one month, she doesn't acquire anything new. No new clothes. No new gadgets. No upgrades. No impulse purchases. If something breaks, she replaces it. If she needs something, she gets it. But nothing beyond need.

The first week is uncomfortable. She feels deprived. Notices how often she thinks I should get that or I deserve this. The mode of passion screaming for activity.

But then something shifts.

By week three, the constant background hum of wanting starts to quiet. Not gone. But softer. And in that quiet, she notices something she's been too busy acquiring to feel: she has enough. She is enough.

She begins redirecting. Not dramatically -- she doesn't sell everything and move to an ashram. But she starts giving. Systematically. Deliberately. A percentage of every paycheck to causes she cares about. Time to people who need it. Attention to work that serves something beyond her own advancement.

And something unexpected happens: the giving satisfies in a way that getting never did. Not because giving is morally superior. But because it breaks the cycle -- the closed loop of earn-acquire-want more that passion creates.

The mode of goodness, Krishna said, produces real knowledge. And the knowledge Rachel is discovering is this: she was never actually hungry for money. She was hungry for meaning. And meaning comes not from accumulation but from contribution.


Practice Box

1. Name what you have. Make a list. Be specific. What do you already possess? What needs are already met? Let yourself see the sufficiency that greed blinds you to.

2. Identify the mode. Is your desire coming from goodness (clear seeing), passion (restless craving), or ignorance (blind accumulation)? The mode of passion produces grief, not satisfaction. Recognizing which mode is driving you is the first step to changing it.

3. Redirect from getting to giving. The antidote to greed isn't deprivation -- it's purposeful generosity. Give something this week. Money, time, attention. Not to feel virtuous. But to break the cycle of acquisition.

4. Practice non-acquisition. For one week, don't buy anything beyond genuine necessity. Notice what happens. Does the hunger intensify? Fade? Reveal itself as habit rather than need?


The Way Forward

Two years later, Rachel turns down another offer.

This one would have made her a millionaire many times over -- equity in a startup, high risk but potentially transformative wealth.

Her younger self would have taken it without thinking. More money = more security = more worth.

But this Rachel knows better.

She doesn't turn it down because she's ascetic or anti-wealth. She turns it down because she's clear about her values: time with her partner, creative work that matters to her, the ability to sleep at night without the stress of high-stakes gambling.

The money would have come at a cost. And she's no longer willing to pay that cost for the promise of "more."

She has enough. Financially. Materially. And more importantly -- existentially.

The hunger still visits sometimes. Old habits die hard. She'll see someone's success and feel the familiar pang: I should have that. I deserve that. The mode of passion, pulling.

But now she recognizes it for what it is: not truth, but greed. One of three gates Krishna warned about. She acknowledges it. Watches it. Lets it pass.

And returns to what's actually here: this moment, this breath, this life that is -- already, always -- enough.

The fire of greed doesn't consume her anymore. Not because she extinguished it through deprivation. But because she stopped feeding it with the belief that more would ever be enough.

And in that stopping, she found what greed had been promising all along: contentment. Peace. Sufficiency.

Not from having everything. But from recognizing she already is everything she needs to be.


Reflection

- What's your "enough" number? And when you reach it, do you think the hunger will actually stop?
- Which mode is driving your desire for more -- goodness, passion, or ignorance? What fruit is it producing?
- What would change if you redirected even a fraction of your acquiring energy toward giving?


From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 15: Lust

The Obsession

Thomas can't stop thinking about her.

Not his wife -- though he loves his wife, married fifteen years, two kids, good life. This is someone else. Maya. The new project manager at work. Twenty-nine. Smart. Funny. The kind of presence that makes a room feel different when she walks in.

It started innocently. Meetings. Emails. Professional. Then coffee to discuss the Mitchell account. Then lunch. Then texts that weren't quite about work but weren't quite not about work either.

Nothing's happened. Nothing physical. But something's happening.

Thomas is obsessed.

He checks his phone constantly -- did she text? He manufactures reasons to walk past her desk. He replays their conversations, analyzing every word for hidden meaning. He fantasizes -- not just sexually, though that too -- but emotionally. What if. What could be. What it would feel like to be seen by her the way he imagines she sees him.

He knows it's destructive. Knows he's playing with fire. Knows where this leads if he doesn't stop.

But he can't stop. Or won't. The line between can't and won't has blurred.

Because the obsession isn't just about Maya. It's about the feeling she represents: aliveness. The marriage has become routine. Comfortable. Safe. And somewhere in that safety, he stopped feeling fully alive. Stopped feeling desired. Stopped feeling like he matters beyond being the provider, the dad, the reliable one.

Maya -- or the fantasy of Maya -- offers escape from that. A return to intensity. To being wanted. To mattering in a way that feels electric instead of merely functional.

So he feeds the obsession. Justifies it. It's just thinking. Just feeling. Nothing's happened.

But Thomas knows the truth: lust has already taken him. Not by making him do something. But by making him want to.


Reflective Bridge

Lust isn't just sexual desire. It's obsessive desire. The kind that consumes attention, distorts perception, overrides values. The kind that makes you willing to destroy what you have for the promise of what you want.

And it operates through a simple mechanism: it convinces you that satisfaction is available if you just act on the desire. That the itch will be scratched, the hunger fed, the emptiness filled -- if you just give in.

But acting on lust doesn't satisfy it. It intensifies it. The affair doesn't cure the loneliness -- it creates more. The conquest doesn't build self-worth -- it erodes it. The forbidden pleasure doesn't bring peace -- it brings chaos.

Because lust isn't actually about the object of desire. It's about the craving itself. And craving can never be satisfied by getting what it wants. It can only be satisfied by ending the craving.

Thomas isn't thinking about work anymore. Isn't present with his kids. Barely sees his wife even when they're in the same room. His attention is colonized -- completely, relentlessly -- by the obsession.

He's lost himself. Not to Maya. But to lust.


The Gita Speaks: The All-Devouring Enemy

When Arjuna asks Krishna what compels people to act against their own values -- what force makes them sin even when they know better -- Krishna's answer is immediate and unambiguous:

  "The Blessed Lord said: It is lust only, Arjuna, which is born of contact with the material modes of passion and later transformed into wrath, and which is the all-devouring, sinful enemy of this world."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 3.37

Lust. Born of passion. Transformed into wrath when thwarted. All-devouring.

Notice the progression: first desire, then attachment, then obsession. And when the desire is blocked, it becomes anger -- at your spouse for being in the way, at yourself for having values, at reality for not conforming to your fantasy. The anger justifies destruction: I deserve this. Anyone standing in my way is the enemy.

Thomas hasn't reached the anger stage yet. But he's moving toward it. Every time his wife interrupts a text exchange with Maya, he feels a flash of irritation. Every time his kids need attention during a fantasy, he resents the intrusion. Lust is already transforming into wrath.

Krishna doesn't leave Arjuna without a strategy:

  "Therefore, O Arjuna, best of the Bharatas, in the very beginning curb this great symbol of sin lust by regulating the senses, and slay this destroyer of knowledge and self-realization."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 3.41

In the very beginning. Not after the affair. Not after the marriage crumbles. Not after the damage is done. At the start. When the desire first takes root. Because lust, like fire, is easiest to extinguish when it's small. Wait, and it consumes everything.

"Regulating the senses" -- this means controlling what you feed. Every text to Maya is fuel. Every glance is kindling. Every fantasy is oxygen. The fire doesn't grow by itself. Thomas is feeding it.

But regulation alone isn't enough. Willpower exhausts itself. Krishna points to the deeper solution:

  "Thus knowing oneself to be transcendental to material senses, mind and intelligence, one should control the lower self by the higher self and thus -- by spiritual strength -- conquer this insatiable enemy known as lust."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 3.43

Knowing oneself to be transcendental. The senses crave Maya. The mind fantasizes about her. The intelligence rationalizes the pursuit. But Thomas is none of these. He is the self beyond senses, mind, and intelligence -- the witness who can observe the entire machinery of lust without being identical to it.

When he identifies with the craving, it's irresistible. When he identifies with the witness, he has space to choose.

Krishna also reveals why the wise don't pursue pleasures born of sensory contact:

  "An intelligent person does not take part in the sources of misery, which are due to contact with the material senses. O son of Kunti, such pleasures have a beginning and an end, and so the wise man does not delight in them."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 5.22

A beginning and an end. That's the nature of sense pleasure. The thrill of the forbidden has a beginning -- the first text, the first private lunch. And it has an end -- the aftermath, the guilt, the destruction. The wise person sees the whole arc, not just the peak. Thomas is staring at the peak. He needs to see the ending.

And finally, the warning that frames the entire struggle:

  "There are three gates leading to this hell -- lust, anger, and greed. Every sane man should give these up, for they lead to the degradation of the soul."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 16.21

Three gates. Lust is the first. If Thomas walks through it, anger and greed follow. The degradation isn't theoretical. It's the slow erosion of everything he actually values -- his family, his integrity, his capacity for real love -- in service of a craving that promises satisfaction and delivers ruin.


Living the Teaching

Thomas is sitting in his car in the work parking lot.

Maya just texted: Drink after work?

His finger hovers over the keyboard. The yes is already formed. He can feel it. The pull. The justification: It's just a drink. Nothing has to happen. We're adults.

But then something interrupts the script. Not a voice. Not a thought. Just... awareness.

This is lust. This is the all-devouring enemy.

He feels the craving in his body. The way his attention is completely captured. The way everything else -- his wife, his kids, his integrity -- has faded to background noise while this one thing screams in the foreground.

And he remembers: in the very beginning. Not later. Not after. Now.

He also remembers: such pleasures have a beginning and an end. He's staring at the beginning. But where does it end? He knows. Everyone knows. It ends in wreckage.

He types a different response: Can't tonight. But thank you.

Hits send.

And then sits there, feeling the craving surge. It's furious. Demanding. You idiot. You're missing your chance.

The lust transforming into wrath. Just as Krishna described.

But he doesn't move. He watches the craving. Observes it the way he'd observe a storm -- intense, temporary, not actually him. He is transcendental to the senses, the mind, the intelligence. He is the witness.

Ten minutes pass. The intensity fades. Not gone. But less absolute.

He drives home. Walks in the door. His wife is making dinner. His kids doing homework. Ordinary. Familiar. Safe.

And he feels something unexpected: gratitude. Not because he conquered lust forever. But because he didn't feed it today. Didn't strengthen it. Didn't walk through the first gate.

He chose his actual life over the fantasy. And his actual life, he remembers now, is enough.


Practice Box

1. Curb it at the beginning. Don't wait until you're deep in the obsession. The moment you recognize the pattern -- the extra attention, the manufactured encounters, the fantasy -- act. Reduce contact. Stop feeding the fire while it's still small.

2. See the whole arc. Lust shows you the peak. Force yourself to see the ending. Where does this lead in six months? A year? What's destroyed? Such pleasures have a beginning and an end.

3. Identify as the witness, not the craving. You are transcendental to the senses, mind, and intelligence. The craving is happening in you, not as you. Find the one who observes the desire. Choose from there.

4. Name the real hunger. What is the lust actually about? Connection? Aliveness? Being seen? Find the legitimate need beneath the destructive craving, and meet it through means that don't require burning your life down.


The Way Forward

Six months later, Maya leaves the company.

Takes a job in another city. Thomas feels relief. Not because he stopped being attracted to her. But because the removal of proximity starves the obsession of fuel.

And in the space created by her absence, Thomas does something he's avoided for years: he talks to his wife.

Not about Maya -- that's his to carry, his to work through. But about the things beneath the obsession. The loneliness. The feeling of being invisible. The way they've drifted into routine and stopped really seeing each other.

It's hard. Vulnerable. Uncomfortable.

But it's real. Not a fantasy. Not an escape. Real connection with the real person he's actually committed to.

And slowly -- not dramatically, not instantly -- things shift. They start dating again. Actual dates. Conversation. Presence. They remember why they chose each other fifteen years ago. They build something new on the foundation of what already is.

The lust still visits sometimes. New objects. New fantasies. The mind does what minds do -- generates desire, creates attraction, offers escape. Such pleasures will always have a beginning.

But Thomas has learned to see the end too.

He doesn't have to obey the craving. He is the witness. Transcendental to senses, mind, and intelligence. And the witness can watch desire arise, acknowledge it, and choose whether or not to feed it.

Most days now, he chooses not to. Not because he's morally superior. But because he's tasted both paths -- the path of feeding obsession, which promises satisfaction and delivers destruction, and the path of witnessing desire, which offers nothing dramatic but makes actual life available again.

He chooses availability. He chooses presence in what's actually here.

Not because it's easy. But because it's true. And in that truth, he finds what lust had been promising all along: aliveness. Connection. Being seen.

Not through possession of the fantasy. But through presence in reality.


Reflection

- What desire has captured your attention so completely that everything else has faded?
- Can you see the whole arc -- not just the peak of pleasure, but the beginning and the end?
- What would it mean to identify as the witness rather than the craving -- to conquer lust not through willpower alone, but through spiritual strength?


From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 16: Work

The 9-to-5 Trap

Kenji's alarm goes off at 6:15 AM.

He doesn't hit snooze. He learned years ago that snooze just makes it worse -- delays the inevitable while making you feel guilty about delaying it.

So he gets up. Showers. Coffee. Toast. Commute. Email. Meetings. Lunch at desk. More meetings. More email. Commute home. Dinner. TV. Sleep.

Repeat.

He's been doing this for twelve years. Software engineer at a mid-sized company. Good salary. Benefits. Stable. Safe.

And he's dying inside.

Not dramatically. Not in a way anyone would notice. He still does his job. Meets deadlines. Participates in meetings. Smiles at appropriate moments.

But inside, there's a quiet desperation. A sense that he's trading his life -- his actual, finite, irreplaceable life -- for a paycheck. That he's spending the majority of his waking hours doing something that doesn't matter to him, doesn't fulfill him, doesn't connect to anything he actually values.

His friends say he's lucky. Stable job in an unstable economy. He should be grateful.

And he is grateful. Intellectually. But gratitude doesn't change the fact that every Sunday evening, he feels a heaviness settle in. The weekend is ending. Monday is coming. And with Monday comes the return to the trap.

The question he can't shake: Is this all there is? Work until retirement, then finally live?

But retirement is thirty years away. And thirty years of this feels like a life sentence.


Reflective Bridge

Most of us have a complicated relationship with work. We need it -- for money, for survival, for identity, for structure. But we resent it -- for the time it takes, the energy it drains, the life it displaces.

Modern culture gives us two equally unsatisfying narratives. The first: "Just endure it." Work is suffering. That's adulthood. Retirement is the reward. The second: "Follow your passion." Quit the soul-sucking job. Do what you love. Money will follow.

Both are incomplete. The first makes work a prison with no meaning beyond survival. The second makes work dependent on feeling passionate, which most people don't feel most of the time.

The Bhagavad-gita offers a third path. Not endurance. Not passion-chasing. But dharma -- your role, your duty, your function in the larger order.

And the radical claim the Gita makes is this: any work -- even work you didn't choose, even work that feels mundane -- can become meaningful if you understand its place in the larger whole and perform it as an offering rather than as a transaction.

Not "I work for money." Not "I work because I'm passionate." But: "I work as my contribution to the functioning of the world. This is my role right now. And I can perform it with excellence, regardless of whether it makes me happy."


The Gita Speaks: Sacrifice, Standards, and Surrender

The entire Bhagavad-gita is, at its core, a conversation about work. Arjuna doesn't want to do his job. He's a warrior. The battlefield is his workplace. But he doesn't want to fight. The work feels wrong, painful, meaningless.

He says to Krishna:
