An intelligent person recognizes that these pleasures have a beginning and an end. Sophie's promotion will age. The title will fade. The recognition will be forgotten. Everything Malik envies is temporary, and delighting in temporary things, whether possessing them or resenting others for possessing them, leads only to suffering.

But why does envy take such deep root? Because it is the opposite of the qualities Krishna holds dear:

"One who is not envious but who is a kind friend to all living entities, who does not think himself a proprietor, who is free from false ego and equal both in happiness and distress, who is always satisfied and engaged in devotional service with determination and whose mind and intelligence are in agreement with Me-he is very dear to Me."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 12.13-14

Notice the connection: Envy arises from false ego. From the belief that you own your achievements. That you deserve recognition. That your worth is determined by external validation.

When you think you are the proprietor of your success, then someone else's success feels like theft. Like they took what was yours.

But the Gita teaches: You are not the proprietor. You are the instrument. Your talents, opportunities, circumstances, all given. Your role is to use them skillfully, not to claim ownership.

And Krishna warns clearly about what happens when envy is left unchecked:

"Those who are envious and mischievous, who are the lowest among men, are cast by Me into the ocean of material existence, into various demoniac species of life."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 16.19

This is not punishment. It is consequence. Envy traps you in a cycle of comparison, resentment, and suffering. It makes you incapable of joy, yours or anyone else's. It turns every success around you into evidence of your own failure.

But liberation is possible. And it comes through a surprising door:

"And one who listens with faith and without envy becomes free from sinful reaction and attains to the planets where the pious dwell."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 18.71

Without envy. That is the key. When you can hear of another's good fortune, another's wisdom, another's achievement, and feel not resentment but openness, you become free. Faith without envy is the exit from the burning house.

Living the Teaching

So what does Malik do?

Pretend he is not envious? Suppress the feeling? Force himself to be happy for Sophie?

No. The Gita does not teach repression. It teaches transformation.

Practice Box

The Envy Transmutation Practice

1. Name the feeling honestly. Do not pretend you are not envious. Do not spiritually bypass with "I'm happy for them." Admit it: "I feel envy. I resent their success." Honesty is the first step.

2. Recognize the temporary nature of what you envy. The promotion, the title, the recognition: all of it has a beginning and an end. The wise person does not delight in temporary pleasures, and does not suffer over another's temporary gains.

3. Trace it to false ego. Ask: "What am I believing about myself that makes their success feel like my failure?" Usually it is: "I am the proprietor of my achievements. I deserve recognition." Release ownership. You are the instrument, not the cause.

4. Practice listening without envy. The next time someone shares good news, notice your reaction. Can you hear it with faith and openness? Not fake positivity, but genuine freedom from resentment? Start there. That single shift changes everything.

The Way Forward

Three weeks after Sophie's promotion, Malik sends her a message:

Can we grab coffee?

She responds immediately: Of course. Tomorrow?

They meet at the cafe near the office. It is awkward at first. Malik can feel the distance that has grown between them.

Sophie breaks the silence. "I know you applied for the position too."

Malik nods.

"I'm sorry," she says. "I know that's weird to say. It's not like I took it from you. But I know you wanted it."

Malik takes a breath. "I've been envious. I want to be honest about that. I've spent the last three weeks resenting you. Telling myself you didn't deserve it as much as I did. And I hate that about myself."

Sophie looks surprised. Then sad. "I didn't deserve it more than you. You know that, right?"

"Maybe," Malik says. "But that's not the point. The point is I made your success about me. Like it was something you took from me instead of something you earned."

He pauses. "And I realized something. Everything I'm envious of, the title, the office, the recognition, it all has an expiration date. It's temporary. And I was making myself miserable over temporary things."

"So what changed?"

"I stopped thinking of myself as the owner of my results. I did the work. I showed up. But the outcome was never mine to control. And it was never mine to resent you for."

Sophie reaches across the table, touches his hand briefly. "For what it's worth, I'll probably need your advice constantly."

Malik smiles, for the first time in weeks, genuinely. "I'll send you my consulting rates."

They both laugh. And something eases.

The envy does not disappear completely. It is still there, a dull ache when he sees her name on leadership emails. But it is not consuming him anymore.

Because he stopped feeding it. Stopped telling himself the story that her gain was his loss. Stopped clinging to the belief that he owned his achievements and therefore deserved specific recognition.

He did the work. He used his skills. He performed his duty.

The results were never his to control. And he learned to listen to the news of another's success without envy.

In that, the Gita teaches, is freedom.

Reflection

- Whose success triggers envy in you? What story are you telling yourself about why their achievement diminishes yours?
- Can you see that the things you envy, titles, recognition, status, all have a beginning and an end? What would change if you stopped delighting in temporary things?
- What would it mean to listen with faith and without envy? Not to suppress resentment, but to genuinely release it? What freedom might that bring?

---

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 33: Discriminated

Opening Story

Aisha walks into the conference room and feels it immediately.

The shift. The slight pause in conversation. The way eyes flicker toward her, then away.

She is the only Black woman in the room. Again.

The meeting is about the new product launch. She is the lead designer. Her work, her vision, her strategy. But when the VP starts asking questions, he directs them to Tom. Her white, male colleague. Who works for her.

"Tom, walk us through the design rationale."

Tom glances at Aisha, uncomfortable. "Actually, Aisha led this. She should--"

"Right, right," the VP interrupts. "But give us the technical overview."

Tom does. Because what else can he do?

And Aisha sits there, smiling professionally, while her work is discussed as if she is not in the room.

This is not new. It has happened in every company she has worked for. Every meeting. Every presentation. The subtle erosion of her presence, her authority, her expertise.

Not overtly racist. No one uses slurs. No one tells her she does not belong.

They just act like she is not there. Ask her male colleagues to explain her ideas. Question her decisions more than anyone else's. Assume she is junior when she is senior. Compliment her for being "articulate" like it is surprising.

Death by a thousand cuts.

And the worst part? When she tries to name it, she is told she is being sensitive. Reading into things. Playing the victim.

So she stops naming it. Swallows it. Performs twice as hard to get half the recognition.

And slowly, imperceptibly, she is eroding from the inside out.

Reflective Bridge

Discrimination does not always announce itself.

Sometimes it is loud, hateful, overt, undeniable. But more often, it is quiet. Insidious. A pattern you feel but cannot quite prove.

The way your resume gets passed over despite your qualifications. The way your ideas are ignored until a man repeats them. The way you are followed in stores, questioned by security, assumed to be the help instead of the guest.

It is exhausting. Not just the discrimination itself, but the gaslighting that follows.

Are you sure that's what happened? Maybe they just didn't see you. You're being too sensitive.

Until you start questioning yourself. Am I imagining this? Am I making it about race when it is not?

But you are not imagining it. The data proves it. The patterns prove it. Your lived experience proves it.

And yet you are expected to smile. To not make people uncomfortable. To be grateful for the scraps of recognition you do get.

Aisha knows this dance well. She has perfected it. The professional mask. The strategic silence. The careful modulation of her tone so she is not "aggressive."

But the cost is immense. Every time she swallows her truth, she loses a piece of herself.

The Gita speaks to this. Not about discrimination specifically, but about dignity in the face of injustice. About a vision so clear it sees through every false designation the world imposes.

The Gita Speaks: Equal Vision

Krishna offers a radical standard for seeing reality as it truly is:

"The humble sage, by virtue of true knowledge, sees with equal vision a learned and gentle brahmana, a cow, an elephant, a dog and a dog-eater outcaste."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 5.18

Look carefully at that list. It contains every category the ancient world used to sort beings into hierarchies: the scholar, the animal, the untouchable. And Krishna says the wise person sees through all of it. Every designation. Every category. Every hierarchy.

The discrimination Aisha faces comes from people who cannot see her soul. They see only her skin. Only her gender. They are trapped in the illusion that external markers determine worth.

But that is their blindness. Not her deficiency.

And Krishna takes this further:

"Those whose minds are established in sameness and equanimity have already conquered the conditions of birth and death. They are flawless like Brahman, and thus they are already situated in Brahman."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 5.19

The person established in equanimity has conquered the very conditions that produce discrimination: the illusion that birth determines value. The person who sees with equal vision is not merely tolerant. They have transcended the entire framework of hierarchy. They are, Krishna says, flawless.

Which means the VP who cannot see Aisha is not merely rude. He is spiritually impoverished. He has not conquered the most basic illusion.

And what does true equality look like in practice?

"He is a perfect yogi who, by comparison to his own self, sees the true equality of all beings, both in their happiness and distress, O Arjuna!"
  -- Bhagavad-gita 6.32

The perfect yogi feels your happiness as their happiness. Your distress as their distress. This is not abstract philosophy. It is the highest standard of human consciousness. And anyone who discriminates has fallen from it entirely.

But here is the part that speaks most directly to Aisha. Krishna himself models the equal vision he teaches:

"I envy no one, nor am I partial to anyone. I am equal to all. But whoever renders service unto Me in devotion is a friend, is in Me, and I am also a friend to him."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 9.29

God himself is equal to all. Not partial. Not biased. Not impressed by caste, skin color, gender, or title. The only distinction Krishna makes is devotion, and that is available to every being equally.

This does not tell Aisha to accept the discrimination. It tells her to recognize its source: ignorance. And it tells her that her worth is not determined by whether the VP acknowledges it. Her worth is inherent, eternal, recognized by the very source of all existence.

Living the Teaching

So what does Aisha do?

Smile and bear it? Internalize the oppression? Pretend it does not hurt?

No. The Gita does not teach passivity. It teaches steadiness of mind. Acting from inner dignity rather than reactive pain.

Practice Box

The Equal Vision Practice

1. Name the injustice clearly. Do not gaslight yourself. If you are being discriminated against, name it. To yourself. To trusted others. To those with power when it is safe. The person who sees with equal vision names inequality. They do not pretend it is not there.

2. Recognize their blindness, not your deficiency. Their inability to see you is their spiritual poverty, not your failing. They have not conquered the conditions of birth and death. They are still trapped in the illusion that external markers determine worth. That is their problem.

3. Act from dignity, not reaction. Respond strategically, not reflexively. Sometimes that means speaking up. Sometimes it means documenting. Sometimes it means leaving. But choose from strength, not wound. The person established in equanimity acts from a conquered mind.

4. Remember: God is equal to all. Your worth is not determined by the VP, the company, or the culture. It is inherent. Recognized by the source of reality itself. Stand in that knowledge.

The Way Forward

After the meeting, Aisha walks back to her office. Tom follows.

"That was unacceptable," he says. "I tried to redirect--"

"I know. Thank you."

"Are you going to say something? To HR? To the VP?"

Aisha sits down. Breathes. Feels the familiar calculation: Is it worth it? Will it change anything? Or will I just be labeled as difficult?

Then she realizes she has been asking the wrong question.

Not: "Will this change them?"

But: "Can I live with my silence?"

She thinks about equal vision. Not hers. Theirs. Their inability to see her. Their spiritual poverty. Their failure to recognize what Krishna says is obvious: that every being is equal.

She does not need them to see her in order to know her own worth. But she does need to name the injustice. Because the person established in equanimity does not pretend inequality is acceptable. They name it from a place of unshakeable ground.

She picks up her phone. Sends an email to the VP, copying HR:

During today's meeting, you repeatedly directed questions about my project to Tom instead of to me, despite my role as lead designer. This is not the first time this has happened. I am requesting a conversation about how to ensure project leads are recognized and included in discussions about their own work.

Professional. Clear. Documented.

Her hand shakes slightly as she hits send.

Tom watches. "You think he'll respond?"

"Probably not the way I want," Aisha says. "But I'm not doing this for him. I'm doing it for me."

The response comes two hours later. Defensive. "I didn't mean to overlook you. Just trying to keep the meeting moving."

No apology. No acknowledgment.

Aisha expected this. But she feels lighter anyway.

Because she named it. Stood in her truth. Refused to gaslight herself to make him comfortable.

The discrimination will not stop. Not today. Maybe not ever in this company.

But something shifted: She stopped eroding.

That night, she updates her resume. Starts reaching out to her network. Maybe she stays. Maybe she leaves. But either way, she will do it from dignity.

Not because anyone gave her permission.

But because her worth, her eternal, unshakeable worth, was never theirs to grant in the first place. God is equal to all. And so is she.

Reflection

- Where are you experiencing discrimination, overt or subtle? Have you been gaslighting yourself to avoid seeing it clearly?
- Can you distinguish between their blindness and your worth? What would change if you stopped performing for the approval of people who cannot see you?
- What strategic action becomes possible when you act from the knowledge that your worth is inherent, equal, and recognized by the source of all existence?

---

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 34: Discovering Meaning

Opening Story

Nina wakes up at 6:00 AM, like she has for the past fifteen years.

Shower. Coffee. Commute. Desk by 8:00. Emails. Meetings. Lunch at the desk. More meetings. Emails. Commute. Dinner. TV. Sleep.

Repeat.

She is thirty-nine. Successful by most measures. Senior manager at a consulting firm. Good salary. Nice apartment. Stable.

But lately, and she cannot pinpoint when it started, she has been waking up with a question that will not leave her alone:

What is the point?

Not in a depressed way. Not suicidal. Just genuinely confused about what all of this is for.

She is checking boxes. Meeting goals. Climbing the ladder. But to what end?

Her friends tell her she is having a midlife crisis. "Get a hobby," they say. "Travel more. Date someone new."

But that is not it. She has hobbies. She travels. She has dated.

The question is not about filling time. It is deeper.

Why am I here? What am I supposed to be doing with this one life I have?

She tries to ignore it. Throws herself into work. Stays busy. Distracts herself.

But the question persists. Growing louder. More insistent.

And Nina realizes: She has lived almost forty years without ever asking what her life is actually for.

She has been operating on autopilot. Doing what she is supposed to do. Following the script society handed her.

But she never asked if it was her script.

Reflective Bridge

We are taught to pursue success. Achievement. Comfort. Security.

Get the degree. Land the job. Build the career. Make the money. Acquire the things.

And somewhere along the way, we will find... what? Happiness? Purpose? Meaning?

But it does not work that way.

Because meaning is not something you stumble into. It is not a reward for checking enough boxes.

Meaning is discovered. Chosen. Lived into.

And most of us, like Nina, spend decades avoiding the question entirely. Because asking it means admitting we might have been living for the wrong things.

The wrong goals. The wrong values. The wrong definition of success.

That is terrifying.

So we stay busy. We pursue the next thing. We tell ourselves: Once I get the promotion, the relationship, the house, then I will feel fulfilled.

But we never do. Because external achievements cannot answer an internal question.

Viktor Frankl, who survived the Nazi concentration camps, said it plainly: "He who has a why to live can bear almost any how."

Nina has figured out the "how." She is good at it. Efficient. Successful.

But she has lost the "why." If she ever had one.

And without it, everything feels hollow.

The Gita speaks directly to this crisis.

The Gita Speaks: Beyond the Bewildered Life

Krishna identifies the root of Nina's emptiness with surgical precision. There are people, he says, who go through the motions of life without ever grasping its deeper purpose:

"Those who are thus bewildered are attracted by atheistic and demonic views. In that deluded condition, their hopes for liberation, their fruitive activities, and their cultivation of knowledge are all frustrated."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 9.12

This is not an insult. It is a diagnosis. Nina is not evil. She is bewildered. She has pursued fruitive activities, career success, financial security, social status, and all of it has been frustrated. Not because she failed at them, but because they cannot deliver what she is actually looking for. When the deeper dimension of life is missing, even our knowledge and our achievements ring hollow.

But there is a way out. And it begins with equanimity:

"Those whose minds are established in sameness and equanimity have already conquered the conditions of birth and death. They are flawless like Brahman, and thus they are already situated in Brahman."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 5.19

Meaning is not found in the next promotion or the next relationship. It is found when the mind stops chasing and becomes established in equanimity. The person who conquers the restless pursuit of "more" has already conquered the conditions of birth and death. They have found what Nina is looking for: a ground beneath the machinery of life.

And there is something even more radical available:

"When the embodied being can transcend the three modes associated with the material body, they can be liberated from birth, death, old age, and their afflictions, and can enjoy nectar even in this life."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 14.20

Nectar even in this life. Not after death. Not in some distant future. Now. The Gita promises that meaning, real meaning, is accessible to anyone willing to transcend the modes of material nature: the constant push and pull of ambition, lethargy, and restlessness that keep us on the treadmill Nina knows so well.

Krishna then reveals the weight of this teaching:

"This is the most confidential part of the Vedic scriptures, O immaculate one, and now I have revealed it. Whoever understands this will become wise, and their efforts will attain perfection."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 15.20

Whoever understands this will become wise, and their efforts will attain perfection. Not their career. Not their bank account. Their efforts. Everything they do becomes meaningful when they understand the confidential truth of their own existence.

And lest the seeker despair, Krishna offers reassurance:

"The transcendental qualities lead to liberation, while demonic qualities lead to captivity. Do not worry, O son of Pandu, for you are born with divine qualities."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 16.5

Do not worry. The very fact that Nina is asking the question, that she refuses to accept the hollow script, that she hungers for something real, is evidence of her divine nature. The demonic quality is contentment with captivity. Nina's restlessness is not a crisis. It is a calling.

Living the Teaching

So what does Nina do?

Quit her job? Abandon everything? Move to an ashram?

No. The Gita does not teach escape. It teaches clarity.

Practice Box

The Meaning Discovery Practice

1. Recognize your bewilderment without shame. You are not broken. You are waking up. The frustration you feel is the sound of a life that has outgrown its script. Name it honestly: "I have been living without asking why."

2. Stop chasing fruitive results. The next promotion will not answer the question. The next relationship will not fill the void. Meaning does not come from results. It comes from understanding your own nature.

3. Cultivate equanimity. Begin establishing your mind in sameness. Not indifference, but the steady ground beneath the ups and downs. Meditate. Reflect. Stop reacting to every fluctuation of success and failure.

4. Trust your divine nature. The fact that you are asking these questions means you are born with transcendental qualities. Do not worry. Your restlessness is not a problem. It is the beginning of wisdom.

The Way Forward

Nina starts journaling. Not because it is profound. Because it is simple.

Every morning, before the autopilot kicks in, she writes:

What actually matters to me? What do I value when I strip away everyone else's expectations?

At first, the answers are vague. Surface-level. "I want to be happy. I want to help people."

But she keeps writing. Digging deeper.

And slowly, something emerges:

She values connection. Real connection. Not networking. Not transactional relationships. Deep, honest human presence.

And she realizes: She has not felt that in years. Not at work. Not in her social life. Nowhere.

She has been performing connection. Attending the right events. Saying the right things. But not actually connecting.

One Saturday, she volunteers at a community center. Teaching resume writing to people re-entering the workforce after incarceration.

It is not glamorous. It does not pay. It will not help her career.

But sitting across from Jerome, fifty-two, fresh out of prison, terrified of job interviews, something shifts.

"I don't know how to talk about the gap," Jerome says. "Fifteen years. How do I explain that?"

"You tell the truth," Nina says. "You made mistakes. You paid for them. Now you want to contribute. Anyone worth working for will respect that."

Jerome looks at her. Really looks. "You think so?"

"I know so."

And in that moment, Nina feels something she has not felt in years:

Purpose.

Not because she solved Jerome's problem. But because she showed up. Fully. As herself. With presence and honesty.

She remembers the verse: Whoever understands this will become wise, and their efforts will attain perfection. Her effort in that room, with that man, felt perfect. Not because of the result, but because of the alignment.

She does not quit her job. Not yet. But she starts shifting. Spending more time at the community center. Less time chasing promotions she does not want. The bewilderment that had frustrated her hopes begins to lift.

Three months later, Jerome gets a job. Texts her: You were right. They respected the truth. Thank you.

Nina reads it sitting at her desk. Surrounded by emails and meetings and the machinery of a life she built but never chose.

And she knows: This is just the beginning.

Because meaning is not found in the machinery. It is found when the mind stops chasing and starts seeing. When the restlessness that felt like crisis reveals itself as calling.

Nina was born with divine qualities. She just needed to stop being bewildered long enough to find them.

Reflection

- Are you bewildered, pursuing fruitive activities that leave you empty despite your success? Can you name that honestly?
- What would it mean to establish your mind in equanimity rather than chasing the next achievement? What ground exists beneath the machinery of your life?
- Do you trust that your restlessness is a sign of divine nature rather than a problem to solve? What would change if you stopped worrying and started listening to the question?

---

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 35: Decisions

Opening Story

Liam has two job offers sitting on his desk.

Offer A: Senior architect at a prestigious firm in New York. Double his current salary. Corner office. Status. Everything he has worked toward for ten years.

Offer B: Lead designer at a nonprofit in his hometown. Designing affordable housing. Modest salary. Small team. Meaningful work.

He has forty-eight hours to decide.

And he is paralyzed.

His girlfriend says New York is the obvious choice. "You'd be insane to turn down that money."

His mother says hometown. "You've been gone too long. Come home. Do something that matters."

His mentor says follow the prestige. "You can always do good work later. Build your reputation first."

Everyone has an opinion. And they all sound right.

Liam makes a pro-and-con list. Updates it daily. It does not help.

Because this is not about pros and cons. It is about who he wants to become. What kind of life he wants to live. What he will regret more when he is seventy.

And he has no idea.

The paralysis is not from lack of information. It is from too much. Too many voices. Too many considerations. Too many possible futures collapsing into this single choice.

So he does what most people do when facing a big decision: He freezes. Waits. Hopes the answer will become obvious.

But time does not clarify. It just runs out.

Reflective Bridge

Big decisions terrify us because they feel final. Irreversible. Like choosing one path means forever abandoning all the others.

So we delay. Gather more information. Consult more people. Make more lists.

But here is the truth: The perfect decision does not exist.

Because you cannot know the future. Cannot predict which choice will lead to happiness, success, or fulfillment. Cannot control the ten thousand variables that will shape the outcome.

All you can do is choose. And then make your choice right by how you live it.

Liam is waiting for certainty. For a guarantee that whichever job he picks will be the "right" one.

But certainty does not exist. Not before the choice. Only after, when you commit to making it work.

The Gita addresses this directly. Not as self-help advice, but as spiritual law.

The Gita Speaks: Get Up and Fight

Arjuna faces a decision that makes Liam's look trivial.

Fight his own family in a war that will kill thousands? Or walk away, abdicate his duty, and let injustice prevail?

Both options feel impossible. Both come with enormous consequences. Both will haunt him.

And he freezes. Exactly like Liam.

"I don't know what is better," he tells Krishna, "whether we should conquer them or they should conquer us."

Translation: I am paralyzed. Tell me what to do.

But Krishna does not hand him a pro-and-con list. He does not weigh options or calculate outcomes. He cuts through the paralysis with a single command:

"O son of Kunti, either you are killed on the battlefield and go to the heavenly planets, or you triumph and enjoy the earthly kingdom. So get up with determination, and fight."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 2.37

Read that carefully.

Krishna does not tell Arjuna which outcome will happen. He does not promise victory. He does not guarantee survival. He says: Either you die and attain heaven, or you win and enjoy the kingdom. Either way, the result is favorable.

So get up. With determination. And fight.

This is the antidote to decision paralysis. Not more analysis. Not more opinions. Not a guarantee of the right outcome. Just the recognition that both paths lead somewhere worth going, and the only losing move is to sit frozen on the ground refusing to choose.

Liam is Arjuna on that battlefield. Sitting between two futures, paralyzed by the fear of choosing wrong.

But Krishna's message is clear: There is no "wrong" choice between two genuine paths. There is only the refusal to choose, which guarantees you go nowhere.

Get up with determination, and fight.

Not "get up once you are certain." Not "get up when the analysis is complete." Not "get up when everyone agrees."

Get up with determination. Now.

Living the Teaching

So what does Liam do?

Flip a coin? Take the money? Do what his girlfriend says?

No. He needs to stop waiting for certainty and start acting with determination.

Practice Box

The Determination Decision Practice

1. Accept that both paths lead somewhere worth going. If you are choosing between two genuine options, neither is a disaster. Stop looking for the "right" answer. There is no wrong battlefield.

2. Silence the external voices. Stop asking everyone's opinion. Their advice reflects their values, not yours. You need to hear your own voice first. Krishna spoke to Arjuna alone.

3. Get up with determination. Choose. Not with certainty. With resolve. The quality of your decision is determined not by which option you pick, but by the determination you bring to it after choosing.

4. Fight. Once you choose, commit fully. No hedging. No looking back. No "what if." You are on the battlefield now. Act.

The Way Forward

Liam stops making lists.

Stops asking people what they think.

Instead, he sits quietly. Alone. With Krishna's words echoing: Either you are killed on the battlefield and go to the heavenly planets, or you triumph and enjoy the earthly kingdom.

Either way, the result is favorable.

New York: He builds an impressive career, earns well, gains status. That is a life. A real one.

Hometown: He designs homes for people who have never had a safe place to live. That is a life. A real one.

Neither is a disaster. Neither is a mistake. Both lead somewhere worth going.

The question is not which is right. The question is: Which will he get up for with determination?

And the answer comes. Not as logic, but as feeling:

He would get up at five in the morning with determination to design affordable housing. He would not get up at five in the morning with determination for a corner office.

He calls his girlfriend that night.

"I'm taking the nonprofit job."

Silence. Then: "Are you serious? You're choosing poverty over success?"

"I'm choosing the battlefield where I'll actually fight. Not the one that looks impressive from the stands."

She does not understand. They break up two weeks later.

It hurts. But Liam knows: If she could not support the fight he chose, the relationship was already over.

He moves home. Takes the nonprofit job. Designs affordable housing for people who have never had a safe place to live.

The first year is hard. The pay is tight. The work is frustrating. He questions his choice a dozen times.

But he does not regret it.

Because he got up with determination. He did not sit frozen between two futures, waiting for a certainty that would never come. He chose a battlefield and he fights on it. Every day.

Five years later, his work wins a national award for community impact. The prestigious New York firm reaches out: "We saw your project. Want to join us and do this at scale?"

Liam considers it. But this time, he is not paralyzed.

Because he knows his battlefield now. And Krishna's teaching holds: Either outcome is favorable. The only failure is refusing to fight.

Get up with determination. That is the whole teaching on decisions.

Everything else is commentary.

Reflection

- What decision are you avoiding? Are you waiting for certainty, or are you simply afraid to choose?
- Can you see that both paths lead somewhere worth going? What changes when you stop looking for the "right" answer and start looking for the fight you are willing to commit to?
- What would it mean to get up with determination today? Not with confidence. Not with certainty. Just with resolve?

---

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 36: Responsibility

Opening Story

Sarah's phone rings at 2:47 AM.

She knows before she answers. You always know.

Her mother's voice is calm. Too calm. "It's your father. He had a stroke. They don't think--" The sentence breaks.

Sarah is on a plane by dawn.

Three days later, her father is stable but changed. The right side of his body does not work. The doctor uses words like "significant impairment" and "long-term care needs."

Sarah's younger brother lives in Seattle. Her sister is in the middle of a messy divorce. Both of them are sympathetic. Both of them are unavailable.

Which leaves Sarah.

She lives two hours away. She has a career she has spent fifteen years building. A partner who is already stretched thin with his own aging parents. Two kids in middle school who need her.

But her mother cannot do this alone.

The weight settles on her shoulders like physical mass.

This is my responsibility, she thinks. Mine.

But the word feels like a sentence, not a choice.

Reflective Bridge

We know this weight.

Maybe not Sarah's exact situation, but we know the feeling. The phone call. The moment when someone else's need becomes your obligation.

And here is what makes it so hard: it is your responsibility. The need is real. Your capacity is real. The connection, family, friendship, role, position, is real.

But so is the cost.

Responsibility promises meaning. It says, "You matter. You're needed. You're the one who can handle this."

And that is true. But it is not the whole truth.

Because responsibility also drains. It limits. It crowds out other responsibilities, to yourself, to your own family, to your work, to your health.

And when you cannot say no, when the burden is truly yours and no one else will carry it, responsibility stops feeling like purpose and starts feeling like imprisonment.

Sarah sits in the hospital cafeteria, her coffee cold, her phone full of messages she has not answered. Work needs decisions. Her kids need attention. Her partner needs her to talk about what comes next.

But all she can think is: How am I going to do all of this?

The exhaustion is not just physical. It is existential.

The Gita does not promise that responsibility will be light. It promises something better: clarity about what is yours and the freedom that comes from doing it without attachment.

The Gita Speaks: Your Duty, Your Ground

Krishna speaks to Arjuna as a warrior paralyzed by the weight of what is being asked of him. And he begins not with comfort, but with clarity:

"Considering your specific duty as a kshatriya, you should know that there is no better occupation for you than to fight based on religious principles; so, you have no reason to hesitate."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 2.31

Considering your specific duty. Not someone else's duty. Not an abstract ideal. Your duty. Based on who you are, your nature, your role, your position in the web of relationships and obligations.

Sarah is a daughter. That is not a title she chose. It is a fact of her existence. And the duty that flows from it is specific: not to fix everything, not to sacrifice herself entirely, but to fulfill the responsibility that belongs to her by nature.

Krishna is equally clear about how to carry that duty:

"You have a right to perform your prescribed duties, but you are not entitled to the fruits of your actions. Never consider yourself to be the cause of the results of your activities, nor be attached to inaction."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 2.47

This verse sits at the heart of the entire Gita. And it speaks directly to Sarah's crisis.

You have a duty. Sarah's responsibility to her father is real. It is based on relationship, capacity, and natural affection. This is her dharma.

But you are not entitled to the results. She cannot control whether her father recovers. Whether her mother copes well. Whether her siblings step up. Whether she does everything "right."

The responsibility is to act. Not to succeed.

And do not be attached to inaction. Avoiding responsibility because it is hard is not freedom. It is abandonment. Of others, yes. But also of yourself.

Then Krishna adds a warning about what happens when we abandon our duties entirely:

"My dear Arjuna, one who in human life does not follow the established cycle of sacrifices as prescribed by the Vedas certainly leads a life filled with sin. Such a person lives in vain, for they only live for the gratification of the senses."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 3.16

This is not a threat. It is a description. The person who abandons their duty to pursue only their own comfort lives in vain. Their life becomes hollow, no matter how pleasant it looks from the outside.

Sarah's brother in Seattle. Her sister absorbed in her own crisis. They may have reasons. But if those reasons are simply comfort and convenience, then Krishna's words apply: a life lived only for the gratification of the senses is a life lived in vain.

And Sarah knows: She will not live that way. She cannot. Not because she is a martyr, but because she understands that fulfilling her duty, even when it costs her, is the only path that does not hollow her out.

Living the Teaching

Sarah starts making decisions. Not from guilt. Not from "should." From clarity about what is actually hers to carry.

Practice Box

The Conscious Responsibility Practice

1. Identify your specific duty. Ask: "What is my prescribed duty here? Not what would a perfect person do, but what does my specific nature, role, and capacity require?" Krishna told Arjuna to consider his specific duty. Consider yours.

2. Do it fully, without attachment to results. Once you have identified your actual responsibility, commit completely. No resentment. No martyrdom. But also no obsession with outcomes. Do your part. Release the rest.

3. Do not be attached to inaction. Avoiding responsibility because it is hard is not self-care. It is abandonment. If the duty is yours, do it. The person who lives only for their own comfort lives in vain.

4. Set boundaries on what is not yours. Your siblings' duty is their duty. Your company's expectations are not your dharma. Say no to others' responsibilities with clarity, not cruelty: "That is not mine to carry."

Sarah's actual responsibility: She can visit her father regularly. She can coordinate with doctors. She can help her mother find good care options. She can be emotionally present.

What is not hers: Making her siblings show up. Fixing her father's health. Solving her mother's grief. Sacrificing her own family's well-being completely.

She calls her brother. No guilt trip. Just clarity: "I can visit twice a week and handle medical coordination. But I need you to cover weekends remotely. FaceTime with Dad, check in with Mom. Can you do that?"

He can. He does.

She talks to her boss. Asks for flexible hours for the next three months. Gets it.

She tells her kids the truth: "Grandpa needs more help now. So I'm going to be busier. But you're not losing me. We're adjusting together."

And she tells her partner: "I need you to hold more at home right now. And I need you to tell me when I'm overdoing it."

Responsibility does not mean doing everything. It means doing your part, clearly, fully, consciously. And trusting that if you do what is yours, the rest will work itself out. Or it will not. But either way, you will have done what you could without destroying yourself.

The Way Forward

Three months later, Sarah's father is in a good facility. Her mother visits daily. Sarah visits twice a week. Her brother comes once a month and calls every Sunday.

It is not perfect. Her father will never fully recover. Her mother is lonely. Sarah is tired.

But she is not drowning.

Because she learned the difference between responsibility and self-destruction. Between fulfilling her specific duty and trying to carry everyone else's.

One day, her daughter asks, "Mom, are you okay? You seem tired but not stressed."

Sarah thinks about it.

"I'm doing what's mine to do," she says. "And I'm letting go of what isn't."

Her daughter nods. "That's really mature."

Sarah laughs. "It's really survival."

But it is also something deeper.

It is the Gita's teaching in action: Consider your specific duty. Perform it without attachment to results. Do not retreat into inaction. And do not live in vain by pursuing only your own comfort.

Responsibility is not a burden when you stop trying to control everything. It is just what is yours to do. Today. This moment. This situation.

Do it well. Then let it go.

Reflection

- What is your specific duty right now? Not the abstract ideal, but the concrete responsibility that belongs to you by nature, role, and capacity?
- Are you carrying responsibilities that are not yours? Whose duties have you absorbed because they refused to carry them?
- What would change if you performed your duty fully but released attachment to the results? What boundaries would you need to set to sustain yourself while fulfilling what is genuinely yours?

---

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 37: Expectations

Opening Story

Trevor has it all mapped out.

The proposal will happen at sunset. The restaurant on the pier--her favorite place. He's arranged for a violinist. The ring is perfect. She's going to cry. She's going to say yes.

He's been planning this for three months.

And when the moment comes, she does cry.

But she doesn't say yes.

She says, "I need time."

Trevor stands there, ring box open, violinist still playing, sunset painting the sky exactly as planned.

Everything was perfect.

Except the outcome.

Two weeks later, they're having coffee. She's apologetic. He's numb.

"I just... I didn't expect to feel so panicked," she says. "I love you. But marriage? I'm not ready. I don't know if I'll ever be ready."

Trevor hears the words. But what he's thinking is: This wasn't how it was supposed to go.

He did everything right. He planned. He waited. He chose the perfect moment.

And it still didn't work.

Reflective Bridge

We all have expectations. About how things should go. About what we deserve. About what happens when we do everything right.

And when reality doesn't match the script, we feel betrayed.

Trevor's expectation was reasonable. People who love each other get married. He proposed beautifully. She should have said yes.

But "should" doesn't create reality.

Expectations promise control. They say, "If you do X, Y will follow. If you plan well enough, work hard enough, love well enough, life will cooperate."

But life doesn't cooperate. People change their minds. Circumstances shift. What you thought was certain becomes uncertain.

And when the gap between expectation and reality opens up, we fall into it.

Trevor can't eat. Can't sleep. Every plan he made--the wedding venue he'd researched, the honeymoon he'd imagined, the life they'd talked about--all of it evaporates. What's left is just the ache of "supposed to."

This is what expectations do when we cling to them. They turn every deviation into a disaster. Every surprise into a betrayal. Every uncertain outcome into evidence that the universe is broken.

Or worse--that we are.

And when the mind spirals, tangled in anxieties of its own making, the result is predictable. We grip tighter. We demand more. We bind ourselves to outcomes that were never ours to control.

The Gita Speaks: Action Without Entitlement

Krishna's central teaching to Arjuna cuts to the heart of this struggle--the gap between action and outcome.

  "You have a right to perform your prescribed duties, but you are not entitled to the fruits of your actions. Never consider yourself to be the cause of the results of your activities, nor be attached to inaction."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 2.47

Read that again.

You have a right to perform your duties. Trevor had every right to propose. To plan. To love her well and ask for a future together.

But you're not entitled to the fruits. He wasn't entitled to a yes. To certainty. To his plan becoming reality.

Don't be attached to inaction. And he also can't just stop acting--stop risking, stop trying, stop living--because outcomes are uncertain.

This is the razor's edge the Gita asks us to walk: act fully, without controlling results.

But why does the mind resist this? Because the ego believes it is the author of every result. Krishna explains:

  "Therefore, fight, O Arjuna, surrendering to Me all your works, with full knowledge of Me, without desire for gain, without sense of possession and free from lethargy."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 3.30

Surrender all your works. Not surrender your effort--surrender your claim on the results. Trevor acted with love. That love was real. But the moment he insisted the outcome match his blueprint, he stopped offering love and started demanding payment.

Why must we continue to act, even without guarantees? Krishna reveals the importance of steady action through his own example:

  "For if I were to fail to carefully perform the prescribed duties, O Partha, no doubt all men would follow my path."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 3.23

Even the Supreme acts without attachment to results. The divine continues to perform duties not for personal gain, but to set an example. Trevor must continue to act--to love, to risk, to show up--not because he's entitled to specific outcomes, but because that's what it means to live with integrity.

Krishna also reveals what life looks like when someone has truly internalized this:

  "He is satisfied with gains that come by themselves, he has overcome duality, he is free from all envy, and he is stable in both success and failure. Therefore, he never gets entangled even though he executes actions."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 4.22

Satisfied with gains that come by themselves. Stable in both success and failure. Never entangled even while acting.

This isn't passivity. It's freedom. The person described here doesn't stop working, doesn't stop loving, doesn't stop proposing at sunset. They simply stop demanding that reality obey their script.

And what happens to those who cannot release their grip?

  "Thus perplexed by various anxieties and bound by a web of illusions, they become too attached to sense enjoyment and fall into hell."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 16.16

A web of illusions. That's what Trevor wove for himself--an elaborate future that existed only in his mind. When reality refused to conform, the web didn't catch him. It trapped him.

The anxiety wasn't caused by her answer. It was caused by his attachment to a different answer.

Living the Teaching

Trevor sits with the wreckage of his expectations for weeks.

Then, slowly, he starts asking different questions.

Not "Why didn't she say yes?" but "What's actually true right now?"

Practice Box

1. Name the expectation. Get honest. What did you expect? What were you attached to? Write it down clearly: "I expected she would say yes."

2. Separate action from outcome. Ask: "Did I act with integrity? Did I do what was mine to do?" If yes, the action was complete. The outcome was never yours to control.

3. Accept what is. Reality is what it is, not what you expected. Can you meet it without resentment? Without making it wrong?

4. Act again without guarantees. Life continues. The next action won't come with certainty either. Can you move forward anyway--surrendering the results, free from lethargy?

Trevor realizes: he acted with love. He was honest. He offered himself fully.

Her response? That was hers. Not his to control. Not his to predict.

He calls her. Not to convince. Just to talk.

"I'm hurt," he says. "But I understand you need time. And I don't want to lose you while you figure it out."

She's quiet. Then: "What does that look like?"

"I don't know," Trevor admits. "But I'd rather find out than walk away because you didn't follow my script."

It's terrifying. No guarantees. No timeline. No certainty.

But it's also real.

And real, he's learning, is better than perfect.

The Way Forward

Six months later, Trevor and his girlfriend are still together.

They're not engaged. Maybe they will be someday. Maybe they won't.

Trevor has stopped trying to control the future. Not because he doesn't care. Because he's learned that attachment to outcomes is just fear wearing a planning hat.

One evening, she asks, "Do you still want to marry me?"

Trevor thinks about it. "I want to be with you. However that looks. If that's marriage someday, great. If it's something else, I'm open."

She smiles. "That's growth."

He laughs. "That's survival."

But it's also freedom. Because when you release expectations, you're not giving up on life. You're giving up on the exhausting attempt to force reality to match your blueprint.

You act. Fully. Honestly. With integrity.

And then you let go.

Satisfied with gains that come by themselves. Stable in both success and failure. Never entangled--even while fully engaged.

That's not resignation. It's liberation.

Reflection

- What expectation are you clinging to that's causing you suffering? What outcome are you demanding from life, from others, from yourself?
- Can you separate your actions--which are yours--from the results--which aren't? Did you act with integrity? If yes, can you release the outcome?
- What web of illusions have you woven from your anxieties? What freedom might be waiting on the other side of your attachment?

---

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 38: Conduct

Opening Story

Maya finds the email by accident.

She's looking for a client file on her colleague Ben's computer--he asked her to grab it while he's out--and there it is. An email to their boss.

Subject line: "Concerns about the Rodriguez project."

She shouldn't read it. She knows she shouldn't.

But she does.

Ben has outlined every mistake Maya made on the project. Every missed deadline. Every communication breakdown. All framed as "concerns" but written with surgical precision to make her look incompetent.

The project launched successfully. The client is happy. But that's not what the email says.

Maya's hands shake.

Ben is her friend. They've worked together for three years. They've had drinks after work. He came to her wedding.

And he's been quietly undermining her for weeks.

When he gets back, she doesn't say anything. She grabs the file. Smiles. Walks away.

But inside, she's already drafting her response. She could forward the email to HR. She could confront him publicly in the next team meeting. She could write her own email to the boss, correcting every lie.

She could destroy him the way he tried to destroy her.

The question isn't whether she can. It's whether she should.

Reflective Bridge

Conduct is easy when it doesn't cost you anything.

Being honest when no one's lying. Being fair when there's nothing to lose. Being kind when kindness is returned.

But real conduct--integrity--only shows up when you're tempted to abandon it.

Maya has every reason to strike back. Ben betrayed her trust. He lied about her work. He tried to damage her reputation.

She'd be justified in defending herself. In exposing him. In making sure he pays for what he did.

But justified doesn't mean right.

This is where conduct gets tested: when you've been wronged and you have the power to wrong back.

Maya's confusion mirrors a more ancient one. She doesn't know what to do--not because she lacks options, but because every option feels wrong. Retaliate, and she becomes what she despises. Stay silent, and injustice stands.

She's paralyzed. Not by weakness. By the weight of choosing who to be.

The Gita Speaks: Clarity Through Principled Action

Arjuna faces a similar paralysis on the battlefield. Overwhelmed by the moral complexity of what lies before him, he admits:

  "Now I am confused about my duty and have lost all composure because of weakness."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 2.7

This is the honest starting point. Not bravado. Not certainty. Confusion. The willingness to say: I don't know what's right here.

But Krishna doesn't leave Arjuna in confusion. He gives him a framework for action that transcends the emotional chaos:

  "Fight to fight, regardless of happiness or sorrow, loss or gain, victory or defeat, and by doing this you will never incur sin."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 2.38

Regardless of happiness or sorrow. The instruction isn't "act when it feels good" or "act when you'll win." It's act from principle--and let the emotional weather be what it is.

Maya doesn't need to feel good about confronting Ben. She doesn't need to win. She needs to act with integrity, regardless of the outcome.

And that kind of action requires a singular focus:

  "Those who are on this path are very determined, and their goal is one."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 2.41

One goal. Not revenge. Not vindication. Not even fairness. The single aim of right conduct--action aligned with principle rather than reaction.

Krishna later describes what this looks like in a person's character:

  "One who is not envious but who is a kind friend to all living entities, who does not think himself a proprietor, who is free from false ego and equal both in happiness and distress, who is always satisfied and engaged in devotional service with determination and whose mind and intelligence are in agreement with Me--he is very dear to Me."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 12.13-14

A kind friend to all living entities. Even Ben. Not because Ben deserves kindness. Because Maya's conduct isn't determined by what others deserve. It's determined by who she is.

Free from false ego. The ego screams, "He disrespected you. Make him pay." But the person described here doesn't need to dominate. Doesn't need to win. They act from wholeness, not from wound.

Living the Teaching

Maya doesn't send the retaliatory email.

Not because she's passive. Because she's strategic about who she wants to be.

Practice Box

1. Name the confusion honestly. What are you tempted to do? What feels justified but violates your actual values? Admit the conflict clearly--like Arjuna admitting his confusion.

2. Act from principle, not reaction. Pause. Let the heat pass. Then choose: regardless of happiness or sorrow, loss or gain--what action aligns with your integrity?

3. Make your goal singular. Not revenge. Not vindication. Right conduct. One aim, clearly held.

4. Be a kind friend--even to adversaries. You can set boundaries, speak truth, and defend yourself without attacking, shaming, or retaliating. Address the situation, not the person.

Maya schedules a meeting with her boss. She doesn't mention Ben's email. She presents her own documentation: client feedback, project metrics, timeline records.

"I wanted to address some concerns that have apparently been raised about the Rodriguez project," she says calmly. "Here's the actual data."

Her boss reviews it. Nods. "This looks solid, Maya. Where are these concerns coming from?"

"I think there may have been some miscommunication," she says. "I wanted to make sure you had the complete picture."

She doesn't throw Ben under the bus. She doesn't need to. The data speaks.

Later, she talks to Ben directly. Not in anger. In clarity.

"I saw the email," she says. "I don't know why you felt the need to undermine me instead of coming to me directly. But I'm not interested in playing that game. If you have concerns about my work, talk to me. Otherwise, we keep this professional."

Ben stammers. Apologizes weakly. Offers excuses.

Maya doesn't engage. She's said what needed to be said.

Her integrity is intact.

The Way Forward

Six months later, Ben is transferred to a different team. Maya doesn't ask for details.

But her boss pulls her aside. "I want you to know--I see how you handle conflict. A lot of people would have made that situation ugly. You kept it professional."

Maya nods. "I try to focus on the work."

"It's more than that," her boss says. "It's character."

Maya doesn't tell her about the drafts she wrote. The angry emails she never sent. The revenge fantasies she entertained.

Because what matters isn't what you're tempted to do. It's what you actually do.

And what she actually did was find clarity through confusion--the same journey Arjuna made. At the end of the Gita, after receiving Krishna's full teaching, Arjuna declares:

  "Arjuna said: My dear Krishna, O You Infallible, now my illusion has dissipated. By Your mercy I have recovered my memory. Now I am firm and free from doubts, and I am willing to act in accordance with Your instructions."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 18.73

Illusion dissipated. Memory recovered. Firm and free from doubt.

Maya started confused. She ended clear. Not because the situation changed. Because she did.

Conduct isn't about being perfect. It's about being principled. Even when it costs you something. Even when no one's watching. Even when you've been wronged.

Because in the end, the person you have to live with is yourself. And you can't build a life of integrity on a foundation of compromised conduct--no matter how justified the compromise felt in the moment.

Reflection

- When have you been confused about duty--genuinely unsure of the right action? Did you act from principle or from reaction?
- What would it mean to act regardless of happiness or sorrow, loss or gain? Can you hold one singular aim when the temptation is to fracture into revenge, fear, or self-pity?
- What illusion needs to dissipate before you can act with clarity? What would it take to say, like Arjuna, "I am firm and free from doubts"?

---

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 39: Respect

Opening Story

Carlos has worked at the hospital for twenty-two years.

He's not a doctor. Not a nurse. He's in environmental services--the official term for custodian.

He mops floors. Empties trash. Cleans rooms after patients are discharged.

Most people don't see him. And when they do, they don't really look.

But Carlos sees everything. The frantic energy of the ER at 3 AM. The quiet grief of families saying goodbye. The exhausted nurses who nod thank you when he restocks the supply closet they forgot to check.

He knows this hospital like he knows his own home.

One afternoon, a new administrator starts her rounds. Young. Ivy League MBA. Here to "optimize operations."

She watches Carlos clean a room. Frowns. "This is inefficient. You're using too much time per room."

Carlos looks up. "Ma'am, this is ICU. These rooms need--"

"I've reviewed the data," she interrupts. "You're 20% slower than the benchmark. We need to bring that down."

"The benchmark doesn't account for--"

"I'm not interested in excuses. Just results. Can you improve, or do we need to find someone who can?"

Carlos doesn't say anything. He just nods. Finishes his work. Moves to the next room.

But something inside him hardens.

Reflective Bridge

Everyone wants to be seen. Really seen.

Not just acknowledged as a function--the person who cleans, who serves, who fixes, who delivers. But recognized as a human being with skill, with care, with value that transcends their role.

Respect isn't about status. It's about dignity.

Carlos doesn't need the administrator to praise him. He doesn't need her to understand the twenty-two years of institutional knowledge he carries. He just needs her to see him as more than a number on a spreadsheet.

But she didn't. And that dismissal cuts deeper than criticism. Because criticism implies you're seen well enough to be evaluated. Dismissal means you're not seen at all.

The administrator's behavior reveals something. Not just rudeness--a worldview. One where people exist as instruments of productivity. Where value is measured by speed, not by care. Where the person in front of you is invisible unless they serve your metrics.

The Gita has a name for this way of seeing. And a warning about where it leads.

The Gita Speaks: The Two Visions

There is an established order to things. A way that human life is meant to function--with reciprocity, with recognition, with sacrifice for the common good. Krishna warns:

  "My dear Arjuna, one who in human life does not follow the established cycle of sacrifices as prescribed by the Vedas certainly leads a life filled with sin."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 3.16

The cycle of sacrifice. Everyone contributes. Everyone is honored for their contribution. The doctor heals. The custodian maintains the space where healing happens. The administrator ensures the system functions. Break any link, and the whole chain suffers.

The administrator dismissed Carlos not because she was evil, but because she didn't see the chain. She saw only her link.

And yet, Krishna also offers this honest observation:

  "Even a man of knowledge acts according to his own nature, for everyone follows his nature. What can repression accomplish?"

  -- Bhagavad-gita 3.33

People act from their conditioning. The administrator was trained to see efficiency, not dignity. She followed her nature--the nature of someone taught that data matters more than people. Repressing that conditioning won't change it. Only awareness will.

But when that conditioning goes unchecked--when a person builds their entire worldview around self-importance and the dismissal of others--the Gita is blunt about what follows:

  "Following these conclusions, demonic people, who are lost and lack intelligence, engage in harmful and dreadful deeds aimed at destroying the world."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 16.9

  "Taking refuge in insatiable lust and absorbed in the vanity of pride and false prestige, demonic people, deceived in this way, are always devoted to dirty deeds, drawn by the temporal."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 16.10

This isn't about horns and pitchforks. It's about a mindset. The vanity of pride and false prestige. The person who believes their MBA makes them more valuable than someone with twenty-two years of hands-on wisdom. The person absorbed in metrics while the human being in front of them disappears.

And where does that mindset end?

  "The demoniac person thinks: 'So much wealth do I have today... I am the lord of everything, I am the enjoyer, I am perfect, powerful and happy... I am the richest man, surrounded by aristocratic relatives.' In this way, such persons are deluded by ignorance."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 16.13-15

"I am the lord of everything." This is the ultimate disrespect--not toward any one person, but toward reality itself. The belief that you are the center. That others exist to serve your vision. That your position grants you the right to dismiss.

Carlos has met this mindset before. Twenty-two years of administrators who come and go. But the hospital stays. He stays.

Living the Teaching

A week later, the administrator is back. She's shadowing Carlos again, clipboard in hand.

But this time, something's different. She watches him work. Really watches. And after a few rooms, she asks, "Why do you spend extra time in ICU rooms?"

Carlos pauses. Usually, this is where he gets told he's wrong. But she's actually asking.

"ICU patients are fragile," he says. "Extra cleaning protocols. And families--they're scared. If they see someone being careless, it adds to their stress. So I go slower. I'm thorough. They notice."

The administrator writes something down. "How long have you worked here?"

"Twenty-two years."

She looks up. "So you've seen administrators come and go."

Carlos allows himself a small smile. "A few."

She nods. "I'm sure you have. I'm sorry I dismissed your input last week. I was focused on the data and missed the context. That was my mistake."

Carlos blinks. He wasn't expecting that.

Practice Box

1. See the person, not the role. Before you interact with anyone--cashier, CEO, stranger--pause. This is a conscious being with thoughts, struggles, dignity. Not a function.

2. Honor the cycle of sacrifice. Everyone contributes. The custodian, the nurse, the administrator--each role sustains the whole. Dismissing any link weakens the chain.

3. Check your conditioning. When you're tempted to dismiss someone, ask: Am I acting from awareness or from the vanity of pride and false prestige? Be honest.

4. Listen like it matters. When someone speaks, listen fully. Not to evaluate. Not to optimize. To understand. Twenty-two years of knowledge doesn't show up on a spreadsheet.

Carlos starts meeting with the administrator weekly. She asks questions. He shares what he's learned. Together, they redesign some of the workflows--not to speed things up, but to reduce waste while maintaining quality.

The administrator credits him in her report. She makes sure his supervisor knows his contribution.

It doesn't erase the initial dismissal. But it shows that people can learn. Can change. Can choose to see what they initially missed.

The Way Forward

Months later, the administrator is still there. Carlos jokes that she might actually stick around.

One day, she asks him, "How do you do it? Stay patient with all the people who've talked down to you over the years?"

Carlos thinks about it.

"I decided a long time ago," he says, "that my dignity doesn't depend on whether people see it. It's there whether they acknowledge it or not."

"But doesn't it hurt?" she asks. "Being dismissed?"

"Of course it does," Carlos says. "But if I tie my respect to whether others give it, I lose twice. Once when they dismiss me. And again when I diminish myself."

The administrator nods slowly. "That's wisdom."

Carlos smiles. "That's survival."

But it's also truth.

The person who sees everyone--who honors the cycle of sacrifice, who refuses to be absorbed in the vanity of pride--that person walks through the world whole. Not because everyone sees them. But because they see everyone.

Including themselves.

Reflection

- Who do you dismiss without realizing it? The person who serves your coffee? The person who cleans your office? Where have you broken the cycle of sacrifice by refusing to see someone's contribution?
- When you feel dismissed, where does your dignity actually come from? External validation--or something deeper that no one can take from you?
- What conditioning shapes how you see others? Are you acting from awareness, or from the vanity of pride and false prestige that the Gita warns against?

---

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 40: Determination

Opening Story

Keisha sits in her car in the gym parking lot.

She's been here four times this week. Monday, Wednesday, Friday, and now Saturday morning.

She's walked in exactly zero times.

Each time, she gets as far as the door. Sees people through the glass--fit, confident, moving like they belong there. And then something in her chest tightens.

Not today.

She tells herself she'll go tomorrow. That she just needs one more day to prepare. To feel ready.

But tomorrow never comes. And ready never arrives.

It's been six months since she signed up. Six months of monthly payments for a membership she hasn't used. Six months of promising herself, "This week will be different."

But it's not.

And sitting here, engine running, Keisha realizes: it's not going to be. Not unless something changes.

Not the gym. Not her body. Not her circumstances.

Her.

She turns off the engine. Grabs her bag. Opens the door.

And this time, she walks in.

Reflective Bridge

We all know how to start.

The first day of the diet. The first page of the book. The first step toward the goal.

Starting is easy. It's fueled by motivation, by excitement, by the vision of who we'll be when we succeed.

But then reality hits. The diet gets boring. The book gets hard. The goal requires more effort than we imagined.

And motivation--that bright, shiny feeling that got us started--evaporates.

What's left is just the work. The repetition. The unglamorous, uncomfortable, daily choice to keep going when you'd rather quit.

This is where most people stop. Not because they can't continue. But because they expect it to feel good. They expect motivation to carry them.

Keisha walks into the gym. She has no idea what to do. She picks a treadmill in the back corner where fewer people can see her. She walks for twenty minutes. Nothing dramatic. No transformation. Just twenty minutes of movement.

The next day, her legs hurt. She doesn't want to go back.

But she does.

The mind says quit. The body says rest. Every distraction in her life offers a reasonable excuse.

And this is exactly where the difference between the resolute and the irresolute becomes visible. Not in the grand moments of commitment. In the quiet mornings when no one is watching and every part of you wants to stay in bed.

The Gita Speaks: One Aim, Unbroken

Krishna identifies the decisive distinction--not between the strong and the weak, but between the focused and the scattered:

  "Those who are on this path are resolute in purpose, and their aim is one. O beloved child of the Kurus, the intelligence of those who are irresolute is many-branched."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 2.41

Their aim is one.

Not two. Not five. Not "I'll try this and also that and maybe something else if this doesn't work out."

One.

The resolute person has decided. The decision is made. What follows is not a series of new decisions but the daily execution of one decision already settled.

Keisha decided to walk through that door. That was the decision. Everything after--the sore legs, the awkwardness, the days she doesn't feel like going--those aren't new decisions. They're just the weather that passes over the one decision she already made.

The irresolute intelligence, Krishna says, is many-branched. It splits. It entertains alternatives. "Maybe I should try yoga instead. Maybe I'm not a gym person. Maybe I should wait until January. Maybe I should--"

Every "maybe" is a branch growing away from the trunk.

And a tree that is all branches and no trunk falls over in the first wind.

This is what makes determination different from motivation. Motivation is a feeling--it comes and goes like weather. Determination is a structure--the trunk that holds regardless of what blows through.

Keisha doesn't feel motivated most mornings. But she goes. Because her aim is one. And one aim, held with resolve, is stronger than a thousand scattered intentions.

The many-branched mind says, "Let me reconsider." The resolute mind says, "Already decided. Moving on."

Living the Teaching

Three months later, Keisha still goes to the gym.

Not every day. Not perfectly. But consistently.

Some days she feels strong. Most days she doesn't.

But she goes anyway.

Practice Box

1. Decide once. Make the decision once: "I'm doing this." Then stop re-deciding every time it gets hard. The many-branched intelligence re-decides daily. The resolute intelligence decided once and acts.

2. Expect resistance, not ease. The path will be uncomfortable. That's not a sign you chose wrong. It's a sign you're on the path. Discomfort is the terrain, not the obstacle.

3. Make it smaller when you need to. If the full effort feels impossible, do less. But don't do nothing. Five minutes on the treadmill is still one aim, held. Zero minutes is a branch growing away from the trunk.

4. Celebrate the trunk, not the branches. The win isn't the transformation. The win is that you showed up. You held your one aim. That's the real victory--the resolute purpose sustained through resistance.

Keisha doesn't track her weight anymore. She tracks her attendance.

Did she show up? Yes. That's the win.

Some days she lifts weights. Some days she just walks. Some days she sits on the mat and stretches for ten minutes.

But she's there.

And slowly--so slowly she almost doesn't notice--things shift. Not her body first. Her mind.

She stops thinking of herself as someone who quits. She starts thinking of herself as someone who shows up.

That's the real transformation. Not the muscles. Not the endurance. The identity shift from "I try and fail" to "I have one aim, and I hold it."

The Way Forward

A year later, Keisha is still going to the gym.

She doesn't look like a fitness model. She's not competing in marathons. She's just consistent.

One day, a new member approaches her. Young woman, nervous, hovering near the door.

"Is it always this intimidating?" she asks.

Keisha laughs. "Yes. But you get used to it."

"How long have you been coming?"

"About a year."

The woman looks surprised. "You seem so confident."

Keisha thinks about the version of herself who sat in the parking lot for months. Who almost turned back a hundred times.

"I'm not confident," she says. "I'm just determined. There's a difference."

Confidence is a feeling. Determination is a choice.

Confidence comes and goes. Determination stays--if you decide it will.

The young woman nods. "So you just... keep showing up?"

"That's all it is," Keisha says. "You decide once. Then you stop re-deciding. Your aim is one. And you just go."

"Even when you don't want to?"

"Especially then."

Because that's when determination becomes real. Not when it's easy. When it's hard. When the many-branched intelligence offers you a hundred exits. And you take none of them.

Because your aim is one. And one is enough.

Reflection

- What have you been re-deciding every day instead of deciding once? Where is your intelligence many-branched when it needs to be singular?
- Can you identify the difference between motivation and determination in your own life? Where has motivation failed you--and where might resolve carry you further?
- What is your one aim right now? Not your five goals or ten aspirations. The one thing you've decided. Can you hold it--not perfectly, but consistently--regardless of the weather?

---

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 41: Demotivated

Opening Story

Adrian stares at the blank canvas.

It's been blank for three weeks.

He used to paint every day. Used to wake up early, coffee in hand, brushes already laid out. Used to lose hours in the flow of color and form.

But something's shifted.

The ideas are still there. He can see them in his mind--compositions, color palettes, the way light would hit certain shapes.

But when he picks up the brush, nothing happens.

It's not that he can't. It's that he doesn't want to.

And that terrifies him more than being unable.

Because inability you can work around. You can learn. You can practice. You can improve.

But when you don't care anymore? When the thing that used to bring you alive now feels like obligation?

That's different.

Adrian's girlfriend asks, "Why don't you just paint something? Anything?"

"I don't feel like it," he says.

"You never feel like it anymore," she says. "What happened?"

He doesn't know. That's the problem.

Nothing happened. No tragedy. No failure. No rejection.

Just emptiness. Where there used to be fire, there's just ash.

Reflective Bridge

Demotivation isn't the same as laziness.

Lazy people don't want to work. Demotivated people want to want to work--but can't find the spark.

It's the artist who can't paint. The writer who can't write. The runner who can't run. Not because they're incapable. Because they've lost the reason.

And here's what makes it so insidious: you can't logic your way out of it.

People tell you, "Just start. Just do five minutes. Just push through."

But when the core motivation is gone, pushing through feels like self-torture. You go through the motions. You produce something. But it's hollow. And you know it's hollow. Which makes it worse.

Adrian tries. He sets up his easel. Mixes paint. Puts brush to canvas. And produces something. Technically competent. Emotionally dead.

He stares at it. This isn't me. This is just paint on canvas.

The demotivation deepens. Because now it's not just "I don't want to paint." It's "Even when I paint, it doesn't matter."

This is the real crisis. Not the absence of action. The absence of meaning.

And every endeavor, even the ones that used to feel pure, now seems covered by some kind of fault. Some smoke that obscures whatever flame once burned there.

The Gita Speaks: Rise and Be the Instrument

Arjuna faces this exact crisis on the battlefield. He's a warrior--one of the greatest. He's trained his whole life for this moment. But when the moment comes, he doesn't want to fight. Not because he's afraid. Because he's lost the reason.

Krishna doesn't coddle him. He doesn't say, "Wait until you feel inspired." He says something far more direct:

  "Therefore get up and prepare to fight. After conquering your enemies you will enjoy a flourishing kingdom. They are already put to death by My arrangement, and you, O Savyasacin, can be but an instrument in the fight."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 11.33

Get up.

Not "get motivated." Not "find your passion." Not "wait until the feeling returns."

Get up.

And notice the reframe: you are an instrument. The outcome is already arranged. Your job isn't to generate the result. Your job is to participate in something larger than your feelings about it.

This is the antidote to demotivation. Not more motivation--a shift in identity. You're not the generator of meaning. You're the instrument through which meaning moves. And instruments don't need to feel inspired to play. They need to be picked up.

Adrian's demotivation comes from believing he is the source. That the fire has to come from inside him. That if he doesn't feel it, it isn't there.

But what if the fire was never his to begin with? What if he's the brush, not the painter?

Krishna then addresses the other trap--the perfectionism that makes demotivated people quit entirely:

  "Every endeavor is covered by some sort of fault, just as fire is covered by smoke. Therefore one should not give up the work which is born of his nature, O son of Kunti, even if such work is full of fault."

  -- Bhagavad-gita 18.48

Every endeavor is covered by some sort of fault. Every painting has something wrong with it. Every performance falls short somewhere. Every effort contains imperfection.

Just as fire is covered by smoke.
