Repression doesn't solve anything. It just relocates the problem. Buries it. Lets it rot inside you.

Her body knows. Chronic headaches. Stomach issues. Insomnia. Panic attacks that come out of nowhere. Her body is screaming what her mind won't acknowledge: this isn't working. You're not fine. You need to feel this.

But she doesn't know how.

The Gita Speaks: What Repression Cannot Accomplish

The Bhagavad-gita doesn't advocate repression. It questions it directly--with a bluntness that stops you cold.

"Even a man of knowledge acts according to his own nature, for everyone follows his nature. What can repression accomplish?"
  -- Bhagavad-gita 3.33

Read that again.

Even a man of knowledge. Not a fool. Not someone weak-willed. Even someone wise, educated, disciplined--even that person acts according to their nature. Knowledge alone doesn't override what lives inside you.

Everyone follows his nature. This is not pessimism. It's realism. You have a nature. Emotions, tendencies, responses that are woven into who you are. They don't vanish because you refuse to acknowledge them.

What can repression accomplish? This is Krishna's challenge. Not a rhetorical flourish--a genuine question. What has pushing it down actually achieved? Has it healed anything? Resolved anything? Or has it simply moved the pain from one room to another, where it festers unseen?

Lisa has been repressing for thirty years. And what has it accomplished?

Headaches. Insomnia. A marriage where her husband can't reach her. A life that looks perfect and feels hollow.

Repression accomplished exactly nothing.

The feelings didn't go away. They went underground. And underground, they grew roots.

Krishna doesn't say: feel nothing. He doesn't say: let your emotions run wild. He says: repression doesn't work. You cannot override your nature by pretending it doesn't exist.

The alternative isn't collapse. It's acknowledgment. Feeling what is there so you can work with it rather than against it. Integration, not suppression.

Lisa has been treating her emotions like enemies to be conquered. Krishna suggests they are her nature--to be understood, not crushed.

Living the Teaching

Lisa's therapist gives her homework.

"Fifteen minutes a day," she says. "Just sit. Feel whatever comes up. Don't push it away."

"What if nothing comes up?"

"Then feel the nothing. But don't distract. No phone. No TV. Just you and whatever's there."

Lisa tries. First day, she lasts four minutes before grabbing her phone. Second day, six minutes.

Third day, she sits for the full fifteen. And halfway through, something breaks. She starts crying. Not gentle tears. Sobbing. Ugly. Loud.

She doesn't even know what she's crying about. Everything. Nothing. Years of unfelt feelings flooding out.

And it's terrifying. And exhausting. And liberating.

Because the feelings don't destroy her. They pass through. Like weather. Intense. Real. But temporary.

And on the other side of them, there's clarity.

Practice Box

The Practice of Integration, Not Repression

1. Ask Krishna's question honestly. Look at what you've been repressing and ask: What has this accomplished? Has suppressing it healed anything? Or has it simply relocated the pain?

2. Feel it in your body. Where does the repressed emotion live? Chest? Stomach? Throat? Let yourself experience the physical sensation without trying to make it stop.

3. Name what surfaces. Don't judge it. Don't fix it. Just name it. "I'm angry." "I'm scared." "I'm grieving." Acknowledgment is the first step toward working with your nature instead of against it.

4. Let it move through you. Cry. Write it out. Move your body. Emotions need expression--not explosion, but a channel. What you feel, you can process. What you repress, owns you.

The Way Forward

She talks to her husband. Really talks. About how scared she is. How disconnected she feels. How much she needs him.

He cries. "I've been trying to reach you for years. But you were always 'fine.'"

"I'm not fine," she says. "I haven't been fine in a long time. But I'm trying to be honest now."

Six months later, Lisa's mother dies. And Lisa grieves. Openly. Fully. She doesn't hold it together at the funeral. She cries. She lets people see her broken.

A relative says, "You're being so brave."

Lisa laughs through tears. "I'm not being brave. I'm just feeling what I feel. For the first time in my life."

Because repression isn't strength. It's fear. Fear that if you feel, you'll be overwhelmed. Destroyed.

But the opposite is true. What you feel, you can process. What you repress, controls you.

Krishna asked: What can repression accomplish?

Lisa finally has her answer: Nothing. Nothing at all.

The path forward isn't around the feeling. It's through it. Feel it. All of it. And discover you're strong enough to survive it.

You always were.

Reflection

- What emotion are you repressing right now? What are you telling yourself you can't afford to feel? Ask Krishna's question honestly: What has that repression accomplished?
- When was the last time you let yourself fully feel something difficult? What happens when you imagine allowing yourself to cry, to rage, to grieve without controlling it?
- What would it mean to work with your nature instead of against it? Can you sit with discomfort for fifteen minutes today? What might surface if you stopped pushing it down?

---

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 51: Forgetfulness

Opening Story

Daniel sets three alarms every night.

One to wake up for meditation. One for his morning workout. One for his gratitude practice.

Every morning, he hits snooze on all three.

By 7:30 AM, he's rushed, stressed, and already behind schedule. No meditation. No workout. No gratitude. Just coffee and crisis mode.

This has been happening for six months.

Six months ago, he went to a retreat. Had a profound experience. Saw clearly--really saw--what mattered. Who he wanted to be. How he wanted to live.

He came home transformed. Committed. Clear.

For two weeks, he did it all. Perfectly.

Then work got busy. Then his son got sick. Then there was the thing with his mother-in-law. Then just life.

And slowly, imperceptibly, he forgot.

Not intellectually. He still knows meditation is important. Still believes in presence. Still wants to live consciously.

But somehow, between knowing and doing, there's forgetting.

And now he's back where he started. Sleepwalking through life. Reactive. Stressed. Unconscious.

How does this keep happening?

Reflective Bridge

We all know what matters.

We know we should be present. Should practice gratitude. Should meditate, exercise, connect with loved ones, live our values.

We've read the books. Been to the workshops. Had the insights.

And yet, we forget. Not because we're stupid. Because we're human. And humans are forgetting machines.

We remember what's urgent. The deadline. The email. The crisis. We forget what's important. The practice. The presence. The purpose.

Daniel remembers to check his phone forty times a day. Forgets to check in with himself once. Remembers every work meeting. Forgets the promise he made to play catch with his son.

This is the default. The gravity. The pull of unconsciousness.

And resisting it requires more than good intentions.

We forget because remembering is uncomfortable. Living consciously means facing what we're avoiding. Feeling what we're numbing. Choosing what's hard over what's easy.

"I forgot to meditate" is easier than "I chose not to meditate because sitting with myself is uncomfortable."

We don't forget randomly. We forget strategically. What we don't want to face, we conveniently don't remember.

The Gita Speaks: The Source of Remembrance and Forgetting

Krishna reveals something startling about forgetfulness. It isn't merely a failure of willpower or discipline. It operates at a far deeper level.

"I am seated in everyone's heart, and from Me come remembrance, knowledge and forgetfulness. By all the Vedas am I to be known; indeed I am the compiler of Vedanta, and I am the knower of the Vedas."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 15.15

I am seated in everyone's heart. The Supreme is not distant. He is within--closer than your own thoughts, present in the very seat of consciousness.

From Me come remembrance, knowledge and forgetfulness. This is the revelation that changes everything. Remembrance and forgetfulness both come from the same divine source. Forgetfulness is not random. It is not simply a failure of your brain or your discipline. It has a deeper origin. When we turn away from what is essential, when we orient our lives toward distraction, forgetfulness follows--not as punishment, but as consequence. And when we sincerely seek what matters, remembrance is granted.

By all the Vedas am I to be known. The purpose of all knowledge points in one direction: toward the Supreme seated in the heart. When Daniel forgets to meditate, he isn't just forgetting a habit. He's forgetting the one who sits within him, waiting to be remembered.

And Krishna reveals the mechanism through which forgetfulness operates in the material world:

"The Supreme Lord is situated in everyone's heart, O Arjuna, and is directing the wanderings of all living entities, who are seated as on a machine, made of the material energy."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 18.61

Directing the wanderings of all living entities. We wander. That's the word. Not marching purposefully toward our goals--wandering. Pulled this way and that by the machinery of material energy. Phone notifications. Work crises. Social obligations. The body's demands for comfort and stimulation.

Seated as on a machine, made of the material energy. The body and mind are a machine. And the machine has its own momentum. Its own gravity. It pulls toward distraction, toward comfort, toward forgetting. Daniel isn't weak. He's sitting on a machine that is designed to carry him away from remembrance unless he consciously steers it back.

But here's the key: the Lord who permits forgetfulness also grants remembrance. The same source offers both. The difference is the direction of the heart. Turn toward distraction, and forgetfulness deepens. Turn toward the divine, and remembrance awakens.

Daniel's problem isn't willpower. It's orientation.

Living the Teaching

Daniel realizes: willpower isn't enough. He needs architecture. He needs to build remembrance into his environment. His schedule. His systems.

But more than systems, he needs to understand what he's actually forgetting. Not just a morning routine. He's forgetting the one seated in his heart.

He starts simply. One practice. Not three alarms for three habits. One. Sitting quietly for ten minutes, turning his attention inward, toward the presence that is already there.

Practice Box

The Practice of Remembrance

1. Recognize the source. Remembrance and forgetfulness both come from a deeper place than your willpower. Stop blaming yourself for forgetting. Start asking: What am I oriented toward? Distraction--or the presence within?

2. Simplify ruthlessly. Don't try to remember ten things. Remember one. The one seated in the heart. Let everything else flow from that single orientation.

3. Build environmental cues. Put reminders everywhere. Not just alarms--objects, images, words that redirect your attention inward. Make forgetting harder than remembering.

4. Return without guilt. When you forget--and you will--don't spiral. The machine of material energy will carry you away. Just steer back. Same day. No shame. The Lord who permits forgetfulness also welcomes your return.

The Way Forward

Daniel makes changes.

He puts his meditation cushion in the middle of the floor. Can't miss it. Can't walk past it. He tells his wife, "I'm sitting at 6 AM. Will you check on me if I'm not up?" He sets one alarm. Puts the phone across the room so he has to get up to reach it.

And slowly, it works. Not perfectly. He still forgets sometimes. Hits snooze. Skips days.

But more often than not, he remembers. And on the days he forgets, he returns. Same day. No guilt. No spiral.

Because remembrance isn't about perfection. It's about orientation. Turning the heart back toward what matters. Again and again. Despite the wandering machine that carries you elsewhere.

A year later, Daniel still meditates most mornings. His son asks, "Dad, why do you sit on that pillow every morning?"

"To remember," Daniel says.

"Remember what?"

"That something lives in my heart that's bigger than my to-do list. That I'm more than the machine I'm riding. That if I don't pause, I'll wander right past what matters."

His son nods, not fully understanding. But one morning, Daniel finds him sitting on the cushion. Eyes closed. Breathing.

"What are you doing?" Daniel asks.

"Remembering," his son says. "Like you."

And Daniel realizes: what you practice, you teach. What you remember, you pass on. His son won't remember every lesson Daniel tries to teach. But he'll remember what Daniel does. Consistently. Daily. Even imperfectly.

Remembrance. The practice of returning, again and again, to the one seated in the heart.

Reflection

- What do you keep forgetting? What practice, promise, or presence keeps slipping away? Is it truly forgetting--or is it the machine of material energy carrying you toward distraction?
- Where is your heart oriented? Toward the presence within, or toward the endless wandering of external demands? What would it mean to reorient--even slightly--toward remembrance?
- What one thing do you want to remember? Not ten things. One. The one seated in your heart. What would your life look like if you built it around that single remembrance?

---

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 52: Illusion

Opening Story

Sophia looks at her life from the outside and sees success.

VP at a Fortune 500 company. Corner office. Six-figure salary. Respected. Accomplished. Powerful.

From the inside, she sees something else entirely.

Exhaustion. Emptiness. A gnawing sense that she's been chasing the wrong thing for twenty years.

She got the promotion she wanted. Felt nothing. Bought the house she dreamed of. Still felt hollow. Earned the respect she craved. Still feels inadequate.

Every goal she reached revealed the same truth: it wasn't what she thought it would be. The satisfaction lasted days. Sometimes hours. Then the emptiness returned, demanding the next achievement, the next validation, the next thing that would finally make her feel whole.

But nothing does.

And she's starting to suspect: nothing ever will.

Because the problem isn't that she's achieving the wrong goals. The problem is believing that achievement will fill what's empty.

That's the illusion. And she's been living it her entire adult life.

Reflective Bridge

Illusion isn't fantasy. It's misidentification.

Thinking the temporary is permanent. The material is essential. The external is internal.

Sophia's illusion: she believed that becoming powerful would make her feel powerful. That being successful would make her feel successful. That having more would make her be more.

But it doesn't work that way. The emptiness isn't lack of achievement. It's lack of connection to her actual self.

She's built an empire on the foundation of an illusion: that she is what she accomplishes. That her value equals her productivity. That her worth requires external validation.

No amount of success can fill a hole that exists because you've forgotten who you are.

This is the fundamental illusion the Gita addresses: mistaking the temporary self for the eternal self. The body for the soul. The role for the reality.

Sophia has been climbing a ladder propped against the wrong wall. And the higher she climbs, the more obvious it becomes: she's still empty. Still searching. Still not home.

Because you can't find yourself by becoming someone else. Even a successful someone else.

The Gita Speaks: Seeing Through Maya

The Bhagavad-gita calls this illusion maya--the divine energy that covers reality so convincingly that we mistake the covering for the thing itself.

"This divine energy of Mine, consisting of the three modes of material nature, is difficult to overcome. But those who have surrendered unto Me can easily cross beyond it."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 7.14

This divine energy of Mine. The illusion isn't a defect. It isn't a mistake in the system. It is divine energy--powerful, purposeful, operating by design. That's why it's so convincing. Sophia didn't fall for a cheap trick. She fell for something crafted by the Supreme. The material world's promise--that the next achievement will complete you, that the next possession will satisfy you--is extraordinarily persuasive because it comes from an extraordinarily powerful source.

Consisting of the three modes of material nature. The illusion operates through three forces: goodness, passion, and ignorance. Sophia's drive was passion--rajas--the mode that says more, faster, higher. Her exhaustion was ignorance--tamas--the heaviness that follows burnout. Even her moments of clarity were sattva--goodness--which, while illuminating, still operates within the material framework. All three modes keep you looking outward. All three modes are part of the covering.

Difficult to overcome. Krishna doesn't minimize this. He doesn't say: just think your way out. He says it's difficult. Twenty years of Sophia's life testify to that difficulty. She is intelligent. Disciplined. Accomplished. And still trapped. Because intelligence alone cannot see through divine illusion. The mind that is covered cannot uncover itself using its own covered tools.

But those who have surrendered unto Me can easily cross beyond it. This is the turn. The escape isn't more effort. More achievement. More willpower. The escape is surrender. Not passive defeat--active redirection. Turning from the illusion toward the source of the illusion. When Sophia stops trying to fill herself with what the material energy offers and turns toward what lies beyond it, the covering lifts. Not through her effort, but through grace. Easily, Krishna says. The word is striking. What decades of striving couldn't accomplish, surrender can--easily.

Sophia has been trying to see through the illusion by climbing higher within it. But the view from the top of the ladder is the same illusion, just from a higher vantage point. The solution isn't a better position within maya. It's stepping beyond maya altogether.

And the door is surrender. Not to defeat. To the divine source that both creates the illusion and offers the way through it.

Living the Teaching

Sophia takes a sabbatical. Three months. No work. No email. No title. Just her.

The first week is agony. Who is she without the work? Without the achievement? Without the constant validation that she's valuable, important, needed?

The second week, she sits with the discomfort. Watches it. Feels it. And realizes: this emptiness has always been there. Work just covered it.

The third week, something shifts. She stops asking "Who am I without my job?" and starts asking "What is this covering that I've mistaken for reality?"

The illusion isn't out there. It's in here--in the assumption that she is her roles, her titles, her accomplishments. The three modes have been running her life: passion driving her forward, ignorance crashing her down, brief moments of clarity never quite enough to break through.

She begins to understand: she can't think her way out. She's been trying for twenty years. The energy is too powerful, too divine in its design.

What she can do is stop fighting it on its own terms. Stop trying to win within the illusion. And turn toward what lies beyond it.

Practice Box

The Practice of Seeing Through Illusion

1. Name the illusion you're living. What temporary thing have you mistaken for permanent? What external achievement have you confused with internal wholeness? Be specific. Be honest.

2. Recognize the three modes at work. When are you driven by passion--restless, striving, never satisfied? When do you crash into ignorance--heavy, avoidant, numb? When do you taste clarity--and why doesn't it last? All three are part of the covering.

3. Stop trying to overcome it alone. Krishna says this energy is difficult to overcome. Stop pretending willpower is enough. The mind that is covered cannot uncover itself.

4. Practice surrender. Not passive resignation. Active turning. Turn your attention from what the material energy promises toward what lies beyond it. Ask: What would it mean to stop trying to fill myself with what will never satisfy?

The Way Forward

When Sophia returns to work, she's different. Not outwardly. Same job. Same title. Same office.

But inwardly, she's free.

She's no longer trying to prove her worth through achievement. She understands now that the entire system--the striving, the achieving, the temporary satisfaction, the return of emptiness--is the illusion functioning exactly as designed. Powerful. Convincing. Divine in origin.

She hasn't escaped the material world. She still works within it. But she's stopped mistaking it for ultimate reality.

A colleague notices. "You seem lighter. What happened on your sabbatical?"

Sophia considers. "I stopped trying to win a game that's designed so you never quite can."

"What do you mean?"

"The next promotion. The next achievement. The next thing that's supposed to finally make you feel complete. It never does. Not because you're failing. Because it's not designed to. It's an energy--powerful, divine even--and it keeps you looking in the wrong direction. I just stopped looking there."

Her colleague looks confused.

Sophia doesn't try to explain further. Krishna said it plainly: this energy is difficult to overcome. You can't argue someone out of an illusion they haven't yet recognized as one.

But those who surrender can easily cross beyond it. Easily. That word stays with her.

Not through twenty more years of striving. Through turning.

Reflection

- What illusion are you living? What temporary thing have you mistaken for permanent? What role have you confused with reality? What are you chasing that you believe will finally make you whole?
- Have you recognized how powerful the covering is? Krishna calls it divine energy--difficult to overcome. Are you still trying to think or achieve your way through it? What would it mean to stop?
- What would surrender look like for you? Not giving up. Turning. From what the material energy promises toward what lies beyond it. What are you afraid of losing if you stop striving? And is what you'd lose even real?

---

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 53: Material World

Opening Story

2015: The apartment is everything.

Three thousand square feet. Floor-to-ceiling windows overlooking the city. Italian marble in the bathroom. A kitchen Raj never uses.

He bought it cash. Bonus from the Goldman deal.

Standing in the empty space the day he got the keys, he felt it: arrival. Success. Proof.

I made it.

The apartment said things Raj couldn't say himself. To his parents who'd worked double shifts so he could go to college. To the kids who'd mocked his accent in middle school. To the part of himself that still felt like the immigrant kid who didn't belong.

See? I'm somebody now.

He fills it carefully. Eames chair. Restoration Hardware couch. Abstract art he doesn't understand but knows is expensive. Each piece another brick in the wall between who he was and who he's become.

The apartment is featured in Architectural Digest. His mother sends copies to relatives in India.

Raj frames the magazine cover.

Reflective Bridge

2025: The apartment is just a place.

Same floor-to-ceiling windows. Same Italian marble. Same expensive furniture. But Raj sees it differently now. Not because the apartment changed. Because he did.

What happened between then and now wasn't dramatic. No crisis. No loss. No spiritual awakening in an ashram. Just accumulation.

The apartment led to the vacation home in the Hamptons. Which led to the vintage car collection. Which led to the art investments. Which led to the watch collection.

Each acquisition promised fulfillment. Each delivered briefly. Then the hunger returned. Sharper.

Raj remembers the moment he knew something was wrong. Standing in his walk-in closet--a room bigger than his childhood bedroom--unable to decide which of forty custom suits to wear. And feeling empty.

Not sad. Not depressed. Empty. Like he'd been filling a vessel with a hole in the bottom.

"You have everything," his brother said during their last visit. Raj heard what his brother didn't say: And you're still not happy.

That's when he started asking the question. Not out loud. Just to himself, late at night, sitting in the Eames chair that cost more than his father's annual salary:

What's all this for?

The Gita Speaks: Understanding What This World Is Made Of

The Bhagavad-gita doesn't condemn the material world. It explains what it actually is--and what it isn't. And that explanation changes everything.

"Earth, water, fire, air, ether, mind, intelligence and false ego--altogether these eight comprise My separated material energies."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 7.4

Earth, water, fire, air, ether. The physical elements. Everything Raj can see, touch, taste. The marble countertops. The Italian leather. The city glittering below his windows. All of it--every atom of the material world--is energy. Not random, not self-generated. Divine energy, separated from its source.

Mind, intelligence and false ego. This is the revelation that stops Raj cold. The material energy isn't just physical. It includes the mind that desires the apartment. The intelligence that strategizes how to earn it. And--most critically--the false ego that says "I am this. I am what I own. I am somebody because of what I've accumulated." The very faculty telling Raj that possessions equal identity is itself part of the material energy. The judge is part of the system being judged.

My separated material energies. Separated. The word matters. This energy belongs to the Supreme but is separated from Him--functioning at a distance, operating according to its own laws. The material world isn't evil. It's separated. And separated things, by definition, cannot reconnect you to the source. Raj has been trying to find wholeness through what is inherently separate. No wonder the vessel has a hole.

But Krishna doesn't stop there. He reveals what the material world is missing:

"Besides this inferior nature, O mighty-armed Arjuna, there is a superior energy of Mine, which are all living entities who are struggling with material nature and are sustaining the universe."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 7.5

Besides this inferior nature. Inferior. Not worthless--inferior. The material energy has its place, its function. But it is lower. Secondary. It was never designed to be ultimate. Raj treated marble and money as if they were the highest reality. Krishna says they are the lower energy.

There is a superior energy. Something higher exists. And it isn't another possession or achievement.

Which are all living entities. The superior energy is consciousness itself. The living beings. Raj himself--not his body, not his bank account, not his closet full of suits--but the aware being looking out through his eyes. That is the superior energy. The one thing in his three-thousand-square-foot apartment that isn't made of earth, water, fire, air, ether, mind, intelligence, or false ego.

Struggling with material nature. Struggling. Not enjoying. Not mastering. Struggling. The living entity--superior by nature--finds itself entangled in the inferior energy, trying to extract satisfaction from what cannot ultimately provide it. Raj's entire career, his entire accumulation, has been a superior energy struggling with an inferior one. Consciousness trying to feel whole through marble.

That's why the vessel has a hole. He's been pouring inferior energy into a container designed for something superior.

Living the Teaching

Raj doesn't sell the apartment. That would be running away. Reacting. Making material renunciation another form of materialism.

Instead, he changes his relationship to it.

The apartment becomes a tool, not an identity. A comfortable place to live. A space to host his aging parents when they visit. But not proof of arrival. Not the thing that makes him somebody.

He stops the acquisitions. Not from deprivation. From clarity. He now understands what the material world actually is: eight elements of separated, inferior energy. Useful. Real. But not ultimate. Not capable of filling what only the superior energy can fill.

The vacation home goes up for sale. Not because ownership is bad. But because maintaining it created stress, not peace.

The car collection gets donated. Not because cars are evil. But because he realized he kept them to impress people he didn't even like.

What remains is what serves.

Practice Box

The Practice of Right Relationship with the Material World

1. Name the eight elements at work. Look at what you're pursuing or clinging to. Is it earth, water, fire, air, ether? Is it mind--desire, craving? Intelligence--strategizing for more? False ego--"I am what I own"? Recognize the material energy for what it is.

2. Identify where you're struggling. You are the superior energy--consciousness itself. Where are you struggling with the inferior energy, trying to extract wholeness from what is inherently separated? Name the struggle honestly.

3. Ask: Does this serve, or do I serve it? For each significant possession or pursuit, ask whether this tool serves your life--or has it become a master demanding your energy, attention, money, and peace.

4. Hold everything lightly. Appreciate what you have without clinging to it. Enjoy the apartment, the car, the meal--fully, presently. Then release it. It is inferior energy. Beautiful, useful, temporary. Not you.

The Way Forward

Raj's parents visit for his fortieth birthday. His mother walks through the apartment slowly. She pauses at the framed Architectural Digest cover.

"You were so proud of this," she says softly.

"I was," Raj admits.

"Are you still?"

He looks at the cover. The perfectly staged apartment. The version of himself that believed arrival came through square footage and marble.

"I'm proud of different things now," he says.

Like volunteering at the community center in Queens, teaching financial literacy to immigrant families. Like restructuring his work schedule so he's home for dinner. Like sitting in his apartment and feeling nothing about it--no pride, no shame. Just a comfortable space made of earth, water, and fire, serving its function.

His mother nods. She doesn't say it, but Raj feels her understanding.

That night, after his parents go to bed, Raj sits in the Eames chair. The city glitters below. Same view. Same chair. Same apartment.

Different person sitting in it.

Someone who knows: the material world is real. Useful. Beautiful, even. Eight elements of divine energy, separated from the source.

But it's not ultimate. It's the inferior nature.

And he--the one sitting in the chair, the one aware of the glittering city and the quiet room and the beating of his own heart--he is the superior energy.

Not because of what he owns. Because of what he is.

Reflection

- What are you trying to prove with your possessions? What do your clothes, your car, your home say about you that you can't say yourself? How much of your identity is built on earth, water, fire, air, ether, mind, intelligence, and false ego?
- Where are you--the superior energy--struggling with the inferior nature? Where are you trying to extract wholeness from what is inherently separated from its source? What would it feel like to stop?
- Can you use the material world without being used by it? Can you appreciate the eight elements for what they are--real, useful, beautiful, but inferior--and look for what is superior? What would that shift change in your daily life?

---

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 54: Nature

Opening Story

March.

Everything begins again.

Dr. Nakamura watches the cherry blossoms outside her office window and feels the pull. Energy. Ambition. The drive to do.

She's been a psychiatrist for fifteen years, but spring always makes her restless. New patients. New research proposals. New protocols to try. More. Better. Faster.

The feeling is electric. She rides it hard--sees patients back-to-back, stays late writing grant proposals, volunteers for the hospital ethics committee.

By May, she's running on five hours of sleep and three cups of coffee.

"You're burning out," her partner says gently.

"I'm productive," Dr. Nakamura counters.

But she knows the truth: she's not in control of the energy. The energy is in control of her.

July. The crash comes exactly when it always does. The grants get rejected. Two long-term patients relapse. The committee work becomes political and draining.

Dr. Nakamura feels the heaviness descend. Not quite depression. More like inertia. She shows up to work. Goes through the motions. But the spark is gone. At night, she scrolls social media for hours. Can't stop. Can't start anything that matters.

She reads something in a book a patient recommended--the Bhagavad-gita. Something about the forces that drive material nature. And for the first time, she has language for what keeps happening to her.

Reflective Bridge

Most of us live at the mercy of forces we don't understand.

We don't choose our moods. They choose us. We don't decide to burn out or shut down. Something larger operates through us--drives us forward, pulls us into heaviness, occasionally grants us clarity.

Dr. Nakamura has spent fifteen years studying the human mind. She can diagnose her patients with precision. But she can't explain why she cycles through the same pattern every year--frenetic spring, collapsed summer--as if something beyond her willpower is running the show.

And something is.

Not a psychological disorder. Not a chemical imbalance, though those are real. Something more fundamental. The forces of nature itself, operating through her body and mind with or without her consent.

She didn't choose to feel driven in March. She didn't choose to feel heavy in July. These aren't failures of character. They're the machinery of material nature doing what it does.

The question isn't: why does this happen? The question is: who is the "I" that it happens to? And what is the relationship between that "I" and the nature that seems to control it?

The Gita Speaks: The Forces Behind the Curtain

Krishna pulls back the curtain on what drives the material world--and the beings within it.

"The spirit soul, confused by the influence of the false ego, believes itself to be the author of activities that are actually performed by the three modes of the material nature."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 3.27

The spirit soul, confused. Not ignorant. Not stupid. Confused. Dr. Nakamura is brilliant--and confused. She thinks she is the one driving herself to exhaustion in spring. She thinks she is the one collapsing into inertia in summer. But Krishna says the activities are performed by the three modes of material nature. She is watching from inside a machine, mistaking the machine's operations for her own choices.

Believes itself to be the author. This is the core confusion. "I am productive." "I am lazy." "I am driven." "I am stuck." But what if none of these are you? What if they are the modes--passion, ignorance, goodness--cycling through the machinery of nature, and you are the consciousness observing it all?

Actually performed by the three modes. The three modes--goodness, passion, ignorance--perform the activities. Not you. The spring energy is passion. The summer collapse is ignorance. The rare moments of balanced clarity are goodness. Dr. Nakamura didn't create these forces. They operate through her.

Krishna adds a compassionate warning:

"Confused by the modes of material nature, the ignorant devote themselves entirely to material activities and become attached to them. But the wise should not disturb them, although these duties are inferior because of the lack of knowledge that the executors suffer from."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 3.29

Confused by the modes, the ignorant devote themselves entirely. This is most of humanity. Not evil. Not foolish. Simply confused--so identified with the modes that they believe the modes are them. They ride passion until it burns out, crash into ignorance, and call this "life."

The wise should not disturb them. Krishna doesn't say: argue with them. Correct them. Fix them. He says: don't disturb them. This is profound compassion. People must discover the truth of their own nature in their own time. Dr. Nakamura couldn't hear this five years ago. Now she can.

But what exactly is this nature that operates through her?

"Earth, water, fire, air, ether, mind, intelligence, and false ego, these eight elements together constitute My separate material energies."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 7.4

Eight elements. Everything Dr. Nakamura experiences--her body, her thoughts, her sense of identity--is made of these eight material elements. Including the mind that races in spring. Including the intelligence that plans the grant proposals. Including the false ego that says "I am productive" or "I am failing." All material. All nature. None of it is her--the conscious observer within.

And this nature doesn't operate randomly:

"This material nature, which is one of My energies, functions under My direction, O son of Kunti, and produces all moving and unmoving beings. By its command, this manifestation is created and annihilated again and again."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 9.10

Functions under My direction. Nature isn't chaos. It operates under divine direction. The cycles Dr. Nakamura experiences--the seasons of productivity and collapse--are not accidents. They are the material energy functioning as designed. Created and annihilated, again and again. The spring surge will collapse. The summer heaviness will lift. The cycle will repeat. Because that's what material nature does.

Produces all moving and unmoving beings. Everything in the material world--every body, every mind, every personality, every mood--is a product of this directed energy. Dr. Nakamura's body and mind are products of nature. But she is not.

Finally, Krishna reveals the timeless scope of this arrangement:

"It should be known that material nature and living entities have no beginning. Their transformations and the modes of matter are products of material nature."
  -- Bhagavad-gita 13.20

Material nature and living entities have no beginning. This isn't a recent arrangement. It's eternal. The modes have always been operating. The living entities have always been within this field. Dr. Nakamura's struggle isn't new. It's the ancient situation of consciousness entangled with matter.

Their transformations are products of material nature. Every transformation--spring's drive, summer's collapse, the aging of the body, the shifting of moods--is a product of nature. Not of the soul. The soul observes. Nature transforms.

Dr. Nakamura has been identifying with the transformations. Krishna says: you are not the transformation. You are the one watching it happen.

Living the Teaching

Dr. Nakamura doesn't quit her job. Doesn't run to the mountains.

She begins to observe. That's all. When the drive comes in spring, she notices: this is passion operating. Not me. When the heaviness comes in summer, she notices: this is ignorance operating. Not me.

She stops saying "I'm burning out" and starts saying "The mode of passion is burning through this body." She stops saying "I'm stuck" and starts saying "The mode of ignorance is heavy right now."

The difference seems semantic. It's seismic.

Because when you stop identifying as the author of activities performed by the modes, you gain something you never had before: distance. Space. The ability to observe without being consumed.

Practice Box

The Practice of Working with Nature

1. Recognize that you are not the doer. When you feel driven, heavy, or clear--notice: this is the modes of nature operating through the body and mind. Not you. You are the observer. Name the mode without identifying with it.

2. Stop blaming yourself for nature's operations. You didn't choose to burn out. You didn't choose to feel heavy. The eight elements of material nature--including mind, intelligence, and false ego--are doing what they do. Release the guilt. Observe with clarity.

3. Work with the modes, not against them. When passion is high, channel it wisely--don't ride it to destruction. When ignorance descends, rest genuinely--don't fight it with caffeine. When clarity arises, act from it--it won't last forever.

4. Remember the director. This nature functions under divine direction. The cycles aren't random. Trust the larger intelligence operating behind the transformations, even when they're uncomfortable.

The Way Forward

December. Dr. Nakamura sits in her office, watching the first snow fall.

She feels calm. Not the forced calm of someone suppressing passion. Not the heavy calm of ignorance. A calm born from seeing clearly: she is not the modes. The modes move through her. She watches.

The day ahead is full: six patients, a supervision meeting, a presentation at rounds. But she's not driven by it. And she's not dreading it. She's present.

Her partner notices. "You seem different."

Dr. Nakamura considers. "I think I've stopped believing I'm the author of everything happening in my body and mind."

"What does that mean?"

"It means when I feel driven, I don't say 'I'm on fire.' I say 'Passion is moving through.' When I feel heavy, I don't say 'I'm failing.' I say 'Ignorance is here for now.' It changes everything. Because I'm not those forces. I'm the one watching them."

That night, she writes in her journal:

The spirit soul believes itself to be the author. But the modes are doing the work. I'm not the spring surge or the summer crash. I'm the one who's been here through all of them. Unchanged. Watching.

Nature and the living entity have no beginning. This has always been the arrangement. Consciousness within matter. The observer within the machine.

I can't stop the machine. But I can stop pretending I'm the engine.

She closes the journal. Outside, the snow continues to fall. Quiet. Steady. Nature doing what nature does.

And inside, the observer--watching.

Reflection

- Where are you confusing yourself with the modes of nature? When you say "I'm driven" or "I'm stuck" or "I'm clear"--is that you speaking, or the modes operating through the body and mind? What would it mean to observe the modes without identifying as them?
- What would it look like to stop being the author? If the activities are performed by material nature and you are the spirit soul observing, what changes? How would your relationship to productivity, exhaustion, and mood shift?
- Can you trust the director behind nature? Krishna says this energy functions under His direction. The cycles of creation and annihilation, productivity and rest, are not accidents. Can you trust the larger intelligence--even when the current mode is uncomfortable?

---

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 55: Soul

Opening Story

One moment, James is driving home from the hospital.

The next, there is glass and metal and the world spinning sideways.

Then: nothing.

Not blackness. Not unconsciousness.

Nothing.

When he opens his eyes, he is looking down at his own body. At the paramedics cutting away his shirt. At the blood.

That is me, he thinks distantly. That is my body.

But whoever is thinking it--whoever is watching from above the scene--does not feel injured. Does not feel pain. Does not feel afraid.

Just watches. Calm. Aware. Present.

"We're losing him!"

The paramedic's voice is urgent, but James feels no urgency. He watches them work on the body below.

Then everything reverses. He is pulled back--fast, sudden--into the body on the stretcher. Pain floods in. Sensation returns. He gasps.

"He's back!"

Three months later, James sits in his apartment, physically healed but existentially shaken. The accident report said he was clinically dead for ninety seconds. Enough time to see something impossible: himself, from outside himself.

His doctor calls it a hallucination. Oxygen deprivation.

James knows it was not that.

Because the awareness that watched from above felt more real than anything he had ever experienced. More real than his job. More real than his relationships. More real than the body lying on the stretcher.

If I could observe my body, then I could not be my body. If I could witness my thoughts, I could not be my thoughts.

Then what am I?

Reflective Bridge

James starts reading. Not the medical journals he used to devour. Philosophy. Theology. Ancient texts.

He is looking for someone--anyone--who has articulated what he experienced.

Most of it feels abstract. Theoretical.

Then he finds the Bhagavad-gita.

And reads something that stops him cold.

The Gita Speaks: You Are Not the Body

Krishna tells Arjuna directly:

"As the embodied soul continually passes, in this body, from boyhood to youth to old age, the soul similarly passes into another body at death. The self-realized soul is not bewildered by such a change."
-- Bhagavad-gita 2.13

James reads it three times.

The embodied soul. Not "you are the body with a soul inside." But "the soul passes through bodies."

The soul--consciousness itself--is the constant. The body changes. Childhood becomes adolescence becomes adulthood becomes old age. But the awareness watching that progression never ages. It is the same consciousness at seven years old and seventy.

Krishna makes it even clearer:

"The soul is never born and never dies, nor does it become only after being born. For it is unborn, eternal, everlasting, and ancient; even though the body is slain, the soul is not."
-- Bhagavad-gita 2.20

This is what James experienced. When the paramedics were trying to restart his heart, the awareness that watched was utterly calm. Because it could not die.

The body could fail. The brain could stop. The heart could flatline.

But the soul--the witnessing consciousness--was untouched.

And what of the body's relationship to the soul? Krishna offers a vivid image:

"Just as a person puts on new garments after discarding the old ones, similarly, the soul accepts new material bodies, giving up the old and useless ones."
-- Bhagavad-gita 2.22

The body is clothing. You wear it, use it, and when it wears out, you change it. The wearer persists.

And nothing in this material world can touch the wearer:

"The soul can never be cut to pieces by any weapon, nor burned by fire, nor moistened by water, nor withered by the wind."
-- Bhagavad-gita 2.23

No element, no force, no instrument can destroy consciousness. It is beyond the reach of matter entirely.

Living the Teaching

James does not become a monk. Does not quit his job or renounce the world.

But something fundamental shifts.

He stops identifying primarily with his body. When he looks in the mirror now, he sees a temporary vehicle. Useful. Precious, even. But not ultimate. Not him.

When colleagues complain about aging--gray hair, weight gain, slower recovery--James feels the complaint differently.

The body ages. But I am not the body.

When fear arises--about illness, injury, death--he recognizes it as the body's survival instinct. Valid. Understandable. But not the whole truth.

Because the part of him that experienced those ninety seconds above his dying body knows: the soul cannot die. The body will. The personality might dissolve. The memories could fade. But consciousness itself is eternal.

Not because it is a nice idea. Because he experienced it directly.

Practice Box

1. Find the witness. Right now, notice: you are reading these words. Who is aware of the reading? Not your eyes--they are the instrument. Not your brain--it is processing. Who is witnessing the processing? That is closer to who you are than anything physical.

2. Trace consciousness back through time. Remember being a child. Then a teenager. Then twenty years old. Your body has completely changed. Your thoughts are different. Your circumstances transformed. But the awareness watching it all--that has been constant. That is your soul.

3. Separate identity from form. When you say "my body," notice: if it is yours, you cannot be it. You possess it. You inhabit it. But you are the possessor, not the possession. The same with "my mind," "my thoughts," "my emotions." All possessed. None of them fundamentally you.

4. Rest in the eternal. When fear of death arises, do not suppress it. But recognize: the body can die. The soul cannot. The forms change. Consciousness is constant.

The Way Forward

James's friend asks him over coffee:

"Are you afraid of dying now? After what happened?"

James considers.

"I'm afraid of pain," he says honestly. "The body doesn't want to suffer. That's natural."

"But death itself?"

He shakes his head.

"No."

Because he has tasted it. Ninety seconds of death. And what he found was not oblivion. It was presence. Awareness. Clarity. The soul observing the body's shutdown--and continuing.

"Does that make life meaningless?" his friend asks. "If you know you can't really die?"

James smiles.

"It makes it precious."

Because the body is temporary. This lifetime is finite. The soul is eternal--but this particular expression of it, in this body, with these people, in this time, that is unrepeatable.

"So I take care of this form," James says. "I honor it. Use it well. Love through it."

"But you don't cling to it."

"Right."

Because he knows: he is the driver, not the car. And when this vehicle wears out, he will not stop with it. He will continue. Different form. Same soul.

Unborn. Eternal. Everlasting.

Reflection

- Can you find the witness? Right now, who is reading these words? Who is aware of your thoughts? Who has been watching your entire life unfold? That constant observer--that is closer to your true self than any role, body, or identity.
- What would shift if you truly knew you were eternal? How would you live differently if you recognized that while the body ages and dies, the consciousness witnessing your life is unborn and deathless? What fears would lose their power?
- Are you identified with the vehicle or the driver? When you say "my body," "my thoughts," "my life"--who possesses them? If they are yours, you cannot be them. Can you rest in being the possessor, not the possession?

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 56: Reincarnation

Opening Story

Maya wakes at 3 AM from the same dream.

Again.

She is in a place she has never been--a stone courtyard, mountains in the distance, air so clean it hurts. And she knows it. Every detail. The crack in the third step. The way the light hits the eastern wall at dawn. The sound of bells from the temple she cannot quite see.

I have been here before.

But she has not. She has never left California. Never traveled to the Himalayas. Never seen the place that appears in her dreams with photographic clarity.

Yet the knowing persists. Not like a memory. Like truth.

It starts when Maya is twenty-six. First, just the dreams. Then, the strange familiarities. She meets someone and feels instant recognition--not attraction, not comfort, but knowing. Like picking up a conversation that paused mid-sentence lifetimes ago.

She visits new cities and finds herself navigating without maps, turning down streets she has "never" seen, knowing what is around corners she has "never" turned.

When she is twenty-eight, she finally goes. To Nepal. To the mountains. To find the courtyard from her dreams.

And when she sees it--when she climbs those stone steps and feels the air and hears the bells--she does not feel surprised.

She feels home.

"How long have you been coming here?" the monk asks.

Maya answers before thinking:

"Lifetimes."

Reflective Bridge

Most people dismiss these experiences. Past lives. Reincarnation. Consciousness continuing beyond death. It sounds too strange. Too mystical.

But what if it is not mystical at all? What if it is simply continuity?

Energy does not disappear. It transforms. Matter does not vanish. It reconfigures. Why would consciousness--the most fundamental aspect of existence--be the one thing that just ends?

Maya starts researching. Children who remember previous lives with verifiable details. People who speak languages they have never learned. Phobias that match historical deaths.

The data is there. Strange. Compelling. Uncomfortable.

Because if consciousness continues, then everything changes. Every relationship becomes potentially eternal. Every action carries weight beyond this lifetime. Every lesson unlearned becomes a lesson repeated.

The Gita Speaks: The Soul Changes Bodies

The Bhagavad-gita does not treat reincarnation as belief. It presents it as fact.

Krishna tells Arjuna:

"As the embodied soul continually passes, in this body, from boyhood to youth to old age, the soul similarly passes into another body at death. The self-realized soul is not bewildered by such a change."
-- Bhagavad-gita 2.13

Read that again.

You already experience reincarnation within this lifetime. The body you had at five years old is gone. The body you had at fifteen is gone. The body you had at thirty--gone. And yet you persisted through every change.

The soul passed from a child's body to an adolescent's body to an adult's body. You watched it happen. You were not bewildered because it was gradual.

Death is simply the next change. Less gradual. More dramatic. But the same principle: the soul moves from one body to another. The self-realized person is not bewildered by this, just as you are not bewildered by the fact that your seven-year-old body no longer exists.

The consciousness you cultivate in this life--the patterns you strengthen, the desires you feed, the awareness you develop--that is what continues. If you spend your life obsessed with acquisition, that obsession does not end with death. It propels you toward circumstances where that attachment can play out again. If you spend your life cultivating peace, that peace continues. It shapes what comes next.

Reincarnation is not random. It is resonance. You gravitate toward what matches your consciousness.

Living the Teaching

Understanding reincarnation changes everything for Maya.

She stops treating this lifetime as her only shot. Not because she gets lazy--but because the pressure releases. If she does not "figure it all out" by death, there is continuity. The learning continues. The relationships that matter persist.

But she also stops wasting time. Because while the soul is eternal, this particular lifetime is not. These people. This body. This moment. All temporary. All precious.

She starts asking different questions:

Not "What do I want to accomplish before I die?" But "What consciousness do I want to carry forward?"

Not "How do I maximize this one life?" But "What patterns am I strengthening that I will take with me?"

The focus shifts from external achievement to internal cultivation. Because when death comes, she will not take her money. Will not take her reputation. Will not take her accomplishments. She will take her consciousness. And that consciousness will shape what is next.

Practice Box

1. See this life as one chapter, not the whole book. You are not starting from zero, and you will not end at death. You are continuing a journey. This life is one semester in a very long education. What are you here to learn?

2. Notice patterns that feel older than this lifetime. Instant connections with certain people. Inexplicable fears or attractions. Knowledge that seems to arrive without learning. These might be echoes--consciousness remembering what it has encountered before.

3. Ask: What consciousness am I cultivating? Not what you are achieving or accumulating, but what awareness you are developing. At death, you lose everything external--but the consciousness you have built continues. Invest there.

4. Release urgency without losing focus. You have time--more than one lifetime. But you do not have infinite time in this life, in this body, with these people. Honor both truths.

The Way Forward

Maya stays in Nepal for three months.

She does not "remember" a specific past life in detail. But the familiarity remains. Walking these paths. Sitting in meditation. Watching the mountains.

I have done this before.

"Do you think you lived here?" a fellow traveler asks.

Maya considers.

"Maybe. Or maybe I am living what I started lifetimes ago. Maybe the soul recognizes conditions that support its evolution, and I was drawn back."

"So you believe in reincarnation now?"

She smiles.

"I don't think belief matters. Either it's true or it isn't. And if it's true, then this life isn't about figuring everything out. It's about continuing the work. Whatever I don't finish here, I'll meet again. Whoever I don't forgive here, I'll encounter again. Whatever consciousness I build here, I'll carry forward."

"That's a lot of pressure."

"Or a lot of grace."

Because you get as many chances as you need. As many lifetimes as it takes. Not to achieve perfection. But to remember who you really are.

Maya returns to California six months later. The mountains stay in her dreams. But now she understands: they are not calling her back to a place. They are calling her back to herself. To the consciousness that has been evolving--lifetime after lifetime--toward clarity, toward freedom, toward home.

The soul similarly passes into another body at death. The self-realized soul is not bewildered by such a change.

Maya touches the mala beads she brought from Nepal. One hundred eight beads. She does not know how many lifetimes she has been walking this path.

But she knows: she will keep walking. Until the soul remembers fully. Until consciousness returns home.

Reflection

- What patterns in your life feel older than this lifetime? Are there fears, attractions, or connections that seem to arrive without explanation? What if they are echoes--the soul recognizing what it has encountered before?
- What consciousness are you cultivating that will continue? If you could only take your awareness forward--not your achievements, possessions, or reputation--what are you actually building?
- How would you live differently if relationships were eternal? What if the people in your life are not random--what if you have encountered them before and might encounter them again? How would that change how you treat them?

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 57: Life Cycle

Opening Story

The baby arrives at 3:42 AM.

Sarah holds her daughter for the first time and feels the weight. Not just physical weight. The weight of a new life beginning.

Where did you come from?

The question is not rhetorical. Sarah genuinely wonders: this consciousness now looking at her with unfocused eyes--where was it yesterday? Last week? Last year? Did it exist before this body? Will it continue after?

The baby grips Sarah's finger. And Sarah understands with sudden clarity: she is witnessing a beginning. Not the beginning--but a beginning. One more turn in a cycle that has been spinning long before this moment and will continue long after.

Twenty-five years later, that baby--now named Emma--sits across from Sarah at dinner, talking about her new job. Her apartment. Her plans. Sarah watches and sees: summer. The growth phase. Energy. Ambition. Expansion.

Sarah is sixty-eight when she feels it unmistakably: autumn. Not old age yet. But the shift from expansion to contraction. Her body does not recover as quickly. Her ambitions change. No longer about building--but about completing. Finishing what matters. Releasing what does not.

"Aren't you sad?" Emma asks, now forty-three with children of her own.

"Not sad. Just different. I don't need as much anymore."

Reflective Bridge

Most people fight the cycle. They try to stay in summer forever. Anti-aging. Productivity hacks. The desperate attempt to maintain expansion when contraction is calling.

But what if the cycle is not the enemy? What if it is the design?

Birth. Growth. Maturity. Decline. Death. Then birth again.

Not just across lifetimes--but within this one. Every day: waking, activity, evening, sleep. Every project: initiation, expansion, completion, release. Every relationship: meeting, deepening, changing, sometimes ending.

The cycle repeats at every scale. Fighting it creates suffering. Flowing with it creates grace.

And the force behind this cycle is not blind. It is not random. It has a source.

The Gita Speaks: The Source of All Cycles

Krishna reveals Himself as both origin and end:

"All created beings have their origin in these two natures. From all that is material and from all that is spiritual in this world, know with all certainty that I am both the origin and the dissolution."
-- Bhagavad-gita 7.6

Every cycle--every birth and death, every spring and winter, every beginning and ending--originates in the divine and returns to the divine. The cycle is not purposeless machinery. It is the breathing of God.

And where does the life within the cycle come from?

"I am the source of all spiritual and material worlds. Everything emanates from Me."
-- Bhagavad-gita 10.8

The energy propelling Sarah through her seasons, the force that brought Emma into being, the consciousness animating every living thing--all of it flows from a single source.

And that source governs both creation and completion:

"I am the all-devouring death, and I am the generating principle of all that is to exist."
-- Bhagavad-gita 10.34

Death is not the enemy of life. It is the other half of the same cycle. The same divine energy that generates also dissolves. Spring and winter are not opponents. They are partners in the rhythm of existence.

Living the Teaching

Sarah's eightieth birthday arrives. Three generations gather. Her grandchildren are in their summer--energy, plans, possibilities.

Sarah watches them with deep affection. She remembers that energy. That certainty. That hunger.

She does not envy it. She is in winter now. Not depressed. Not resigned. Just winter. The body is slowing. Sleep comes earlier. She walks instead of runs.

But something else is emerging: clarity. With fewer years ahead than behind, what matters becomes obvious. Not achievement. Not accumulation. Connection. Presence. Love.

"How do you feel about turning eighty?" Emma asks quietly.

Sarah smiles.

"Grateful."

"For what?"

"For having lived long enough to see all the seasons."

Spring: when everything was new and overwhelming. Summer: when she built her career, raised Emma, created a life. Autumn: when she released what no longer served and focused on what mattered. Winter: now. The culmination. The completion before the next beginning.

Practice Box

1. Identify which season you are in. Not just your age--but where you are in any cycle. Career? Relationship? Creative project? Are you in spring, summer, autumn, or winter? Each phase has different needs.

2. Stop fighting your season. If you are in autumn, do not force summer's energy. If you are in spring, do not expect autumn's wisdom. Honor where you actually are.

3. Notice the cycles within cycles. Every day has seasons. Every year. Every project. Practice recognizing the rhythm at all scales.

4. Remember: winter is not the end. After winter comes spring. After death comes birth. The cycle continues. Rest in that continuity instead of clinging to any single phase.

The Way Forward

Sarah is ninety-two when she feels it clearly: the transition. Winter moving toward its end.

Emma visits, now seventy herself.

"Are you scared?" Emma asks, holding her mother's hand.

Sarah shakes her head.

"I've lived a full cycle. Birth to death. I've seen how the rhythm works."

"And after?"

Sarah smiles.

"After winter comes spring."

She can feel it--the way autumn trees feel the coming winter, the way seeds feel the coming spring. The transition. The completion. The continuation.

I am both the origin and the dissolution.

The rhythm eternal. Sarah rests in it. Completely.

Reflection

- Which season are you in right now? In your life? In your career? In your relationships? Can you honor where you actually are instead of where you think you should be?
- What are you fighting that wants to change? Are you clinging to summer when autumn is calling? What would it mean to stop resisting and flow with your actual season?
- Can you see the cycles within cycles? Every day, every year, every project has its own rhythm of beginning, expansion, completion, and rest. What if the cycle is not the enemy but the teacher?

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 58: Love

Opening Story

Rachel and Adrian, Year One:

"I love you."

Adrian says it for the first time over dinner. Rachel feels her heart expand.

Finally. Someone who sees me. Chooses me. Completes me.

The love feels perfect. It promises: you will never be alone again.

Thomas and his son, Day One:

Thomas holds his newborn son and feels something break open. Not romantic. Not possessive. Just vast. He would die for this child. But more than that--he would live for this child. The love does not need reciprocation. It just is.

Rachel and Adrian, Year Three:

The fights start small. Adrian works late. Rachel feels abandoned. Rachel wants more closeness. Adrian feels smothered.

"I thought you loved me," Rachel says after one argument.

"I do," Adrian insists.

But they are both learning: the love they feel is conditional. When Adrian meets her needs, she loves him. When Rachel gives him space, he loves her. But when either one falls short, the love wavers.

Thomas and his son, Year Three:

His son throws a tantrum in the grocery store. Screaming. Thrashing. Embarrassing. Thomas feels frustration. But underneath--still--the love. Unwavering. His son does not have to perform. Does not have to be perfect. The love remains. Not because his son earns it. But because love is.

Reflective Bridge

Most of what we call "love" is actually desire dressed up. Desire for completion. For validation. For security.

We say "I love you," but we mean: "You make me feel good about myself." "You meet my needs." "You complete the picture of the life I want."

That is not love. That is transaction.

Real love does not need anything in return. It does not depend on the object being perfect. It exists independent of conditions. The love a parent feels for a child. The love a teacher feels for a student. Unconditional. Unchanging.

But here is the hard truth: most of us have never experienced it. Not fully. We have experienced attraction. Affection. Attachment. But unconditional love? That requires transformation.

The Gita Speaks: Love That Nourishes and Love That Frees

Krishna describes a love rooted in mutual nourishment:

"The demigods, being pleased by sacrifices, will also please you; thus nourishing one another, there will reign general prosperity for all."
-- Bhagavad-gita 3.11

Real love is not extraction. It is exchange. When both parties give without calculating returns, prosperity--emotional, spiritual, relational--flourishes. The problem arises when love becomes demand rather than offering.

But can we simply force ourselves to love differently? Krishna acknowledges the difficulty:

"Even a man of knowledge acts according to his own nature, for everyone follows his nature. What can repression accomplish?"
-- Bhagavad-gita 3.33

You cannot repress your way into unconditional love. Suppressing neediness does not create generosity. You must transform at the level of nature--not just behavior.

How? Through knowledge that dissolves the very root of grasping:

"Therefore, one who has renounced the fruits of his action, whose doubts are destroyed by transcendental knowledge, and who is situated firmly in the self is not bound by works, O conqueror of riches."
-- Bhagavad-gita 4.41

When you stop needing results--when you love without requiring love in return--you are free. Not bound by the transaction. Not devastated when the other person fails to reciprocate. Love becomes your nature, not your negotiation.

Living the Teaching

Rachel and Adrian, Year Seven:

They almost divorce. The counselor asks each of them: "What do you need from your partner?" Both lists are long.

Then the counselor asks: "What if you could give love without needing love in return?"

Rachel bristles. "That's not fair. Why should I give if I'm not receiving?"

"Not 'should.' Can you? Is it possible to love Adrian not because he completes you--but just because love is what you are capable of offering?"

Rachel goes quiet. She realizes: every ounce of love she has given has been transactional. I love you meant I love you when you meet my needs.

What would it mean to love freely?

Thomas and his son, Year Seven:

His son is struggling in school. Getting in fights. Acting out. Thomas gets a call from the principal.

On the drive home, Thomas asks: "Do you want to talk about what's happening?"

His son is quiet for a long time. Then: "I thought you'd be mad."

"I'm concerned. But I'm not mad."

"Why not?"

Thomas pulls over. "Because my love for you doesn't depend on your behavior. I want you to be okay--not because it makes me look good, but because I want you to be happy."

His son cries. It is the first time he has felt truly seen. Not judged. Not conditionally accepted. Just loved.

Practice Box

1. Notice when love becomes transaction. Pay attention when you feel love withdraw because someone disappoints you. That is conditional love. Real love does not disappear when needs go unmet.

2. Practice loving without needing reciprocation. Start small: give kindness without expecting gratitude. Offer help without needing acknowledgment. Notice how it feels--scary at first, then liberating.

3. Love people as they are, not as you need them to be. Stop trying to change your partner, your children, your friends. Love them in their actuality. That is the beginning of real love.

The Way Forward

Rachel and Adrian, Year Ten:

They are still together. Not because the relationship is perfect. But because they have both learned--slowly, imperfectly--to love differently.

Rachel stops demanding that Adrian complete her. She builds her own life. Cultivates her own peace. And from that wholeness, she offers love. Not because Adrian earns it. But because she has it to give.

They fight less. Not because conflict disappears--but because love is not withdrawn during conflict.

"I'm frustrated with you right now," Rachel says one evening.

"I know," Adrian replies.

"But I still love you."

"I know that too."

That is the shift. Love no longer conditional on perfect harmony. Love present even in imperfection.

Thomas and his son, Year Ten:

His son is sixteen. Pulling away. Testing boundaries.

"I don't need you," his son says during one fight.

"I know," Thomas says calmly. "But I'll be here anyway."

"Why?"

"Because that's what love does. It stays."

Years later, his son will remember this moment. The moment he understood: he was loved not because he performed. But because love was his father's nature.

And that understanding will change everything.

Reflection

- When does your love withdraw? Notice the moments when affection disappears because someone disappoints you. What would it mean to love them anyway--not because they meet your needs, but because love is what you are capable of offering?
- What do you need from others to feel complete? Make an honest list. Then ask: can you be whole without those needs being met? The belief that someone else completes you is the deepest block to unconditional love.
- Who have you loved freely--without needing anything back? A child? A student? Remember that feeling. That is closer to real love than anything transactional. Can you expand it?

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 59: Happiness

Opening Story

Kevin has been chasing happiness for forty-three years.

First, it was the job. When I make six figures, I will be happy. He made six figures. Was not happy.

So then it was the house. Got the house. Still not happy.

The car. The vacation. The promotion. The second house. Each time, the same pattern: desire, pursuit, achievement, brief satisfaction, then emptiness. And the search begins again.

Sitting in his home office--the one he spent fifty thousand dollars remodeling--Kevin feels the familiar hollowness.

When will I finally be happy?

His daughter visits for the weekend. She is twenty-five. Just graduated. Broke. Living in a tiny studio. Working an entry-level job. Driving a fifteen-year-old car.

And she is happy. Genuinely happy.

"How?" Kevin asks over breakfast.

"How what?"

"How are you so content? You have nothing."

She laughs.

"I have enough. And I'm not waiting for more to enjoy what's here."

Kevin feels something crack open. Because he has everything. And he is miserable.

Forty-three years of chasing. And his twenty-five-year-old daughter already knows what he is still learning: happiness is not at the end of achievement. It is underneath it. Available now. If you stop running long enough to notice.

Reflective Bridge

The problem is not desire itself. The problem is the belief that fulfilling desire produces lasting happiness.

Every achieved goal reveals the same truth: satisfaction is temporary. The moment you get what you want, the wanting shifts to something else. The treadmill never stops because it was never designed to stop. It is designed to keep you running.

But what if there were a different kind of happiness? One that does not depend on getting? One that does not fade when circumstances change?

The Gita Speaks: The Seasons of Happiness

Krishna begins with a fundamental insight about the nature of all experience:

"O son of Kunti, the nonpermanent appearance of happiness and distress, and their disappearance in due course, are like the appearance and disappearance of winter and summer seasons."
-- Bhagavad-gita 2.14

Happiness and distress are seasons. They come and go. Neither lasts. Chasing permanent happiness through impermanent conditions is like demanding permanent summer. It contradicts the nature of things.

The wise person does not chase one season or flee the other:

"O Arjuna, the best among men, the person who is not disturbed by happiness and distress and remains steady in both, is certainly eligible for liberation."
-- Bhagavad-gita 2.15

Liberation is not the elimination of distress. It is steadiness through both happiness and distress. The person who remains unmoved--not numb, but stable--when pleasure arrives and when it departs, that person is free.

But how? How do you stop chasing when every instinct says more?

"The embodied soul can be restrained from the enjoyment of the senses, even though the taste for sense objects may still remain. But, when one experiences a higher taste and thus renounces such engagements, their consciousness becomes steady."
-- Bhagavad-gita 2.59

You cannot simply suppress desire. The taste for pleasure persists even when you restrain yourself. The only lasting solution is a higher taste--a deeper happiness that makes the chase unnecessary.

And that deeper happiness is available to those who understand the source:

"The sages, knowing Me as the ultimate purpose of all sacrifices and austerities... attain peace from the pangs of material miseries."
-- Bhagavad-gita 5.29

The peace that resolves all material misery comes from knowing the source of everything. Not accumulating more. Not achieving more. But understanding where happiness actually lives.

Living the Teaching

Kevin starts an experiment.

For one month, he will not pursue anything new. No new projects. No new purchases. No new goals. Just be. With what is already here.

The first week is excruciating. Every day, his mind generates new desires. Maybe I should renovate the kitchen. I could use a new watch. What about that side business?

Each time, he notices the thought. And does not act on it. Just sits with the discomfort of not chasing.

Week two, something shifts. He starts noticing things. The way morning light hits the kitchen counter. The taste of coffee--really tasting it. The presence of his wife across the breakfast table.

Not perfect. Not dramatic. Just here.

By week four, Kevin realizes: he has been happy several times this month. Not because anything changed externally. But because he stopped running long enough to notice the happiness that was already present.

It is quieter than what he imagined. More stable. Less dependent on circumstances.

Practice Box

1. Stop the chase--just for today. Do not pursue anything new. No new goals, purchases, or projects. Just be with what is already here. Notice the discomfort. That is the addiction to becoming revealing itself.

2. Notice when you defer happiness. Pay attention to thoughts like "I will be happy when..." That is the mind postponing contentment. Ask: can I be content now--not when conditions change, but right now?

3. Feel the gap between craving and satisfaction. When desire arises, do not immediately act on it. Sit with the wanting. Notice: does fulfilling it actually deliver lasting happiness? Or does it just create the next craving?

The Way Forward

Six months later, Kevin's life looks almost the same. Same job. Same house. Same circumstances.

But he is different.

His daughter visits again.

"You seem lighter," she says.

Kevin smiles.

"I stopped running."

"Running from what?"

"From now. I was always living in 'when.' When I get this. When I achieve that. When circumstances finally align."

"And now?"

"Now I'm just here."

"Are you happy?"

Kevin considers the question.

"More often than before. Not because anything changed--but because I'm not waiting for it to."

That night, sitting in the home office he once thought would complete him, Kevin feels it: contentment. Not because he has everything. But because he has stopped believing he needs everything to be okay.

Real happiness does not come from getting. It comes from steadiness--through pleasure and through pain, through summer and through winter. Not chasing one season. Not fleeing the other. Just being present. Here. Now.

The person who is not disturbed by happiness and distress and remains steady in both is certainly eligible for liberation.

Reflection

- What are you chasing that you believe will finally make you happy? Have previous achievements delivered lasting happiness? Or just brief satisfaction followed by the next craving? What if the chase itself is the problem?
- Can you be happy now--before circumstances change? Not "I should be grateful" but actually content. What would it feel like to stop waiting for the future to deliver what is already available in the present?
- When did you last feel genuinely content? Were the conditions external or internal? Which kind of happiness lasts?

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 60: Death

Opening Story
