"Six months. Maybe a year."

Dr. Patel's voice is gentle, but the words hit like a hammer. Terminal. Inoperable. Untreatable.

Margaret sits in the office chair and feels the world tilt. She is sixty-seven. She thought she had time. More birthdays. More grandchildren. More mornings waking up next to David.

I am going to die.

The thought is not abstract anymore. It is immediate. Real. Unavoidable.

The first month, Margaret fights. Second opinions. Experimental treatments. Alternative therapies. Anything to push back the inevitable.

"We have to try everything," she tells David.

But underneath the urgency, she knows: she is not fighting the disease. She is fighting death itself. And death does not lose.

The treatments make her sick. Weak. Miserable.

"Is this worth it?" David asks gently one night.

Margaret wants to say yes. But she is exhausted. And the truth is becoming undeniable: she cannot stop this. No one can.

Reflective Bridge

Margaret's oldest friend visits. Nina is seventy-two. A practitioner of meditation for forty years.

They sit in Margaret's garden--the one Margaret planted thinking she would tend it for decades.

"Are you afraid?" Nina asks.

"Terrified."

"Of dying? Or of death?"

Margaret pauses. "What's the difference?"

"Dying is the process. The body shutting down. That can be painful. Difficult. But death itself--what if it's just a transition? Like sleep. Like changing clothes. Like walking from one room into another."

Margaret wants to dismiss it. But something in her softens. Because clinging to this body--to this life--has not stopped the inevitable. It has just made the time she has left miserable.

"How do I stop being afraid?"

"You probably can't. Not completely. But you can start seeing death as natural--not as a tragedy."

Nina pulls out a worn copy of the Bhagavad-gita.

The Gita Speaks: What You Remember, You Become

Nina reads:

"Whatever state of being one remembers when he quits his body, that state he will attain without fail."
-- Bhagavad-gita 8.6

Margaret listens.

Whatever state of being one remembers.

This is not abstract theology. It is practical instruction. Death is not an ending--it is a doorway. And the consciousness you carry through that doorway determines where you arrive.

If you die consumed by fear, that fear shapes what comes next. If you die clinging to possessions, that attachment pulls you toward circumstances of continued grasping. If you die in peace, with awareness directed toward the divine, that consciousness carries forward.

Death, then, is not something that happens to you. It is something you participate in. The final act of consciousness. The last thought, the last state of being, the last orientation of awareness--these matter profoundly.

Margaret feels something release. Not acceptance yet. But recognition.

She has been treating her death as a personal failure. As something wrong. Unfair. Unjust.

But it is not. It is universal. Every person born dies. Without exception. It is not tragedy. It is design.

And the question is not whether she will die. It is how she will die. What consciousness will she carry through the transition?

Living the Teaching

Margaret stops the aggressive treatments. Not giving up. Letting go.

She focuses on quality. On presence. On the time that remains.

She tells her grandchildren stories. Records videos. Writes letters. Not frantically--but meaningfully.

She spends mornings in the garden she will not finish planting. And instead of grief, she feels connection. To the earth. To the cycle. To the continuity of life beyond her particular body.

"I used to think death was the opposite of life," she tells David one evening.

"And now?"

"Now I think it's part of life. The final act. The completion."

David's eyes fill with tears. "I don't want you to go."

"I know. And I'll miss you terribly."

"Will you?"

She smiles. "I don't know. Maybe the soul doesn't miss--because it knows this isn't goodbye. Just see you later."

Practice Box

1. Acknowledge: you will die. Not someday. Not abstractly. You--the person reading this--will die. Your body will stop. Sit with that truth. Let it move from theoretical to real.

2. Ask: What state of being am I cultivating? If whatever you remember at death is what you become, then the consciousness you practice daily is your preparation. What are you practicing? Fear? Gratitude? Resentment? Peace?

3. Live from the end backward. If you knew you had six months, what would matter? Who would you forgive? What would you say? You do not need a diagnosis to live this way. Start now.

4. Practice letting go daily. Every night, release the day. Let it die. Every ending--projects, relationships, phases of life--allow completion. Death is just the final letting go. Train for it by releasing smaller things.

The Way Forward

Margaret's final days are quiet. She is in hospice. Comfortable. Surrounded by family.

David holds her hand.

"Are you scared?" he whispers.

"Less than I thought I'd be."

"Why?"

"Because I've been practicing. Letting go a little more each day. And I've realized--I've died a thousand small deaths. Every night, sleeping. Every ending, releasing. This is just the biggest one."

"What do you think happens after?"

Margaret closes her eyes.

"I don't know for certain. But I've been working on what I'll remember. Not the fear. Not the regret. The love. The gratitude. The awareness of something greater than this body."

She opens her eyes.

"Whatever state of being one remembers when he quits his body, that state he will attain without fail. I want to remember peace. I want to remember the divine."

Three days later, Margaret dies. Peacefully. Surrounded by love.

And in her final moment--according to David, who was holding her hand--she smiled.

Like someone recognizing something familiar. Like someone arriving, not departing.

Reflection

- Can you acknowledge that you will die? Not someday--but actually, inevitably, certainly. What changes when you let that truth become real?
- What state of being are you practicing? If whatever you remember at death shapes what comes next, what consciousness are you cultivating right now? Is it the consciousness you would want to carry through the final doorway?
- If you had six months, what would matter? Who would you forgive? What would you say? What would you release? You do not need a terminal diagnosis to answer these questions. You just need honesty.

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 61: Death of a Loved One

Opening Story

The phone rings at 2 AM.

Alex knows before answering. No one calls at 2 AM with good news.

"Your father. He's gone. Peacefully. In his sleep."

Gone. Peacefully. The words do not register.

Alex sits on the edge of the bed, phone still pressed to her ear, and feels nothing. Numb. Empty.

My father is dead.

The funeral is a blur. People offering condolences. Stories about her father she has heard a thousand times. Alex goes through the motions. But inside, she is drowning. Not just in sadness. In guilt.

The last conversation they had was an argument. About nothing important. Some disagreement Alex cannot even remember now. She did not call him back. Thought she would have time.

There is always tomorrow.

Except there was not.

At night, alone, Alex finally breaks. The tears come in waves.

I never got to say goodbye. I never told him I loved him.

Reflective Bridge

Cleaning out her father's house, Alex finds a box. Letters. Dozens of them. Letters her father wrote to her over the years but never sent.

She opens one at random.

Dear Alex, you are sixteen today. We fought this morning about curfew. You think I am controlling. Maybe I am. But I just want you safe. I know you are angry. I was angry at my father too at your age. That is how it works. But I want you to know: even when we fight, I love you. Even when you are furious with me, I am proud of you. You do not have to be perfect for me to love you. You just have to be you. Love, Dad.

Alex's hands shake.

He knew. All along, he knew. The fights did not matter. The disagreements did not change anything. He loved her. Unconditionally. Always.

And somehow--reading his words now--she feels it. Not gone. Still here. Love does not die when the body does.

The Gita Speaks: The Soul Is Eternal

Alex's aunt visits a month after the funeral. She brings tea and a book.

"Your father was reading this before he died. He had it marked."

It is the Bhagavad-gita. Aunt Linda opens to a bookmarked page:

"For the soul there is never birth nor death. Nor, having once been, does he ever cease to be. He is unborn, eternal, ever-existing, undying and primeval."
-- Bhagavad-gita 2.20

He is not slain when the body is slain.

"Do you believe that?" Alex asks.

"I don't know. But your father did. He told me once--he wasn't afraid of death because he knew he wasn't the body. He was the consciousness inhabiting it."

Another marked verse:

"As the embodied soul continually passes, in this body, from boyhood to youth to old age, the soul similarly passes into another body at death."
-- Bhagavad-gita 2.13

Alex feels something shift. Not certainty. But possibility. What if her father is not gone? What if he just changed form--like he changed from boy to man to old age?

Then Aunt Linda reads one more passage her father had underlined:

"It is said that the soul is invisible, inconceivable, immutable, and unchangeable. Knowing this, you should not grieve for the body."
-- Bhagavad-gita 2.25

You should not grieve for the body.

Not "do not grieve." Not "suppress your pain." But grieve for the right thing. The body is gone, yes. But the soul--invisible, inconceivable, unchangeable--persists. Grieve the absence. Honor the loss. But do not despair as if the person has ceased to exist.

And one final verse, circled twice in her father's hand:

"For one who has taken his birth, death is certain; and for one who is dead, birth is certain. Therefore, in the unavoidable discharge of your duty, you should not lament."
-- Bhagavad-gita 2.27

Death is certain for the born. Birth is certain for the dead. The cycle does not end. It turns.

Living the Teaching

Alex starts talking to her father. Not out loud. Not prayers, exactly. Just talking.

Dad, I found your letters. Thank you.

I am sorry we fought. I am sorry I did not call.

I love you. I always did.

And sometimes--sitting quietly, feeling the grief--she feels a response. Not words. Not a voice. Just presence. Like he is listening. Like the connection did not sever when his body stopped.

She starts living differently. More intentionally. More lovingly. Because she learned the hard lesson: you do not always get tomorrow. So say it now. Forgive now. Love now.

Practice Box

1. Feel the grief fully. Do not suppress it. Do not rush it. Grief is love with nowhere to go. Let it move through you. Honor what you have lost.

2. Release the regrets. What you did not say. What you did not do. Forgive yourself. They knew. Love does not need perfect words. It just is.

3. Talk to them anyway. The connection does not end with death. Tell them what you wish you had said. Feel the love that remains. It is still real.

4. Trust continuity. The soul is unborn, eternal, ever-existing. They are not gone. They have changed form. And the love you shared does not end either. It transforms. Changes shape. But remains.

The Way Forward

One year after her father's death, Alex visits his grave. She brings flowers. And one of the letters he wrote her. She reads it aloud. Not because she thinks he is in the ground. But because speaking the words makes the love real again.

"I miss you," she says to the headstone. "Every day."

A breeze moves through the cemetery.

Alex closes her eyes.

Not gone. Here. Different form, but present.

The soul that loved her into being--that watched her grow, that wrote those letters, that knew her completely--that soul did not die. It cannot.

For the soul there is never birth nor death. Nor, having once been, does he ever cease to be.

Alex knows: they will meet again. Not here. Not in this form. But consciousness recognizes consciousness. Souls find each other.

This is not goodbye forever. It is goodbye for now.

She stands. Touches the headstone.

"I'll see you again, Dad. I don't know when or where. But I will."

And Alex walks back to her car. Still grieving. But no longer despairing.

Because love does not die. It just changes form. And waits for the next reunion.

Reflection

- Who are you still grieving? Let yourself feel it fully--not to wallow, but to honor what you have lost. Where can you let that love express itself?
- What do you regret not saying? Write the letter now. Say the words. The soul hears. The connection remains.
- Can you trust that they continue? The soul is eternal, ever-existing, undying. What if the one you loved still exists--different form, but ongoing? What if you will meet them again?

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa

Chapter 62: Vital Cycle

Opening Story

Dr. Okafor watches the monitor. Heartbeat. Breath. Brain waves. The vital signs that signal: alive.

He has been an emergency physician for twenty years. And he has learned: life is rhythm. Inhale. Exhale. Systole. Diastole. Waking. Sleeping. The body is a symphony of cycles.

And when the rhythm stops--when the heart flatlines, when breath ceases--death arrives.

But here is what he has also learned: the rhythm does not belong to the body. The body participates in it. But the rhythm itself--the pulsing, breathing, living force animating every heartbeat--that comes from somewhere else.

Late shift. 3 AM. Dr. Okafor sits in the break room, exhausted. He has just pronounced his third death this week. Car accident. Nineteen years old.

One moment: alive. Breathing. Heart beating. Next moment: gone.

But the body looked the same. Same face. Same hands. Same physical form. Something left. Something essential. The life force--prana, the yogis call it--that animating energy that makes a body a being, it departed.

And Dr. Okafor wants to understand: What is that force? Where does it come from? Where does it go?

Reflective Bridge

Medicine can describe what happens when someone dies. The heart stops pumping. Cells lose oxygen. Organs fail. The body cools.

But medicine cannot explain what leaves. The difference between a living body and a dead one is not chemical. Every molecule is still present. Every organ is still in place. Yet something essential is absent.

Dr. Okafor has watched hundreds of people die. And in every case, the same mystery: the body remains, but the person departs. Where does the animating force originate? And when it withdraws, where does it go?

He starts reading. Not medical journals. Philosophy. Ancient texts.

The Gita Speaks: The Source and End of All Life

Krishna reveals the origin of the vital force:

"Of all that is material and all that is spiritual in this world, know for certain that I am both its origin and dissolution."
-- Bhagavad-gita 7.6

The life force is not self-generated. It flows from a source--and returns to that source. Every heartbeat, every breath, every electrical impulse in the brain is an expression of divine energy temporarily animating matter.

And this energy is not limited to individual bodies:

"I am the source of all the material and spiritual worlds. Everything emanates from Me."
-- Bhagavad-gita 10.8

Everything. The force driving cellular division. The energy behind a newborn's first cry. The power sustaining a ninety-year-old's final breath. All of it emanates from one source.

Krishna then reveals both poles of the vital cycle:

"I am the all-devouring death, and I am the generating principle of all that is to exist."
-- Bhagavad-gita 10.34

The same source that generates life also dissolves it. Birth and death are not opposing forces. They are the same divine energy expressing itself in two directions. The generating principle brings life into form. Death withdraws it. Both are acts of the same intelligence.

And nothing exists outside this cycle:

"Furthermore, O Arjuna, I am the generating seed of all existences. There is no being-moving or unmoving-that can exist without Me."
-- Bhagavad-gita 10.39

Every living thing participates in this vital cycle. No being--mobile or immobile, human or animal, plant or microbe--exists independently of the source. The life force flowing through Dr. Okafor's patients is the same force flowing through him, through the trees outside the hospital, through every organism on earth.

And this cycle operates at every scale:

"The Blessed Lord said: Time I am, destroyer of the worlds, and I have come to engage all people."
-- Bhagavad-gita 11.32

Time itself--the medium through which the vital cycle unfolds--is divine. Not a neutral backdrop, but an active force. Time generates, sustains, and dissolves. Galaxies form and collapse. Species emerge and go extinct. Bodies are born and die. All within the rhythm of time.

Living the Teaching

Dr. Okafor changes how he practices medicine. Not the techniques. Not the procedures. But his relationship to patients.

He stops seeing them as bodies. And starts seeing them as consciousness temporarily embodied.

When he treats an elderly patient dying of organ failure, he sees: a body wearing out. A vehicle reaching the end of its usefulness. But the consciousness inside--the awareness looking out through those tired eyes--that is eternal.

When he resuscitates someone whose heart stopped, he does not think: I brought them back to life. He thinks: The life force returned to this body. For now.

It shifts everything. The reverence he feels. The humility. He is not the master of life and death. He is a participant in the vital cycle--helping when he can, accepting when he cannot.

Practice Box

1. Notice the life force in your body. Right now, feel your heartbeat. Your breath. The subtle energy animating every cell. You are not creating this. It is happening through you. That is the life force. Honor it.

2. Recognize: your body is a vehicle, not you. The consciousness reading these words is using eyes, brain, nervous system--but it is not produced by them. You are the driver. The body is the car.

3. See others as souls temporarily embodied. That changes how you relate to everyone. Not bodies interacting--but souls meeting.

4. Accept the cycle. Birth. Life. Death. Rebirth. This is the rhythm. Fighting it creates suffering. Accepting it creates peace. You are part of the vital cycle--not its master, just its participant.

The Way Forward

Dr. Okafor is sixty-five when he retires. His last shift in the ER.

A young doctor asks him: "What's the most important thing you've learned?"

Dr. Okafor considers.

"That we don't give life. We channel it."

"What does that mean?"

"Life--consciousness, the soul, the animating force--it doesn't come from the body. The body is its expression. Its vehicle. And when the vehicle wears out, life continues. Somewhere else. In some other form."

"You believe that?"

"I've seen it. Thousands of times. The moment someone dies--the body's still there. But they're gone. What left wasn't physical. It was the life force. And where did it go?"

He smiles.

"Back to the source. Waiting for the next body. The next cycle."

That night, Dr. Okafor goes home. He sits in his backyard. Feels his heartbeat. Feels his breath. Feels the life force pulsing through every cell.

I am alive.

Not because he earned it. Not because he deserves it. But because the vital cycle--the rhythm of existence itself--is expressing itself through this particular body for this particular time.

I am both the origin and the dissolution.

I am the generating seed of all existences.

Time I am, destroyer of the worlds.

The source of his heartbeat is the source of the stars. The force behind his breath is the force behind creation. He is not separate from the cycle. He is the cycle, temporarily aware of itself in this form.

Dr. Okafor closes his eyes. And breathes.

Inhale. Exhale.

The vital cycle. Perfect. Eternal. Unstoppable.

He is honored to be part of it. For as long as it lasts.

Reflection

- Can you feel the life force in your body right now? Your heartbeat, your breath, the energy animating every cell. You are not creating this--it is happening through you. Can you honor it?
- Do you see your body as you--or as the vehicle you are using? What if you are the consciousness animating this form, not the form itself? How does that change how you face aging and death?
- Are you living consciously or automatically? What would it mean to participate in the vital cycle with full awareness--to honor each breath, to revere the life force moving through you?

From The Gita Playbook: Ancient Solutions to Modern Day Struggles by Br. Jagannatha Mishra Dasa
